Pricing Financial Assets

AØKK08095U  ·  University of Copenhagen  ·  Complete Exam Preparation

Jump to:
GBM & Itô's Lemma All 16 exams
▼

Intuition

Stock prices are modelled as a random walk with drift. GBM says the percentage change in the asset price is normally distributed with constant mean \(\mu\) and volatility \(\sigma\). Because we work with functions of the stock (option prices, forward values…), we need the stochastic chain rule: Itô's Lemma.

Shortcomings of GBM (asked in many exams)

  • No jumps — real prices can gap; GBM paths are continuous
  • Constant volatility — in reality volatility smiles/skews exist
  • Constant drift — no mean reversion; unrealistic for interest rates
  • Lognormal tails — underestimates extreme events (fat tails in practice)
GBM\[dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\]
Itô's Lemma — for any smooth \(G(S,t)\): \[dG = \Bigl(\frac{\partial G}{\partial t} + \mu S\frac{\partial G}{\partial S} + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial S^2}\Bigr)dt + \sigma S\frac{\partial G}{\partial S}\,dz\] The \(\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\) term comes from \((dS)^2=\sigma^2S^2\,dt\).
Four standard transformations
\(G\)\(G_S\)\(G_{SS}\)Result \(dG\)
\(\ln S\)\(1/S\)\(-1/S^2\)\((\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)\,dt+\sigma\,dz\)
\(S^n\)\(nS^{n-1}\)\(n(n-1)S^{n-2}\)\(\bigl(n\mu+\tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\bigr)G\,dt+n\sigma G\,dz\)
\(1/S\)\(-1/S^2\)\(2/S^3\)\((\sigma^2-\mu)G\,dt-\sigma G\,dz\)
\(e^{aZ}\), \(Z\sim\text{BM}\)——\(\tfrac{1}{2}a^2 e^{aZ}\,dt+a\,e^{aZ}\,dz\)
GBM closed-form solution \[S_T = S_0\exp\!\Bigl[\bigl(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)T+\sigma z_T\Bigr],\quad z_T\sim N(0,T)\] \[\ln S_T \;\sim\; N\!\Bigl(\ln S_0+\bigl(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)T,\;\sigma^2 T\Bigr)\]

Derivation: \(G=\ln S\)

Step 1 — Partial derivatives: \(G_t=0,\; G_S=1/S,\; G_{SS}=-1/S^2\)
Step 2 — Substitute into Itô's lemma: \[dG = \Bigl(0+\mu S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S}+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\cdot\bigl(-\tfrac{1}{S^2}\bigr)\Bigr)dt+\sigma S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S}\,dz\]
Step 3 — Simplify: \[d(\ln S)=\bigl(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt+\sigma\,dz\quad\checkmark\] Integrating: \(\ln S_T=\ln S_0+(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T+\sigma z_T\).

Derivation: \(G=S^n\)

\(G_t=0\), \(G_S=nS^{n-1}\), \(G_{SS}=n(n-1)S^{n-2}\). Substituting: \[dG=\bigl(G_t+\mu SG_S+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2G_{SS}\bigr)dt+\sigma SG_S\,dz\] So \(S^n\) also follows GBM with drift \(n\mu+\frac{n(n-1)}{2}\sigma^2\) and volatility \(n\sigma\).

When you see: "Use Ito's lemma to find the process for G(S)"

Step 1 — Write the three derivatives of G
Compute \(G_t\), \(G_S\), \(G_{SS}\) explicitly.
Step 2 — State Ito's Lemma\[dG = \left(G_t + \mu S G_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\right)dt + \sigma S G_S\,dz\]
Step 3 — Compute \((dS)^2\)
\[(dS)^2 = (\mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz)^2 = \mu^2S^2\,dt^2 + 2\mu\sigma S^2\,dt\,dz + \sigma^2S^2\,dz^2\]Apply rules \(dt^2=0\), \(dt\,dz=0\), \(dz^2=dt\):\[(dS)^2 = \sigma^2 S^2\,dt\]
Step 4 — Substitute dS and collect terms
Distribute all brackets. Collect \(dt\) and \(dz\) separately.
Step 5 — Replace S with G
E.g. \(S^{-1}=G\). State result: \(dG = [\ldots]\,dt + [\ldots]\,dz\)
Key results
\(G\)DriftDiffusion
\(\ln S\)\(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\)\(\sigma\)
\(1/S\)\(\sigma^2-\mu\)\(-\sigma\)
\(S^n\)\(n\mu+\tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\)\(n\sigma\)
Forward Pricing 14/16 exams
▼

Intuition

A forward contract locks in today the price \(F\) you'll pay at time \(T\). No money changes hands now, so \(F\) is set by no-arbitrage: two strategies to own the asset at \(T\) must have the same present value.

Carry-and-compare logic: Strategy A = enter forward. Strategy B = buy spot, borrow/lend at \(r\), receive/pay any income. Same terminal payoff → same initial cost → pin down \(F\).

AssetForward PriceWhy
Non-dividend stock\(F=S_0e^{rT}\)Carry at cost \(r\)
Continuous dividend \(\delta\)\(F=S_0e^{(r-\delta)T}\)Dividends reduce carry cost
FX (foreign rate \(r_f\))\(F=S_0e^{(r-r_f)T}\)Invest abroad at \(r_f\)
Commodity (storage \(u\), yield \(y\))\(F=S_0e^{(r+u-y)T}\)Pay storage, gain convenience
Value of an existing forward (delivery price \(K\), time \(t\)): \[V_t = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)} - K e^{-r(T-t)}\] At initiation \(K=F_0\) → \(V_0=0\). At expiry → \(V_T=S_T-K\).
BSM PDE check on forward value (no dividends, \(V=S-Ke^{-r(T-t)}\)): \[\Theta = rKe^{-r(T-t)},\quad \Delta=1,\quad \Gamma=0\] \[\text{LHS: }rKe^{-r(T-t)}+0+rS\cdot1 = r(S-Ke^{-r(T-t)})=rV\quad\checkmark\]

No-arbitrage derivation: non-dividend stock

Strategy A: Enter forward. Cost today = 0. At \(T\): receive \(S_T\), pay \(F\).
Strategy B: Borrow \(S_0\) at \(r\), buy stock. Cost today = 0. At \(T\): own \(S_T\), repay \(S_0e^{rT}\).
Same payoff structure → \(F=S_0e^{rT}\).
If \(F>S_0e^{rT}\): sell forward + buy spot → arbitrage profit \(F-S_0e^{rT}>0\).
If \(F<S_0e^{rT}\): buy forward + short spot → arbitrage profit \(S_0e^{rT}-F>0\).

FX derivation (Garman-Kohlhagen)

Borrow \(S_0e^{-r_fT}\) domestic → convert to \(e^{-r_fT}\) foreign → invest at \(r_f\) → get 1 foreign at \(T\).
Repay domestic loan: \(S_0e^{-r_fT}\cdot e^{rT}=S_0e^{(r-r_f)T}\). So \(F=S_0e^{(r-r_f)T}\).

When you see: "Find the forward price by an arbitrage argument"

Step 1 — "Consider two strategies delivering the asset at \(T\) at zero cost today:"
Step 2 — Strategy A: Enter forward. Pay \(F(0,T)\) at \(T\), receive asset.
Step 3 — Strategy B: Borrow \(S_0\) at \(r\). Buy stock. Repay \(S_0e^{rT}\) at \(T\).
Step 4 — No-arbitrage \(\Rightarrow F(0,T) = S_0 e^{rT}\)
Variations
AssetStrategy BResult
No dividendsBorrow \(S_0\) at \(r\)\(S_0e^{rT}\)
Dividend \(\delta\)Borrow \(S_0e^{-\delta T}\)\(S_0e^{(r-\delta)T}\)
FX (rate \(r_f\))Borrow \(S_0e^{-r_fT}\), invest at \(r_f\)\(S_0e^{(r-r_f)T}\)
Commodity (storage \(u\))Borrow \(S_0\), pay storage\(S_0e^{(r+u)T}\)
Binomial Model & State Prices 12/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Discretise time: at each step the stock jumps to \(uS\) (up) or \(dS\) (down). Find risk-neutral probabilities \(q,1-q\) such that the expected return equals the risk-free rate \(r\). Then discount expected payoffs at \(r\) to price any derivative.

Arrow-Debreu state prices \(\psi_u,\psi_d\) are the present values of 1 unit of cash in each state — the most fundamental pricing objects.

Risk-neutral probabilities (\(a=e^{r\Delta t}\)): \[q=\frac{a-d}{u-d},\qquad 1-q=\frac{u-a}{u-d}\] No-arb requires \(d < a < u\).
Arrow-Debreu state prices: \[\psi_u=e^{-r\Delta t}q,\qquad \psi_d=e^{-r\Delta t}(1-q)\] Verify: \(\psi_u+\psi_d=e^{-r\Delta t}\) (price of riskless ZCB).   \(\psi_u uS+\psi_d dS=S\) (price of stock).
One-step option price: \[f_0=e^{-r}[qf_u+(1-q)f_d]=\psi_u f_u+\psi_d f_d\]
Delta hedge: \[\Delta=\frac{f_u-f_d}{(u-d)S_0}\] Portfolio \(\Delta S-f\) is riskless (same value up or down).
Two-step backward induction: \[f_0=e^{-2r}\bigl[q^2 f_{uu}+2q(1-q)f_{ud}+(1-q)^2 f_{dd}\bigr]\] Always work backwards: terminal payoffs → penultimate nodes → \(f_0\).
Forward price in binomial: \(F=aS_0=e^{r\Delta t}S_0\).
Verify: \(\psi_u(uS)+\psi_d(dS)=e^{-r}[qu+(1-q)d]S=e^{-r}\cdot a\cdot S=S\) → PV of forward payoff \(=S_0\). ✓

Deriving \(q\) via replication

Step 1: Portfolio \(\Pi=\Delta S-f\). Require same value up and down: \[\Delta uS-f_u=\Delta dS-f_d \implies \Delta=\frac{f_u-f_d}{(u-d)S}\]
Step 2: Riskless portfolio earns \(r\): \((\Delta S-f)e^r=\Delta uS-f_u\).
Solve for \(f\): \(f=e^{-r}[qf_u+(1-q)f_d]\) where \(q=(e^r-d)/(u-d)\). ✓

Arrow-Debreu from two assets

Two equations: (1) ZCB: \(\psi_u+\psi_d=e^{-r}\). (2) Stock: \(\psi_u uS+\psi_d dS=S\).
Solve: \(\psi_u=e^{-r}\frac{a-d}{u-d}=e^{-r}q\), \(\psi_d=e^{-r}\frac{u-a}{u-d}=e^{-r}(1-q)\). ✓

When you see: "Find risk-neutral probabilities and state prices"

Step 1 — \(S_0 = e^{-rT}\bigl[q\cdot uS_0 + (1-q)\cdot dS_0\bigr]\)
Step 2 — Divide by \(S_0\), multiply by \(e^{rT}\):\[e^{rT} = qu+d-qd \implies e^{rT}-d = q(u-d)\]
Step 3 — \(q = \dfrac{a-d}{u-d}\), \quad \(1-q = \dfrac{u-a}{u-d}\)   where \(a=e^{rT}\)
Step 4 — \(\psi_u = e^{-rT}q\), \quad \(\psi_d = e^{-rT}(1-q)\)
Verify: \(\psi_u+\psi_d = e^{-rT}\) ✓   \(\psi_u uS_0+\psi_d dS_0 = S_0\) ✓
\(f_0 = \psi_u f_u + \psi_d f_d = e^{-rT}\bigl[qf_u+(1-q)f_d\bigr]\)
Black-Scholes-Merton Model All 16 exams
▼

Intuition

Under the risk-neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\), \(S_T\) is lognormal with drift \(r\) (not \(\mu\)). The BSM formula is just the expected discounted payoff \(E^{\mathbb{Q}}[e^{-rT}\max(S_T-K,0)]\) computed in closed form using the lognormal distribution.

\(d_1\) = adjusted log-moneyness; \(d_2=d_1-\sigma\sqrt{T}\). \(\Phi(d_2)\) = risk-neutral probability of exercise. \(\Phi(d_1)\) = delta = hedge ratio.

BSM Call and Put (no dividends): \[c=S_0\Phi(d_1)-Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[p=Ke^{-rT}\Phi(-d_2)-S_0\Phi(-d_1)\] \[d_1=\frac{\ln(S_0/K)+(r+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}},\quad d_2=d_1-\sigma\sqrt{T}\]
With continuous dividend \(\delta\): \[c=S_0e^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1)-Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2),\quad d_1=\frac{\ln(S_0/K)+(r-\delta+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}\]
Key interpretations:
  • \(\Phi(d_2)=\mathbb{Q}(S_T>K)\) = RN probability of exercise
  • \(\Delta_{\text{call}}=e^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1)\) = hedge ratio; \(\Delta_{\text{put}}=e^{-\delta T}(\Phi(d_1)-1)\)
  • \(\Delta_c=\tfrac{1}{2}\) when \(d_1=0\), i.e. \(K=S_0e^{(r-\delta+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}\)
BSM PDE: \[\frac{\partial V}{\partial t}+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial S^2}+rS\frac{\partial V}{\partial S}=rV\] Shorthand: \(\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta=rV\)
Greeks: \[\Delta_c=\Phi(d_1),\quad \Gamma=\frac{\phi(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt{T}},\quad \text{Vega}=S\sqrt{T}\phi(d_1)\] \[\Theta_c=-\frac{S\phi(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt{T}}-rKe^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]

BSM PDE derivation (delta-hedge argument)

Step 1: Apply Itô to \(V(S,t)\): \[dV=\bigl(V_t+\mu SV_S+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}\bigr)dt+\sigma SV_S\,dz\]
Step 2: Construct delta-hedge \(\Pi=V-\Delta S\) with \(\Delta=V_S\). The \(dz\) terms cancel: \[d\Pi=\bigl(V_t+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}\bigr)dt\quad\text{(riskless)}\]
Step 3: No-arbitrage: riskless portfolio earns \(r\): \[V_t+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}=r(V-V_S S)\implies \Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta=rV\quad\checkmark\]

PDE verification template

Given \(V(S,t)\): compute \(\Delta=V_S\), \(\Gamma=V_{SS}\), \(\Theta=V_t\). Substitute into LHS \(=\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta\). Show equals \(rV\).

When you see: "Derive the BSM PDE" or "Verify V satisfies the PDE"

A — Deriving the PDE

Step 1 — Ito on \(V(S,t)\): \(dV = (V_t + \mu SV_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2V_{SS})dt + \sigma SV_S\,dz\)
Step 2 — Delta-hedge \(\Pi=V-V_SS\). The \(dz\) cancels: \(d\Pi=(V_t+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2V_{SS})dt\)
Step 3 — No-arbitrage: \(d\Pi = r\Pi\,dt\). Rearrange: \[\boxed{\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta = rV}\]

B — Verifying a given V

Step 1 — Compute \(\Delta=V_S\), \(\Gamma=V_{SS}\), \(\Theta=V_t\)
Step 2 — Substitute into LHS \(=\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta\)
Step 3 — Show LHS \(=rV\). Conclude: "\(V\) satisfies the BSM PDE. ✓"
Put-Call Parity & Greeks All 16 exams
▼

Intuition

PCP links European put and call prices through a pure no-arbitrage argument — no model assumptions. Once you have PCP, differentiate w.r.t. \(S\) to get delta relationships, differentiate again for gamma, and w.r.t. \(t\) for theta.

Put-Call Parity: \[c-p=S_0e^{-\delta T}-Ke^{-rT}\quad\text{(continuous dividend }\delta\text{)}\] No div: \(c-p=S_0-Ke^{-rT}\).   Futures: \(c-p=(F-K)e^{-rT}\).
Greeks from PCP (differentiate \(c-p=Se^{-\delta T}-Ke^{-rT}\)): \[\frac{\partial}{\partial S}:\quad \Delta_c-\Delta_p=e^{-\delta T}\;(\text{no div: }1)\] \[\frac{\partial^2}{\partial S^2}:\quad \Gamma_c=\Gamma_p\] \[\frac{\partial}{\partial t}:\quad \Theta_c-\Theta_p=\delta Se^{-\delta T}-rKe^{-rT}\] Vega is also equal: \(\text{Vega}_c=\text{Vega}_p\).
BSM PDE + delta-neutral gamma-theta tradeoff: \[\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta=rV\] For \(\Delta=0\): \(\Theta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2S^2\Gamma=r\Pi\).
Long option: long gamma \(\Rightarrow\) negative theta (you pay daily for the optionality).
Short option: short gamma \(\Rightarrow\) positive theta.

PCP proof (two portfolios)

Portfolio A: Long call + \(Ke^{-rT}\) cash. Value at \(T\): \(\max(S_T-K,0)+K=\max(S_T,K)\).
Portfolio B: Long put + \(e^{-\delta T}\) shares (or long stock with dividend reinvested). Value at \(T\): \(\max(K-S_T,0)+S_T=\max(K,S_T)\).
Same payoff at \(T\) → same price today: \(c+Ke^{-rT}=p+S_0e^{-\delta T}\implies c-p=S_0e^{-\delta T}-Ke^{-rT}\). ✓

When you see: "Prove put-call parity" or "find Greeks from PCP"

A — Proving PCP

Portfolio A: Long call + \(Ke^{-rT}\) cash. At \(T\): \(\max(S_T,K)\)
Portfolio B: Long put + long stock. At \(T\): \(\max(K,S_T)\)
Same payoff \(\Rightarrow\) \(\boxed{c-p=S_0-Ke^{-rT}}\)

B — Greeks from PCP

\(\partial/\partial S\): \(\Delta_c-\Delta_p=1\)
\(\partial^2/\partial S^2\): \(\Gamma_c=\Gamma_p\)
\(\partial/\partial t\): \(\Theta_c-\Theta_p=-rKe^{-rT}\)
Market Price of Risk 10/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Any two derivatives driven by the same Brownian motion must share the same excess return per unit of risk, or there is an arbitrage. This ratio is the market price of risk \(\lambda\). It's the foundation of risk-neutral pricing: changing from \(\mathbb{P}\) to \(\mathbb{Q}\) is equivalent to setting \(\lambda=0\).

Setup: Two assets driven by \(dz\): \[\frac{df_i}{f_i}=\mu_i\,dt+\sigma_i\,dz,\quad i=1,2\]
Market price of risk: \[\lambda=\frac{\mu_1-r}{\sigma_1}=\frac{\mu_2-r}{\sigma_2}\] \(\lambda\) is the same for all derivatives on the same underlying risk factor.
With dividend yield \(\delta\) (for a traded asset \(S\)): \[\mu+\delta-r=\lambda\sigma\] Total return (capital gain + dividend) must compensate for risk.
Change to \(\mathbb{Q}\): Define \(d\tilde{z}=dz+\lambda\,dt\) (Girsanov). Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \[\frac{dS}{S}=r\,dt+\sigma\,d\tilde{z}\] Every traded asset earns exactly \(r\) under \(\mathbb{Q}\).

Derivation of \(\lambda\)

Step 1: Construct a portfolio that eliminates risk. Hold \(\sigma_2 f_2\) units of \(f_1\) and short \(\sigma_1 f_1\) units of \(f_2\). The \(dz\) terms cancel.
Step 2: This is a riskless portfolio, so by no-arbitrage it earns \(r\,dt\): \[\mu_1\sigma_2 f_1 f_2-\mu_2\sigma_1 f_1 f_2=r(\sigma_2 f_1 f_2-\sigma_1 f_1 f_2)\]
Step 3: Cancel \(f_1 f_2\): \[\mu_1\sigma_2-\mu_2\sigma_1=r(\sigma_2-\sigma_1)\implies\frac{\mu_1-r}{\sigma_1}=\frac{\mu_2-r}{\sigma_2}\equiv\lambda\quad\checkmark\]

When you see: "Market price of risk" or "drift under Q"

A — Finding \(\lambda\)

Step 1 — Two assets: \(df_i/f_i = \mu_i\,dt+\sigma_i\,dz\)
Step 2 — Build zero-risk portfolio (\(dz\) cancels). No-arb: earns \(r\).
Step 3 — \(\boxed{\lambda = (\mu_1-r)/\sigma_1 = (\mu_2-r)/\sigma_2}\)

B — Drift under Q (Girsanov)

Step 1 — \(\theta=(\mu-r)/\sigma\) (stock) or \(\theta=(\mu_S-r+r_f)/\sigma_S\) (FX)
Step 2 — \(d\tilde{z}=dz+\theta\,dt\), so \(dz=d\tilde{z}-\theta\,dt\). Substitute:\[dS=\mu S\,dt+\sigma S(d\tilde{z}-\theta\,dt)=(\mu-\sigma\theta)S\,dt+\sigma S\,d\tilde{z}\]
Step 3 — Insert \(\theta\): \(\mu-\sigma\cdot\frac{\mu-r}{\sigma}=r\). So: \(\boxed{dS=rS\,dt+\sigma S\,d\tilde{z}}\)
Short Rate Models 12/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Model the instantaneous risk-free rate \(r(t)\) as an SDE. Price ZCBs as \(P(r,t)=E^{\mathbb{Q}}[e^{-\int_t^T r\,ds}]\). Affine models (Vasicek, CIR, Hull-White) give \(P=Ae^{-Br}\) in closed form, where \(B(t,T)\) is the duration of the ZCB.

Itô on ZCB \(P(r,t)\) with \(dr=m\,dt+s\,dz\): \[dP=\bigl(P_t+mP_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr}\bigr)dt+sP_r\,dz\] Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): drift \(=rP\) (ZCB earns risk-free rate). Fundamental PDE: \[P_t+m^{\mathbb{Q}}P_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr}-rP=0,\quad P(r,T)=1\]
Affine form and duration: \[P(r,t)=A(t,T)\,e^{-B(t,T)\,r}\] \[P_r=-B\cdot P,\quad P_{rr}=B^2\cdot P\] \[\boxed{D(t,T)=-\frac{1}{P}\frac{\partial P}{\partial r}=B(t,T)}\]
ModelSDE under \(\mathbb{Q}\)\(B(t,T)\)Key property
Vasicek\(dr=a(b-r)dt+\sigma\,dz\)\(\frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\)Mean-rev., can go negative
CIR\(dr=a(b-r)dt+\sigma\sqrt{r}\,dz\)More complexStays non-negative
Hull-White\(dr=[\theta(t)-ar]dt+\sigma\,dz\)\(\frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\)Fits initial term structure
Ho-Lee\(dr=\theta(t)dt+\sigma\,dz\)\(T-t\)HJM special case, \(a=0\)
Vasicek \(B(t,T)\): \[B_t=aB-1,\quad B(T,T)=0\implies B(t,T)=\frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\] As \(a\to0\): \(B\to T-t\) (Ho-Lee). ZCB vol: \(\sigma_P=\sigma B(t,T)\to0\) as \(t\to T\) (pull-to-par).

Itô → PDE → Affine structure

Apply Itô to \(P(r,t)\): \[dP=\bigl(P_t+mP_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr}\bigr)dt+sP_r\,dz\]
Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), drift \(=rP\): \[P_t+m^{\mathbb{Q}}P_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr}=rP\]
Guess affine \(P=Ae^{-Br}\): Then \(P_r=-BP\), \(P_{rr}=B^2P\), \(P_t=(A_t/A-B_tr)P\). Substitute and separate \(r\) terms to get ODEs for \(A(t)\) and \(B(t)\).
Duration: \(D=-P_r/P=B(t,T)\). For Vasicek: \(B\to 1/a\) as \(T\to\infty\); for Ho-Lee: \(B=T-t\) (linear).

When you see: "Apply Ito to P(r,t)" or "Vasicek / CIR / Ho-Lee"

Step 1 — \(dr=m(r,t)\,dt+s(r,t)\,dz\)
Step 2 — Ito on \(P(r,t)\):\[dP=(P_t+mP_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr})dt+sP_r\,dz\]
Step 3 — Write \(dP/P=\mu_P\,dt+\sigma_P\,dz\) where \(\sigma_P=sP_r/P\)
Step 4 — Under Q, ZCB earns \(r\):\[P_t+m^Q P_r+\tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_{rr}=rP\]
Vasicek \(dr=a(b-r)dt+\sigma\,dz\): mean-reverts to \(b\), can go negative, \(P=Ae^{-Br}\), \(B=\frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\), Duration\(=B\). Not consistent with initial curve → Hull-White: add \(\theta(t)\).
HJM Framework & Ho-Lee 8/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Instead of modelling the short rate, HJM models the entire forward rate curve \(f(t,T)\) directly. The key result: under no-arbitrage, once you choose the volatility structure \(\sigma(t,T)\), the drift is fully determined. You can't choose both freely.

Ho-Lee is the simplest HJM model: constant volatility \(\sigma(t,T)=\sigma\).

HJM forward rate dynamics (under \(\mathbb{Q}\)): \[df(t,T)=\alpha(t,T)\,dt+\sigma(t,T)\,dz\] No-arbitrage drift condition: \[\alpha(t,T)=\sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,du\] The drift is completely determined by the volatility.
Ho-Lee (\(\sigma(t,T)=\sigma=\) const): \[df(t,T)=\sigma^2(T-t)\,dt+\sigma\,dz\] Short rate: \(dr=\theta(t)\,dt+\sigma\,dz\) where \(\theta(t)=f_T(0,t)+\sigma^2 t\).
ZCB volatility in HJM: \[\text{vol}(P(t,T))=-\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,du\] Ho-Lee: \(|\text{vol}(P)|=\sigma(T-t)\). Pull-to-par: as \(t\to T\), vol \(\to0\). ✓
Non-Markov property: In general HJM, \(r(t)=f(t,t)\) depends on the entire history of the forward rate path → not Markov. Can't use simple recombining trees. Ho-Lee and Hull-White are special cases that ARE Markov (one-factor short-rate models).

Deriving the HJM drift condition (sketch)

Express \(P(t,T)=e^{-\int_t^T f(t,u)du}\). Apply Itô (carefully handling the integral).
Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(dP/P=r\,dt+(\ldots)dz\). The drift must equal \(r\,dt\). This forces: \[\alpha(t,T)=\sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,du\quad\checkmark\]
Ho-Lee check: \(\sigma=\text{const}\implies\alpha(t,T)=\sigma\cdot\sigma(T-t)=\sigma^2(T-t)\). ✓

When you see: "HJM drift condition" or "Ho-Lee"

Step 1 — Forward rate SDE: \(df(t,T)=\alpha(t,T)\,dt+\sigma(t,T)\,dz\)
Step 2 — No-arb drift: \(\alpha(t,T)=\sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,du\)
Step 3 — Ho-Lee: \(\sigma=\)const \(\Rightarrow\) \(\alpha=\sigma^2(T-t)\), so \(df=\sigma^2(T-t)\,dt+\sigma\,dz\)
Step 4 — ZCB vol \(=\sigma(T-t)\to 0\) as \(t\to T\) (pull-to-par)
Step 5 — Short rate: \(dr=\theta(t)\,dt+\sigma\,dz\), \(\theta(t)=f_T(0,t)+\sigma^2t\)
LIBOR Market Model 6/16 exams
▼

Intuition

LMM models simple (LIBOR) forward rates \(F_k(t)\) directly. Each rate is a martingale under its own natural numeraire \(P(t,t_{k+1})\) — so it's perfectly suited for cap pricing via Black's formula. Under the rolling numeraire (money-market account), the rates pick up a drift correction term.

Forward LIBOR definition: \[1+\tau_k F_k(t)=\frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})},\quad\tau_k=t_{k+1}-t_k\]
Under \(P(t,t_{k+1})\) numeraire (forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\)): \[dF_k=\zeta_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}^{k+1}\quad\text{(martingale — no drift!)}\] Lognormal → Black's formula for caplets.
Under rolling numeraire \(P(t,t_{m(t)})\): \[dF_k=\zeta_k F_k\left[\sum_{j=m(t)}^{k}\frac{\tau_j\zeta_j F_j}{1+\tau_j F_j}\right]dt+\zeta_k F_k\,dz\] The drift is a sum over all forward rates from the current period to \(k\).
Caplet Black's formula (under \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\)): \[\text{Caplet}_k=\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k(0)\Phi(d_1)-L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\] \[d_1=\frac{\ln(F_k/L_K)+\frac{1}{2}\zeta_k^2 t_k}{\zeta_k\sqrt{t_k}},\quad d_2=d_1-\zeta_k\sqrt{t_k}\]

Why \(F_k\) is a martingale under \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\)

\(F_k=(P(t,t_k)/P(t,t_{k+1})-1)/\tau_k\). This is a ratio of two traded assets in units of \(P(t,t_{k+1})\). Under numeraire \(P(t,t_{k+1})\), all such ratios are martingales by the fundamental theorem.
Since \(F_k>0\) and is a martingale, by Itô's representation: \(dF_k=\zeta_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}^{k+1}\) (lognormal). ✓
Changing from \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\) to the rolling numeraire introduces a drift correction (Girsanov). The correction for each period \(j\) contributes \(\tau_j\zeta_j F_j/(1+\tau_j F_j)\) to the total drift.

When you see: "LIBOR Market Model" or "show \(F_k\) is a martingale"

Step 1 — \(1+\tau_k F_k(t)=P(t,t_k)/P(t,t_{k+1})\)
Step 2 — Under numeraire \(P(t,t_{k+1})\): \(F_k\) = ratio of traded assets \(\Rightarrow\) martingale, no drift:\[dF_k=\zeta_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}^{k+1}\]
Step 3 — Under rolling numeraire (drift appears):\[dF_k=\zeta_k F_k\!\left[\sum_j\frac{\tau_j\zeta_j F_j}{1+\tau_j F_j}\right]dt+\zeta_k F_k\,dz\]
Caplet: \(\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k\Phi(d_1)-L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\)
Credit Risk 10/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Credit risk = risk of default. Three approaches: (1) Reduced-form: model default as a Poisson process with hazard rate \(\lambda\). (2) Structural (Merton): default when firm value falls below debt. (3) Portfolio (copula): model joint defaults via correlation structure.

Hazard rate model: \[\lambda(t)=\text{instantaneous default intensity}\] \[V(t)=\mathbb{P}(\tau>t)=e^{-\int_0^t\lambda(u)du}\quad\text{(survival probability)}\] Constant \(\lambda\): \(V(t)=e^{-\lambda t}\), \(\dot{V}/V=-\lambda\).
Credit triangle (constant hazard rate): \[\bar{\lambda}=\frac{s}{1-R}\] \(s\)=CDS spread, \(R\)=recovery rate, \(\bar{\lambda}\)=risk-neutral hazard rate.
Derivation: fair CDS → premium PV = protection PV → \(s=\bar{\lambda}(1-R)\).
CDS (Credit Default Swap):
  • Protection buyer pays spread \(s\cdot\text{notional}\) continuously until default or maturity
  • Protection seller pays \((1-R)\cdot\text{notional}\) at default
  • Fair spread: \(s=\bar\lambda(1-R)\) (for constant \(\lambda\))
Merton structural model: \[E_0=V_0\Phi(d_1)-De^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\quad\text{(equity = call on firm)}\] \[d_1=\frac{\ln(V_0/D)+(r+\frac{1}{2}\sigma_V^2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt{T}},\quad d_2=d_1-\sigma_V\sqrt{T}\] Default prob: \(\mathbb{Q}(\text{default})=\Phi(-d_2)\).   Relationship: \(\sigma_E E_0=\Phi(d_1)\sigma_V V_0\).
Gaussian one-factor copula: \[x_i=a_iF+\sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i,\quad F,Z_i\overset{iid}{\sim}N(0,1)\] Correlation: \( ho_{ij}=a_ia_j\). Default of \(i\) iff \(x_i<\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T))\).
Correlation ↑Equity trancheSenior tranche
HighBenefits (losses spread to seniors)Suffers (tail risk)
LowSuffers (concentrates losses)Protected (losses stop at equity)

Credit triangle derivation

Premium leg PV: \(s\int_0^T e^{-rt}e^{-\lambda t}dt=s\int_0^T e^{-(r+\lambda)t}dt\)
Protection leg PV: \((1-R)\lambda\int_0^T e^{-(r+\lambda)t}dt\)
Set equal (fair CDS): \(s=(1-R)\lambda\implies\lambda=s/(1-R)\). ✓

When you see: "CDS", "default probability", "CDO", "copula"

A — Survival probability

\(V(t)=e^{-\lambda t}\). Default prob in \([t,t+\Delta t]\): \(\approx\lambda V(t)\Delta t\)

B — CDS fair spread

Premium leg \(= s\int_0^T e^{-(r+\lambda)t}\,dt\). Protection leg \(= (1-R)\lambda\int_0^T e^{-(r+\lambda)t}\,dt\).
Set equal: \(\boxed{s=\lambda(1-R)}\)

C — Gaussian copula

\(x_i=a_iF+\sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i\). Corr: \(\rho_{ij}=a_ia_j\). Default iff \(x_i<\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T))\)
Corr ↑: equity safer, senior riskier. Corr ↓: equity riskier, senior safer.
Change of Numeraire 9/16 exams
▼

Intuition

The most powerful tool in interest rate pricing. For any positive traded asset \(N\) (numeraire), we can find a measure \(\mathbb{Q}^N\) under which every price ratio \(V/N\) is a martingale. Choosing the right numeraire simplifies the pricing problem dramatically.

Core result: \[\frac{V_t}{N_t}=E^{\mathbb{Q}^N}\!\left[\frac{V_T}{N_T}\,\Big|\,\mathcal{F}_t\right]\] Equivalently: \(V_0=N_0\cdot E^{\mathbb{Q}^N}\!\left[V_T/N_T\right]\).
Numeraire \(N\)MeasureKey martingaleUse case
Money mkt \(B_t=e^{\int r\,ds}\)\(\mathbb{Q}\)\(V/B_t\)Equity options (BSM)
ZCB \(P(t,T)\)\(\mathbb{Q}^T\) (T-fwd)Forward pricesIR derivatives
Stock \(S_t\)\(\mathbb{Q}^S\)\(V/S_t\)Exchange options
Annuity \(A=\sum\tau_kP(t,t_k)\)\(\mathbb{Q}^{swap}\)Forward swap rateSwaptions
Forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^T\): Under \(\mathbb{Q}^T\), forward prices \(G(t,T)=V_t/P(t,T)\) are martingales. In particular, \(E^{\mathbb{Q}^T}[S_T]=F(t,T)=S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\).
Girsanov: changing drift between measures. Going from \(\mathbb{Q}^{N_1}\) to \(\mathbb{Q}^{N_2}\), the BM gains drift: \[d\tilde{z}^{N_2}=d\tilde{z}^{N_1}-\sigma_{N_2/N_1}\,dt\] where \(\sigma_{N_2/N_1}\) = volatility of the ratio \(N_2/N_1\).

Why \(f/g\) is a \(\mathbb{Q}^g\)-martingale

Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): both \(f/B\) and \(g/B\) are martingales (discounted prices). Abstract Bayes formula: \[E^{\mathbb{Q}^g}\!\left[\frac{f_T}{g_T}\right]=\frac{E^{\mathbb{Q}}[f_T/B_T\cdot g_T/B_T/\text{const}]}{\ldots}=\frac{f_0}{g_0}\quad\checkmark\]
The Radon-Nikodym derivative: \(\frac{d\mathbb{Q}^g}{d\mathbb{Q}}=\frac{g_T/g_0}{B_T/B_0}\). This is a positive martingale under \(\mathbb{Q}\), ensuring \(\mathbb{Q}^g\) is a valid probability measure.

When you see: "Show X is a martingale" or "price using numeraire"

Step 1 — Identify numeraire \(N\)
Step 2 — Show \(X=f/N\) where both \(f,N\) are traded \(\Rightarrow\) \(X\) is \(\mathbb{Q}^N\)-martingale
Step 3 — Or: compute SDE of \(X\) under Q, verify no \(dt\) term
Pricing: \(V_0=N_0\cdot E^{\mathbb{Q}^N}[V_T/N_T]\)
NumeraireMeasureUse
\(e^{rt}\)\(\mathbb{Q}\)BSM
\(P(t,T)\)\(\mathbb{Q}^T\)IR derivatives
Annuity\(\mathbb{Q}^{swap}\)Swaptions
IR Derivatives: Caps, Floors & Swaptions 8/16 exams
▼

Intuition

Caps protect a floating-rate borrower from rates rising above \(L_K\). Floors protect a floating-rate investor from rates falling below \(L_K\). Swaptions are options to enter a swap at a fixed rate \(R_K\). All priced via Black's formula applied under the appropriate numeraire measure.

Cap = sum of caplets: \[\text{Cap}=\sum_k\text{Caplet}_k,\quad\text{Caplet}_k=\tau_kP(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k\Phi(d_1)-L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\] \[d_1=\frac{\ln(F_k/L_K)+\frac{1}{2}\sigma_k^2 t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}},\quad d_2=d_1-\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\]
Floorlet (put on LIBOR = call on ZCB): \[\text{Floorlet}_k=\tau_kP(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[L_K\Phi(-d_2)-F_k\Phi(-d_1)\bigr]\] Cap-floor parity: Cap\(-\)Floor\(=\)Forward rate agreement (floating minus fixed leg).
Swaptions:
  • Payer swaption (PS): right to pay fixed \(R_K\) — profitable when swap rates rise
  • Receiver swaption (RS): right to receive fixed \(R_K\) — profitable when swap rates fall
Swaption parity: \(\text{PS}-\text{RS}=\text{Forward swap}\).
At-the-money when \(R_K=R_{fwd}\) (forward swap rate): PS=RS.
Black's swaption formula: \(\text{PS}=A(0)[R_{fwd}\Phi(d_1)-R_K\Phi(d_2)]\)
Bachelier (normal) model for caplets: \[\text{Caplet}_k=\tau_kP(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[(F_k-L_K)\Phi(z)+\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\phi(z)\bigr],\quad z=\frac{F_k-L_K}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}}\] Used when rates can go negative (post-2015 environment).

Floorlet = call on ZCB

Floorlet at \(t_{k+1}\): payoff \(=\tau\max(L_K-L_{t_k},0)\).
Note: \(P(t_k,t_{k+1})=1/(1+\tau L_{t_k})\), so \(L_{t_k}=(1/P-1)/\tau\).
Substituting: floorlet payoff \(=(1+\tau L_K)\max(P(t_k,t_{k+1})-1/(1+\tau L_K),0)\).
This is a call on ZCB with strike \(K^*=1/(1+\tau L_K)\). ✓

When you see: "Cap", "Floor", "Swaption", "parity"

Swaption parity

Payer (PS): pay fixed \(R_K\). Receiver (RS): receive fixed \(R_K\).
\(\text{PS}-\text{RS}=\text{Forward swap}\). At-the-money: PS\(=\)RS.

Caplet / Floorlet (Black)

Caplet: \(\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k\Phi(d_1)-L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\)
Floorlet: \(\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[L_K\Phi(-d_2)-F_k\Phi(-d_1)\bigr]\)
Cap-floor parity: Cap \(-\) Floor \(=\) FRA
\(d_1=\dfrac{\ln(F_k/L_K)+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2t_k}{\sigma\sqrt{t_k}}\), \quad \(d_2=d_1-\sigma\sqrt{t_k}\)
Martingales & Risk-Neutral Pricing 9/16 exams
▼

Intuition

A martingale has zero expected change — it's a "fair game." Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), every discounted asset price is a martingale. This is the precise mathematical statement of "no free lunch." Checking whether a process is a martingale (under the right measure) is a standard exam task.

Martingale: \(M_t\) is a martingale if \(E[M_T|\mathcal{F}_t]=M_t\) for all \(t<T\).
Equivalent (Itô): \(M_t\) is a martingale iff its SDE has no \(dt\) term (pure diffusion).
Standard martingales to know:
  • BM \(z_t\): drift = 0 trivially
  • \(z_t^2-t\): \(d(z^2-t)=2z\,dz\) (no \(dt\)) ✓
  • \(e^{\sigma z_t-\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 t}\): \(d(\cdot)=\sigma(\cdot)dz\) ✓ (Doléans-Dade exponential)
  • \(e^{-rt}S_t\) under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(d(e^{-rt}S)=\sigma Se^{-rt}d\tilde{z}\) ✓
  • \(F_k(t)\) under \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\): \(dF_k=\zeta_k F_k d\tilde{z}\) ✓
Risk-neutral pricing theorem: \[V_0=e^{-rT}E^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\] Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(dS=rS\,dt+\sigma S\,d\tilde{z}\) (drift = \(r\), not \(\mu\)).
Girsanov's theorem: Define \(d\tilde{z}=dz+\lambda\,dt\). Then \(\tilde{z}\) is a BM under \(\mathbb{Q}\) (where \(\lambda=(µ-r)/\sigma\) is the market price of risk). Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), all assets earn \(r\).
"Show X is a Q-martingale": Compute the SDE of \(X\) under \(\mathbb{Q}\) (replace \(dz\) with \(d\tilde{z}-\lambda\,dt\), substitute, simplify). Verify no \(dt\) term.
Alternative: Show \(X=f/N\) where \(f\) and \(N\) are both traded assets. Then \(X\) is automatically a \(\mathbb{Q}^N\)-martingale.
Conditional expectation of lognormal: \[E\bigl[e^{\sigma z_T}\,|\,\mathcal{F}_t\bigr]=e^{\sigma z_t+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2(T-t)}\] Use this when computing option prices under \(\mathbb{Q}\).

Formula Reference Sheet

All key results for Pricing Financial Assets — University of Copenhagen

● GBM & Itô's Lemma
GBM
\[dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\]
Log price
\[d(\ln S) = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\]
Solution
\[S_T = S_0\exp\!\Bigl[(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T + \sigma z_T\Bigr]\]
Itô's Lemma — for \(G(S,t)\)
\[dG = \Bigl(G_t + \mu S G_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\Bigr)dt + \sigma S G_S\,dz\]
\(G=S^n\)
\[dG = \bigl(n\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\bigr)G\,dt + n\sigma G\,dz\]
● Black-Scholes-Merton
Call
\[c = Se^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]
Put
\[p = Ke^{-rT}\Phi(-d_2) - Se^{-\delta T}\Phi(-d_1)\]
\[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S/K)+(r-\delta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}},\quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T}\]
PCP
\[c - p = Se^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\]
FX (G-K)
\[c_{\text{FX}} = Se^{-r_f T}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]
Greeks
\(\Delta\)
\[\Delta_c = e^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1),\quad \Delta_p = -e^{-\delta T}\Phi(-d_1)\]
\(\Gamma,\mathcal{V}\)
\[\Gamma = \frac{e^{-\delta T}\phi(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt{T}},\quad \mathcal{V} = Se^{-\delta T}\phi(d_1)\sqrt{T}\]
BSM PDE
\[\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma + rS\Delta = rV\]
● Forward & Futures Pricing
No dividend
\[F = S_0 e^{rT}\]
Div. yield \(\delta\)
\[F = S_0 e^{(r-\delta)T}\]
FX (\(r_f\))
\[F = S_0 e^{(r-r_f)T}\]
Commodity
\[F = S_0 e^{(r+u-y)T}\]
Value at \(t\)
\[V_t = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]
● Binomial Model
RN prob.
\[q = \frac{e^{(r-\delta)h}-d}{u-d},\quad 1-q = \frac{u-e^{(r-\delta)h}}{u-d}\]
Price
\[f_0 = e^{-rh}\bigl[qf_u + (1-q)f_d\bigr]\]
Delta
\[\Delta = \frac{f_u-f_d}{(u-d)S},\quad B = e^{-rh}f_d - \Delta dS\]
CRR
\[u = e^{\sigma\sqrt{h}},\quad d = e^{-\sigma\sqrt{h}}\]
● Market Price of Risk
Asset driven by \(dx = a\,dt + b\,dz\):
\[\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} + (a-\lambda b)\frac{\partial f}{\partial x} + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2\frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x^2} = rf\]
MPR
\[\lambda = \frac{\alpha-r}{\sigma}\quad\text{(same for all derivatives on }x\text{)}\]
Risk-neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\):
\[d\widetilde{z} = dz + \lambda\,dt,\quad dx = (a-\lambda b)\,dt + b\,d\widetilde{z}\]
● Short-Rate Models
Vasicek
\[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,d\widetilde{z}\]
CIR
\[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\sqrt{r}\,d\widetilde{z}\]
Ho-Lee
\[dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,d\widetilde{z}\]
Hull-White
\[dr = (\theta(t)-ar)\,dt + \sigma\,d\widetilde{z},\quad B=\tfrac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\]
ZCB price \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r}\):
Fwd rate
\[f(t,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(t,T)}{\partial T}\]
Spot
\[r(t) = \lim_{T\to t}f(t,T) = f(t,t)\]
● HJM Framework
Dynamics
\[df(t,T) = \alpha(t,T)\,dt + \sigma(t,T)\,d\widetilde{z}\]
No-arb drift
\[\alpha(t,T) = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\!\sigma(t,u)\,du\]
ZCB
\[P(t,T) = \exp\!\left(-\int_t^T\!f(t,u)\,du\right)\]
● LMM & IR Derivatives
LIBOR fwd
\[dL_i(t) = \sigma_i L_i(t)\,d\widetilde{z}_i\quad(\text{under }\mathbb{Q}^{T_{i+1}})\]
Caplet
\[\tau P(0,T_{i+1})\bigl[L_i\Phi(d_1)-K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\]
Swap rate
\[S_0 = \frac{P(0,T_0)-P(0,T_n)}{\sum_{i=1}^n\tau P(0,T_i)}\]
Swaption
\[A\bigl[S_0\Phi(d_1)-K\Phi(d_2)\bigr],\quad A=\sum\tau P(0,T_i)\]
● Credit Risk & Numerics
Merton
\[E = V\Phi(d_1) - De^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]
Credit spread
\[s = -\tfrac{1}{T}\ln\!\left(\tfrac{P_{\text{risky}}}{P_{\text{rf}}}\right) \approx h(1-\phi)\]
CDS spread
\[s_{\text{CDS}} = h(1-\phi),\quad h = \frac{s_{\text{CDS}}}{1-\phi}\]
Floor (Black)
\[\tau P(0,T_{i+1})\bigl[K\Phi(-d_2)-F_i\Phi(-d_1)\bigr]\]
Log-Euler
\[\ln S_{t+h} = \ln S_t + (\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)h + \sigma\varepsilon\sqrt{h}\]
MC error
\[\text{SE} = \hat{\sigma}/\sqrt{n}\]
● Martingales & Change of Measure
RN pricing
\[V_0 = e^{-rT}\,\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\]
Girsanov
\[\frac{d\mathbb{Q}}{d\mathbb{P}} = \exp\!\left(-\lambda z_T - \tfrac{1}{2}\lambda^2 T\right)\]
Fwd measure
\[V_0 = P(0,T)\,\mathbb{E}^{T}[V_T]\]
Martingale
\[dM = \sigma_M M\,d\widetilde{z} \implies M\text{ is }\mathbb{Q}\text{-martingale}\]

June 2025 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Let the spot exchange rate for a foreign currency be \(S\), which denotes the value of one unit of the foreign currency measured in the domestic currency. Assume that the exchange rate can be modeled (under the original probability measure \(P\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dS = \mu_S S\,dt + \sigma_S S\,dz\] where \(\mu_S\) and \(\sigma_S > 0\) are constants, and where \(dt\) and \(dz\) are the standard shorthand notations for a small time-step and a Brownian increment. Assume that the continuously compounded domestic and foreign risk-free interest rates are constants \(r\) and \(r_f\), respectively.
(a) What is the forward exchange rate, \(F(0,T)\), i.e. the contracted price for the delivery of one unit of the foreign currency at \(T\)? Find the answer by an arbitrage argument.
(b) What will the drift rate of the exchange rate \(S\) be under the domestic risk neutral probability measure (\(Q\)) and a no-arbitrage assumption? Comment on the influence of the difference of domestic and foreign interest rates on the result.
(c) Let \(Z = 1/S\) be the exchange rate as measured in the foreign currency. Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(Z\) where you eventually substitute \(S\) by \(1/Z\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hull, Section 17.5. The forward exchange rate \(F(0,T)\) is the price for one unit of the foreign currency to be delivered and paid for at time \(T\) as contracted at time 0. There are the following two ways at time 0 to attain one unit of the foreign currency at \(T\):
A) Borrow \(S_0 e^{-r_f T}\) in the domestic currency at the domestic interest rate \(r\), and use the proceeds to buy \(e^{-r_f T}\) units of the foreign currency. Place the foreign currency at deposit earning the foreign interest rate \(r_f\), and at time \(T\) you will have 1 unit of the foreign currency in your deposit (with no net outlay at time 0). At time \(T\) also repay the domestic currency loan which now stands at \(S_0 e^{-r_f T}e^{rT} = S_0 e^{(r-r_f)T}\).
B) Or you can enter into the forward contract to buy 1 unit of the foreign currency paying \(F(0,T)\) at time \(T\).
If there is to be no arbitrage opportunities we must have \[F(0,T) = S_0 e^{(r-r_f)T}\]
(b) Refer Hull, Chapter 17. The drift rate under \(Q\) will be \((r - r_f)\) (proportional to \(S\)) because the total return on holding the foreign currency must be \(r\) (we can treat the return on the foreign currency as we treat a continuously paid dividend on a stock).
(c) Refer Hull, Questions 17.31 (11th ed). The transformation \(Z\) is independent of \(t\), and you (using \(\frac{\partial Z}{\partial S} = -S^{-2}\) and \(\frac{\partial^2 Z}{\partial S^2} = 2S^{-3}\)) get \[dZ = \Bigl(-S^{-2}\mu_S S + \tfrac{1}{2}\cdot 2S^{-3}\sigma_S^2 S^2\Bigr)dt + S^{-2}\sigma_S S\,dz\] where we for the volatility use the sign convention that this is positive. By substitution we get \[dZ = \bigl(\sigma_S^2 - \mu_S\bigr)Z\,dt + \sigma_S Z\,dz\] (under \(P\)) or equivalently \(dZ = (\sigma_S^2 + r_f - r)Z\,dt + \sigma_S Z\,dz\) (under \(Q\)). A solution under either of the two measures is satisfactory. The exchange rate when measured in the foreign currency has the same volatility, but the drift changes, such that \(\mu_S\) quite intuitively enters with a negative sign. However, since the transformation is not linear, the two PDEs are not symmetric, and a volatility adjustment also enters into the drift rate. In what is not part of the syllabus and thus not required of the answer, this asymmetry (Siegel's Paradox, Hull Business Snapshot 30.1) is resolved if we change measure to the foreign risk neutral probability measure \(Q_f\) (where we should add \(-\sigma_S^2\) to the drift).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward exchange rate — arbitrage

Step 1 — Two ways to own 1 unit of foreign currency at T
Strategy A: Borrow \(S_0 e^{-r_f T}\) at domestic rate \(r\). Buy \(e^{-r_f T}\) foreign currency units and invest at \(r_f\). At \(T\): foreign account = 1 unit. Domestic loan repayment = \(S_0 e^{-r_f T}\cdot e^{rT} = S_0 e^{(r-r_f)T}\).
Strategy B: Enter forward contract, pay \(F(0,T)\) at \(T\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: costs must be equal \[\boxed{F(0,T) = S_0\,e^{(r-r_f)T}}\]

(b) Drift under domestic risk-neutral measure Q

Step 1 — Foreign currency earns \(r_f\) like a dividend yield
Under \(Q\), total expected return on holding foreign currency = \(r\) (domestic risk-free rate).
Capital gain drift + \(r_f\) = \(r\), so capital gain drift = \((r-r_f)\).

Step 2 — Result \[\boxed{dS = (r - r_f)S\,dt + \sigma_S S\,dz^Q}\] If \(r > r_f\): currency expected to appreciate. If \(r < r_f\): expected to depreciate (uncovered interest rate parity).

(c) Process for \(Z = 1/S\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Partial derivatives of \(Z = S^{-1}\) \[\frac{\partial Z}{\partial t} = 0,\quad \frac{\partial Z}{\partial S} = -S^{-2},\quad \frac{\partial^2 Z}{\partial S^2} = 2S^{-3}\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô's lemma, use \((dS)^2 = \sigma_S^2 S^2\,dt\) \[dZ = (-S^{-2})(\mu_S S\,dt + \sigma_S S\,dz) + \tfrac{1}{2}(2S^{-3})\sigma_S^2 S^2\,dt\] \[dZ = (-\mu_S S^{-1} + \sigma_S^2 S^{-1})\,dt - \sigma_S S^{-1}\,dz\]

Step 3 — Substitute \(S^{-1} = Z\) \[\boxed{dZ = (\sigma_S^2 - \mu_S)Z\,dt + \sigma_S Z\,dz}\quad\text{(under }P\text{)}\] Under \(Q\): replace \(\mu_S\) with \(r-r_f\) giving \(dZ = (\sigma_S^2 + r_f - r)Z\,dt + \sigma_S Z\,dz^Q\).

Question 2

Suppose that certain securities have prices that depend on a single state variable \(\theta\) given by the process \[\frac{d\theta}{\theta} = m\,dt + s\,dz\] where \(dz\) is a Brownian increment. Consider two such securities (i.e. with risk derived from \(\theta\)) with non-zero prices \(f_1\) and \(f_2\) and no dividends. Assume that the prices of the securities follow geometric Brownian motions. Also, assume a continuously compounded risk-free interest rate of \(r\).
(a) Find an arbitrage relationship between the drift rate of the two securities, their risk (expressed as volatility) and the risk free interest rate.
(b) Derive an expression for a general risk premium, \(\lambda\), per unit of \(\theta\)-risk.
(c) What will the relationship be for a security that earns a continuously compounded dividend rate of \(\delta\)?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hull, section 28.1. Note that \(\theta\) is a state variable and not itself a traded asset. Suppose that the processes followed by \(f_1\) and \(f_2\) are \(\frac{df_1}{f_1} = \mu_1\,dt + \sigma_1\,dz\) and \(\frac{df_2}{f_2} = \mu_2\,dt + \sigma_2\,dz\) where \(\mu_1, \mu_2, \sigma_1\), and \(\sigma_2\) are functions of \(\theta\) and \(t\). Now form a portfolio of \(\sigma_2 f_2\) units of the first security and \(-\sigma_1 f_1\) of the second security, and note that this portfolio is locally risk free, i.e. does not depend on \(z\). Thus it must in arbitrage equilibrium have a drift rate equal to the risk free interest rate \(r\). The value of the portfolio \(\Pi\) will be \(\Pi = (\sigma_2 f_2)f_1 - (\sigma_1 f_1)f_2\) and the (risk-less) drift of this will be \(\mu_1\sigma_2 f_1 f_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1 f_1 f_2 = r(\sigma_2 f_1 f_2 - \sigma_1 f_1 f_2)\) where the equal sign follows from the above arbitrage argument. Divide by \(f_1 f_2\) and rearrange to get: \[(\mu_1 - r)\sigma_2 = (\mu_2 - r)\sigma_1 \quad \text{and} \quad \frac{\mu_1 - r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2 - r}{\sigma_2}\] Thus the two securities have the same return in excess of the risk free interest rate per unit of risk.
(b) Since the two securities were chosen arbitrarily the above relationship must hold for all such traded securities in this economy. This motivates a definition of a risk premium per unit of risk by \[\lambda = \frac{\mu - r}{\sigma}\]
(c) Refer Hull, question 28.7. If there is a security with price \(f\) and a continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\) and drift rate of \(\mu\), consider a new security identical but for dividends continuously being reinvested in the security. This new security must have a drift rate of \(\mu + \delta\) and same proportional risk. Thus this security can be treated as the previously analysed securities, and it must satisfy: \[\mu + \delta - r = \lambda\sigma\]

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Market Price of Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) No-arbitrage: market price of risk from riskless portfolio

Step 1 — Write processes for \(f_1, f_2\): Both depend on \(\theta\), so: \[\frac{df_i}{f_i} = \mu_i\,dt + \sigma_i\,dz,\quad i=1,2\]

Step 2 — Form a riskless portfolio: Hold \(+\sigma_2 f_2\) units of \(f_1\) and \(-\sigma_1 f_1\) units of \(f_2\). Portfolio value: \(\Pi = \sigma_2 f_2 f_1 - \sigma_1 f_1 f_2 = (\sigma_2 - \sigma_1)f_1 f_2\).

Step 3 — Compute \(d\Pi\) (assuming \(f_1 f_2\) changes governed by Itô): \[d\Pi = \sigma_2 f_2\,df_1 - \sigma_1 f_1\,df_2\] \[= \sigma_2 f_2(\mu_1 f_1\,dt + \sigma_1 f_1\,dz) - \sigma_1 f_1(\mu_2 f_2\,dt + \sigma_2 f_2\,dz)\] \[= f_1 f_2(\mu_1\sigma_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1)\,dt + f_1 f_2(\sigma_1\sigma_2 - \sigma_1\sigma_2)\,dz\] The \(dz\)-terms cancel exactly: \(d\Pi\) is riskless.

Step 4 — Riskless portfolio earns \(r\): \(d\Pi = r\Pi\,dt\) \[f_1 f_2(\mu_1\sigma_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1)\,dt = r(\sigma_2-\sigma_1)f_1 f_2\,dt\] Divide by \(f_1 f_2\), rearrange: \[\mu_1\sigma_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1 = r\sigma_2 - r\sigma_1\] \[(\mu_1-r)\sigma_2 = (\mu_2-r)\sigma_1\] \[\boxed{\frac{\mu_1-r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2-r}{\sigma_2} =: \lambda}\]

(b) Market price of risk \(\lambda\)

The ratio \(\lambda = (\mu-r)/\sigma\) is the same for every security driven by the same Brownian \(z\) — it is the excess return per unit of risk. \(\lambda\) depends on \(\theta\) and \(t\) but not on which derivative we choose.

(c) Security with continuous dividend rate \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Create a reinvestment process: continuously reinvest dividends back into \(f\). The reinvested process has drift \(\mu + \delta\) and same volatility \(\sigma\). This composite process is non-dividend-paying, so the no-arbitrage condition applies: \[\frac{(\mu + \delta) - r}{\sigma} = \lambda\] \[\boxed{\mu + \delta - r = \lambda\sigma}\] Dividend income is part of total return; excess total return per unit risk = \(\lambda\).

Question 3

Suppose that the price at time \(t\) of a zero-coupon bond maturing at time \(T\), \(P(t,T)\) follows the Itô-process \[dP(t,T) = \mu_P P(t,T)\,dt + \sigma_P P(t,T)\,dz\] and the price \(f\) of a derivative dependent on the bond follows the process \[df = \mu_f f\,dt + \sigma_f f\,dz\] Assume only one source of uncertainty represented by \(z\), and that \(f\) provides no income.
(a) What is the forward price \(F(t,T)\) of \(f\) at time \(t\) for a contract maturing at time \(T\)?
(b) What is the process followed by \(F\) in a world defined by \(P(t,T)\) as numeraire? (Hint: Analyze the ratio \(f/P(t,T)\) by using Itô's lemma on the logarithm.)
(c) What is the process followed by \(F\) in the traditional (\(Q\)) risk-neutral world?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) With no income from \(f\) no-arbitrage means that \[F(t,T) = \frac{f(t)}{P(t,T)}\] since you can either buy the derivative with price \(f\) at time \(t\) financed by a short sale of \(\frac{f(t)}{P(t,T)}\) bonds (you issue bonds to receive the needed \(f\)), you repay the loan at time \(T\); or enter into the forward contract. In both cases, there is no cash flow at time \(t\), and you have one unit of \(f\) at time \(T\).
(b) Refer Hull section 28.3. From Itô's lemma we get: \[d\ln P = \bigl(\mu_P - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_P\,dz\] \[d\ln f = \bigl(\mu_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_f\,dz\] Therefore, \[d\ln\frac{f}{P} = d(\ln f - \ln P) = \bigl(\mu_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2 - \mu_P + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2\bigr)dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)\,dz\] so that (in a last step again using the Itô-lemma, but now on the exponential function) \[d\frac{f}{P} = \bigl(\mu_f - \mu_P + \sigma_P^2 - \sigma_P\sigma_f\bigr)\frac{f}{P}\,dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)\frac{f}{P}\,dz\] or \(dF = (\mu_f - \mu_P + \sigma_P^2 - \sigma_P\sigma_f)F\,dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)F\,dz\).
(c) In the (\(Q\)-) risk neutral world prices of both the traded instruments \(f(t)\) and \(P(t,T)\) will have a drift rate equal to the instantaneous short rate \(r\), thus \(\mu_f = \mu_P = r\). We see that the drift derived above is altered by the change of probability measure but is not equal to zero. However, the volatility part remains unchanged. Note also that while the drift rate of \(F\) under \(Q\) will not generally be \(r\) (because \(F\) is not the price of a traded asset, however for a given fixed contract price \(K\) the value of that contract will have a drift rate of \(r\) under \(Q\)). You may also note that in a (\(Q^T\)-) risk neutral world defined by the numeraire \(P(t,T)\), the drift of \(F\) is zero. The process for \(F\) in this world is thus \(dF = (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)F\,dz\). Also here, the volatility is unchanged by the change of measure. Either of the above observations (under \(Q\) or \(Q^T\)) will be considered as a satisfactory answer.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing  ·  Numeraire & Change of Measure   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward price \(F(t,T) = f(t)/P(t,T)\)

Step 1 — Two ways to hold \(f\) at time \(T\) with no cash at \(t\):
A) Buy \(f\) now, finance by selling \(f(t)/P(t,T)\) bonds (receive bonds, repay at \(T\)).
B) Enter forward contract at price \(F(t,T)\).
Both give 1 unit of \(f\) at \(T\) with zero upfront cost. No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F(t,T) = \frac{f(t)}{P(t,T)}}\]

(b) Process for \(F\) under T-forward measure

Step 1 — Apply Itô to \(\ln f\) and \(\ln P\) (since \(df/f = \mu_f\,dt + \sigma_f\,dz\), \(dP/P = \mu_P\,dt + \sigma_P\,dz\)): \[d\ln f = \bigl(\mu_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_f\,dz\] \[d\ln P = \bigl(\mu_P - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_P\,dz\]

Step 2 — Since \(\ln F = \ln f - \ln P\): \[d\ln F = \bigl(\mu_f - \mu_P + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2\bigr)dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)\,dz\]

Step 3 — Exponentiate: apply Itô to \(F = e^{\ln F}\).
Let \(X = \ln F\), so \(dX = a\,dt + b\,dz\) where \(a = \mu_f - \mu_P + \tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_P^2 - \sigma_f^2)\), \(b = \sigma_f - \sigma_P\).
Itô on \(e^X\): \(dF = F\bigl(dX + \tfrac{1}{2}(dX)^2\bigr) = F\bigl[(a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2)dt + b\,dz\bigr]\)
Compute \(a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2\): \[a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2 = \mu_f - \mu_P + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2 + \tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f - \sigma_P)^2\] \[= \mu_f - \mu_P + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2 + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2 - \sigma_f\sigma_P + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_P^2\] \[= \mu_f - \mu_P + \sigma_P^2 - \sigma_f\sigma_P = \mu_f - \mu_P + \sigma_P(\sigma_P - \sigma_f)\] \[\boxed{dF = \bigl(\mu_f - \mu_P + \sigma_P(\sigma_P-\sigma_f)\bigr)F\,dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_P)F\,dz}\]

Step 4 — Under \(Q^T\) (bond \(P(t,T)\) as numeraire): by the T-forward measure, \(F\) is a martingale (no drift): \[\boxed{dF = (\sigma_f - \sigma_P)F\,dz^T}\]

(c) Process for \(F\) under standard risk-neutral measure \(Q\)

Step 1 — Under \(Q\): all traded assets earn \(r\), so \(\mu_f = \mu_P = r\): \[dF = \bigl(r - r + \sigma_P(\sigma_P-\sigma_f)\bigr)F\,dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_P)F\,dz^Q\] \[\boxed{dF = \sigma_P(\sigma_P-\sigma_f)F\,dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_P)F\,dz^Q}\] Under \(Q\), \(F\) has non-zero drift because \(F = f/P\) is not itself a traded asset price. Only under \(Q^T\) (using \(P(t,T)\) as numeraire) does \(F\) become driftless.

August 2025 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Consider an economy with securities available for investment at time \(t = 0\) and with ultimate payment at time \(t = T\). A stock and a zero coupon bond can be purchased at time 0 at prices \(S_0\) and \(e^{-rT}\), respectively, where \(r\) is a constant. Neither of these securities has payments between time 0 and \(T\). Assume that at time \(T\) the realization of two possible states of the world is revealed. The stock has a payoff of \(uS_0\) and \(dS_0\), depending on the state, where \(u > a > d \geq 0\) are constants, and \(a = e^{rT}\). The bond has a payment of 1 at \(t = T\), irrespective of the state.
(a) Explain what is meant by risk neutral probabilities and (Arrow-Debreu) state prices and determine these for this economy.
(b) What is the forward price \(F(0,T)\) at time 0 for delivery of the stock at time \(T\)? What is the arbitrage argument?
(c) Consider a forward contract on a stock maturing at time \(T\). Compare the price of a European put option on the stock with the price of a European put on the forward contract on the stock, both maturing at \(T\) and having the same strike \(K\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) State prices \(\psi\) are implicit prices that satisfy that current prices of each security is the sum of the time and state dependent payoffs of the security at corresponding (security independent) state prices. Risk neutral probabilities \(q\) are probabilities that satisfy that current prices of each security is the discounted expected payoff using of these (security independent) probabilities and discounting with a risk free rate. The existence of both follows if there are no arbitrage opportunities in the economy. In the concrete case, the state prices \((\psi_u, \psi_d)\) satisfy the two equations: \[1 = a\psi_u + a\psi_d \quad \text{and} \quad 1 = u\psi_u + d\psi_d\] with the solution \(\psi = (\psi_u, \psi_d) = \left(a^{-1}\frac{a-d}{u-d},\, a^{-1}\frac{u-a}{u-d}\right)\) which are well defined and positive with our assumptions. The risk neutral probabilities are \(q = (q_u, q_d) = a\psi\), and note that \(q_u + q_d = 1\).
(b) Consider two strategies to end with the stock at time \(T\): A) Enter into a forward contract. There is no cash flow at time 0. You pay the agreed forward price at time \(T\). B) Borrow \(S_0\) at the risk free rate (i.e. shorting the bond), buy the stock and hold it to time \(T\), where you repay the loan by paying \(aS_0\). Here also there is no net cash flow at time 0. In the absence of arbitrage opportunities, the forward price contracted at time 0 is thus \(F(0,T) = aS_0\).
(c) Since the forward contract will expire with \(F(T,T) = S_T\), the two put options will have the same price.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Binomial Model  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Risk-neutral probabilities and Arrow-Debreu state prices

Risk-neutral probabilities: \(q,1-q\) such that every asset's expected payoff discounted at \(r\) equals its current price. Not real-world probabilities.

Step 1 — Stock no-arbitrage condition \[S_0 = e^{-rT}[q\cdot uS_0 + (1-q)\cdot dS_0]\]

Step 2 — Divide by \(S_0\), multiply by \(e^{rT} = a\) \[a = qu + d - qd = d + q(u-d)\] \[a - d = q(u-d)\] \[\boxed{q = \frac{a-d}{u-d},\qquad 1-q = \frac{u-a}{u-d}}\]

Arrow-Debreu state prices: \(\psi_u,\psi_d\) = price today of £1 in one state, £0 in other. \[\boxed{\psi_u = e^{-rT}\cdot q = a^{-1}\frac{a-d}{u-d},\qquad \psi_d = e^{-rT}(1-q) = a^{-1}\frac{u-a}{u-d}}\] Check: \(\psi_u + \psi_d = e^{-rT}\) = bond price \(\checkmark\)

(b) Forward price \(F(0,T)\) — arbitrage argument

Step 1 — Two strategies to own stock at T
A) Buy stock at \(S_0\), finance with loan at \(r\). Cost at \(T\): \(S_0 e^{rT} = aS_0\).
B) Enter forward at price \(F(0,T)\), pay at \(T\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage \[\boxed{F(0,T) = S_0 e^{rT} = aS_0}\]

(c) Put on stock vs put on forward — comparison

Step 1 — Payoffs at T
Put on stock: \(\max(K - S_T, 0)\)
Put on forward: \(\max(K - F_T, 0)\). At expiry, forward price \(F_T = S_T\), so payoff = \(\max(K - S_T, 0)\).

Step 2 — Conclusion
Payoffs are identical at \(T\). By no-arbitrage: \[\boxed{p_{\text{stock}} = p_{\text{forward}}}\] The prices are the same.

Question 2

(a) In the one-factor Gaussian model of defaults the default of an obligor \(i\) is indicated by \[x_i = a_i F + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}\,Z_i\] where constants \(a_i \in [-1,1]\), and the random variables \(F\) and \(Z_i\), \(i = 1,\ldots,n\), have independent standard normal distributions. Give an interpretation of the model and its parameters. What is the (copula) default correlation between \(x_i\) and \(x_j\) for some \(j \neq i\)?
(b) In this model the indicator \(x_i\) is compared to the probability \(Q_i(T)\) that the obligor has defaulted before or at time \(T\). Suppose that the obligor defaults at time \(t\). Which value of the indicator \(x_i\) does this correspond to?
(c) Explain for a tranched structured product such as a Credit Debt Obligation what the terms attachment point and detachment point refer to.
(d) With many obligors, is the value of the most senior tranche in general positively or negatively influenced by an increase in the default correlation of obligors, keeping the average credit spread constant? (Hint: Consider the extreme case where defaults are totally dependent, so all obligors either default or not, and the extreme case where there is no systematic risk and very many obligors.)

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull sections 24.8 and 25.10. Note that \(F\) is a common factor determining the covariance structure, whereas \(Z_i\) are idiosyncratic factors, and \(a_i\) are sensitivities to the common factor. Note that \(\text{Var}[x_i] = a_i^2 + (1-a_i^2) = 1\) and \(\text{Cov}[x_i,x_j] = a_i a_j\) using that \(F\) and \(Z_i\) are standard normal and independent. In implementation the \(a_i\) are often approximated as the correlation between \(i\)'s equity return and some broad market index (i.e. P-type correlations). They could also be inferred from e.g. prices of tranched CDOs (i.e. Q-type correlations)).
(b) The indicator can be interpreted as corresponding to a value of \(\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(t))\) (where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal distribution function), i.e. we investigate defaults on the inverse normal evaluated on the relevant percentile of the given (weakly increasing) cumulated probability of default such that an observation of a larger \(x_i\) than this critical value means a default before or at \(t\), i.e. a large \(x_i\) indicates a late default (or no default if after the maturity of the debt).
(c) Refer to Hull section 25.8. The payoff of the debt instruments underlying a cash CDO (or credit derivatives underlying a synthetic CDO) is distributed to the different tranches of the CDO so that the most senior tranche has priority to the payoffs (in a waterfall structure). Losses thus first hit the most junior tranche, called the equity tranche. The specific tranche is characterized by its attachment point and detachment point. The attachment point is the level of total losses relative to the total underlying portfolio that are protected by more junior tranches (thus for the equity tranche the attachment point is zero). For larger losses than the attachment point of the tranche the tranche will suffer losses. The detachment point is the level of total losses relative to the total portfolio that will deplete the tranche completely. There are further arrangements to protect the senior tranches, but the above will be a satisfactory answer.
(d) Refer to Hull section 25.9. The most senior tranche will — expected loss and recovery being equal as reflected in a constant credit spread — in general benefit from diversification (and thus from low correlations), since then the stochastic losses will be closer to the expected losses and less likely to hit the most senior tranche. On the contrary, with high correlation the dispersion of total losses will be high with some risk of hitting the senior tranche.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Gaussian copula model — interpretation and copula correlation

Model: \(x_i = a_i F + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}\,Z_i\), where \(F, Z_i \sim N(0,1)\) independently.

Interpretation:
\(F\) = common systematic factor (macro/market conditions).
\(Z_i\) = idiosyncratic (firm-specific) factor.
\(a_i\) = sensitivity to systematic factor. \(a_i^2\) = share of systematic risk. \(1-a_i^2\) = idiosyncratic share.

Step 1 — Verify variance \[\text{Var}(x_i) = a_i^2\text{Var}(F) + (1-a_i^2)\text{Var}(Z_i) = a_i^2 + (1-a_i^2) = 1\]

Step 2 — Covariance between \(x_i\) and \(x_j\), \(j\neq i\) \[\text{Cov}(x_i,x_j) = \text{Cov}(a_i F + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i,\; a_j F + \sqrt{1-a_j^2}Z_j) = a_i a_j \cdot 1\] (Cross terms zero since \(F, Z_i, Z_j\) independent.)

Step 3 — Copula correlation \[\boxed{\rho_{ij} = a_i a_j}\]

(b) Default threshold and conditional default probability

Step 1 — Default indicator
\(x_i\) has standard normal distribution. Default before \(T\) if \(\Phi(x_i) \leq Q_i(T)\), i.e.: \[x_i \leq \Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T)) =: C_i(T)\]

Step 2 — Conditional probability given systematic factor \(F\) \[P(\text{default}_i \mid F) = P\!\left(\sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i \leq C_i(T) - a_i F\right)\] \[\boxed{P(\text{default}_i \mid F) = \Phi\!\left(\frac{C_i(T) - a_i F}{\sqrt{1-a_i^2}}\right)}\]

(c) Tranched CDO — structure, attachment and detachment points

Losses flow to tranches from bottom up (waterfall structure).
Equity tranche: first to absorb losses (attachment=0%, detachment=e.g.3%).
Mezzanine: absorbs intermediate losses (e.g. 3%–7%).
Senior: only absorbs losses after lower tranches are wiped out (e.g. 7%–100%).
Attachment point: portfolio loss % at which tranche starts losing. Detachment point: loss % at which tranche is fully wiped out.

(d) Effect of default correlation on tranches

Low correlation: losses are diversified → concentrated in junior tranches → senior very safe, senior spread low.
High correlation: either most or none default → senior also exposed → senior spread rises.
Senior benefits from low correlation. Equity benefits from high correlation (all-or-nothing outcomes).

Question 3

Consider a one factor interest rate model with the following process for the instantaneous short rate \(r\): \[dr = m(r,t)\,dt + s(r,t)\,dz\] where \(z\) is a Brownian motion.
(a) View the price of a zero-coupon bond with maturity date \(T\) as a function \(P_T(r,t)\). Use Itô's lemma to find an expression for the process that \(dP_T(r,t)\) follows. What will the drift rate be under the traditional risk neutral probability measure (\(Q\))?
(b) In the Vasicek model the instantaneous short term interest rate \(r\) is described by the process: \[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] where \(a \geq 0\), \(b\) and \(\sigma\) are constants. Give an interpretation of the model and its parameters.
(c) Explain that the Vasicek model is not generally consistent with a given initial interest rate curve. How can the model be modified to be consistent with such an initial curve?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) A direct implementation of the Itô-lemma yields (with function arguments suppressed): \[dP_T = \Bigl(\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t} + \tfrac{1}{2}s^2\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2} + m\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}\Bigr)dt + s\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}\,dz\] The expected return under \(Q\) must be the risk free rate, so the drift rate of the zero coupon price must be \(rP_T\).
(b) Refer Hull section 31.2. The interest rate will exhibit a mean reversion to a level of \(b\) at a rate determined by \(a\). It will have normally distributed innovations of size determined by \(\sigma\). As rates conditional on the initial level are normally distributed they can become negative.
(c) Refer Hull section 32.1. The Vasicek model is of the so-called equilibrium type of interest rate models that has an initial yield curve as an output. The model has only a few parameters to calibrate to an initial yield curve. The so-called no-arbitrage models can take an initial yield curve as an input. As shown by Hull and White the Vasicek model can be modified to a no-arbitrage model by letting the parameter \(b\) above be a function of time. It can be explicitly derived (which is not required here).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Itô's lemma on \(P_T(r,t)\) — process and Q-measure drift

Step 1 — Apply Itô's lemma with \(dr = m\,dt + s\,dz\) \[dP_T = \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}dr + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2}(dr)^2\]

Step 2 — Substitute \((dr)^2 = s^2\,dt\) \[\boxed{dP_T = \left(\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t} + m\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r} + \tfrac{1}{2}s^2\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2}\right)dt + s\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}\,dz}\]

Step 3 — Drift under \(Q\)
Under \(Q\), bond price must earn the short rate \(r\): \[\boxed{\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t} + m^Q\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r} + \tfrac{1}{2}s^2\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2} = r P_T}\]

(b) Vasicek model — mean reversion

Model under Q: \(dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz^Q\)

Interpretation:
Mean reversion to \(b\) at speed \(a\): when \(r > b\), drift is negative (pulled down); when \(r < b\), drift is positive (pulled up).
Normally distributed increments \(\Rightarrow\) rates can go negative.
Volatility is constant \(\sigma\).

(c) Hull-White extension vs Vasicek

Vasicek is an equilibrium model: the initial term structure is determined by \(a,b,r_0\) — it may not match observed market prices.
Hull-White replaces \(ab\) with a time-dependent \(\theta(t)\) to exactly fit the current term structure, making it arbitrage-free with respect to currently traded bonds.

June 2024 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Consider an economy with securities available for investment at time \(t = 0\) and with ultimate payment at time \(t = T\). A stock and a zero coupon bond can be purchased at \(t = 0\) at prices \(S_0\) and \(e^{-rT}\), respectively, where \(r\) is a constant. Neither of these securities has payments between time 0 and \(T\). At time \(T\) the realization of two possible states of the world is revealed. The stock has a payoff of \(uS_0\) and \(dS_0\), depending on the state, where \(u > e^{rT} > d \geq 0\) are constants. The bond has a payment of 1 at \(t = T\), irrespective of the state.
(a) Explain what is meant by (Arrow-Debreu) state prices and risk neutral probabilities and determine these for this economy.
(b) What is the arbitrage free price at \(t = 0\) for a call and a put on the stock, both options with maturity at \(t = T\) and strike \(K\)?
(c) Consider a portfolio at \(t = 0\) that is long the call and short the put. Also consider a forward contract on the stock priced so the current value is zero. What is the difference in value and payoff of the option portfolio and the forward?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hansen chapter 1 and Hull chapter 13. State prices \(\psi\) are implicit prices that satisfy that current prices of each security is the sum of the time and state dependent payoffs of the security and corresponding (security independent) state prices. Risk neutral probabilities \(q\) are probabilities that satisfy that current prices of each security is the discounted expected payoff using of these (security independent) probabilities and discounting with a risk-free rate. Existence of both follows if there are no arbitrage opportunities in the economy. Let for ease of notation \(a = e^{rT}\). In the concrete case the state prices \((\psi_u, \psi_d)\) satisfies the two equations: \[1 = a\psi_u + a\psi_d \quad \text{and} \quad 1 = u\psi_u + d\psi_d\] with the solution \(\psi = (\psi_u, \psi_d) = \left(a^{-1}\frac{a-d}{u-d},\, a^{-1}\frac{u-a}{u-d}\right)\) which are well defined and positive with our assumptions. The risk neutral probabilities are \(q = (q_u, q_d) = a\psi\), and note that \(q_u + q_d = 1\) and \(\psi_u + \psi_d = 1/a\).
(b) This can be equivalently formulated using the state-prices and the risk neutral probabilities. The payoff of the call \(c_T\) and put \(p_T\) at time \(T\) respectively will be \(c_T = \max[S_T - K;\, 0]\) and \(p_T = \max[K - S_T;\, 0] = K - S_T + \max[S_T - K;\, 0]\). We find the price at \(t = 0\) to be \[c_0 = \psi_u\max[uS_0 - K;\, 0] + \psi_d\max[dS_0 - K;\, 0]\] \[p_0 = K/a - S_0 + c_0\]
(c) The value of a portfolio of the call and short the put at time 0 will by the above be \[c_0 - p_0 = c_0 - K/a + S_0 - c_0 = S_0 - K/a\] This is obviously also the value difference to the forward contract that by the definition has value 0 at time 0. Define the forward price that gives the forward contract value 0 at time \(t\) as \(F_0^T\). If arbitrage free it must be so that \(F_0^T = aS_0\) (the arbitrage argument is that you can get one stock at time \(T\) by either buying spot at price \(S_0\) or by buying the forward contract and place \(F_0^T/a\) at interest). The payoff at time \(T\) from the option-portfolio was shown to be \(c_T - p_T = S_T - K\), whereas the payoff from the forward is \(S_T - F_0^T = S_T - aS_0\). The difference in payoffs at time \(T\) is thus \(-K + aS_0 = F_0^T - K\). Since this is risk-free it has a time-0 value of \(S_0 - K/a\), i.e. the same as the difference in time-0 prices found above (consistent with the arbitrage free pricing assumed). Also it is the present value of the difference between the current forward price and the strike of the two options.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Binomial Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Arrow-Debreu state prices and risk-neutral probabilities

Risk-neutral probabilities: \(q,1-q\) such that discounted expected payoffs equal current prices.

Step 1 — Set up (let \(a = e^{rT}\))
Bond equation: \(1 = a\psi_u + a\psi_d\)
Stock equation: \(1 = u\psi_u + d\psi_d\)

Step 2 — Solve for \(\psi_u, \psi_d\)
From bond equation: \(\psi_u + \psi_d = 1/a\)
Subtract \(d\times\) bond from stock: \((u-d)\psi_u = 1 - d/a\)
\[\psi_u = \frac{a-d}{a(u-d)},\qquad \psi_d = \frac{u-a}{a(u-d)}\]

Step 3 — Risk-neutral probabilities \(q = a\psi_u\) \[\boxed{q = \frac{a-d}{u-d},\qquad 1-q = \frac{u-a}{u-d}}\] \[\boxed{\psi_u = e^{-rT}q,\qquad \psi_d = e^{-rT}(1-q)}\]

(b) Arbitrage-free call and put prices

Step 1 — Price = sum of AD prices × payoffs
Call payoffs: \(C_u = \max(uS_0 - K, 0)\), \(C_d = \max(dS_0 - K, 0)\).
Put payoffs: \(P_u = \max(K - uS_0, 0)\), \(P_d = \max(K - dS_0, 0)\).

Step 2 — Prices \[\boxed{c = e^{-rT}\bigl[q\cdot\max(uS_0-K,0) + (1-q)\cdot\max(dS_0-K,0)\bigr]}\] \[\boxed{p = e^{-rT}\bigl[q\cdot\max(K-uS_0,0) + (1-q)\cdot\max(K-dS_0,0)\bigr]}\] These are the unique no-arbitrage prices (complete market).

(c) Long call + short put vs forward contract

Step 1 — Payoff of (long call – short put) at T \[\max(S_T-K,0) - \max(K-S_T,0) = S_T - K\] This equals the payoff of a forward on the stock with delivery price \(K\).

Step 2 — Value at \(t=0\) of this portfolio \[c - p = e^{-rT}E^Q[S_T - K] = S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\]

Step 3 — Forward priced so current value = 0
Set \(c - p = 0\): the forward delivery price that achieves this is \(K = S_0 e^{rT} = F(0,T)\).
Conclusion: \(\boxed{c - p = S_0 - Ke^{-rT}}\) (put-call parity). If \(K = F(0,T)\), then \(c = p\).

Question 2

In the BSM-model for options on a stock paying no dividends before the expiry of the option we have the formula \(c = S_0\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\) for the price \(c\) of a call at current time 0, where \(S_0\) is the current stock price, \(K\) is the strike of the call, \(\Phi\) is the cumulative standard normal probability function, \(r\) is a constant, continuously compounded risk free interest rate, \(d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_0/K) + (r + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}\), \(d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T}\), where the stock price follows the Geometric Brownian Motion \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\), and \(z\) is a Brownian Motion.
(a) In a model by Merton the value of equity in a limited liability company with a zero coupon debt of a notional \(N\) maturing at \(T\) is seen as a call option on the value \(V\) of company assets. Assuming that the value of these assets can be described by a geometric Brownian motion derive a formula for pricing the zero coupon debt using the above model.
(b) Give an interpretation of \(\Phi(d_2)\) in this model.
(c) Define the gearing \(G = V_0/(Ne^{-rT})\). Show that the credit spread \(s\) on the zero coupon bond (implicitly defined by \(B_0 = e^{-(r+s)T}N\)) is \[-\frac{1}{T}\Bigl[\ln\Phi(d_2) + \Phi(-d_1)G\Bigr]\]

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hull section 24.6. Let the market value of equity be \(E_0\) at time \(t\). The market value of the debt will be \(B_0 = V_0 - E_0\) assuming no costs or frictions in the form of third party payments in the event of default. Using the Black-Scholes-Merton formula to price the equity as a call on the assets with strike \(N\) and maturity \(T\) you get \[B_0 = V_0 - V_0\Phi(d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2) = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] where we have altered the definition of \(d_1, d_2\) to fit our interpretation, i.e. \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(V_0/N) + (r + \frac{1}{2}\sigma_V^2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt{T}}, \quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_V\sqrt{T}\] where \(\sigma_V\) is the volatility of the asset value.
(b) In this model \(\Phi(d_2)\) is the risk neutral probability that the implicit call option of the shareholders is in the money at maturity — i.e. that the company is not in default (and equivalently \(1 - \Phi(d_2) = \Phi(-d_2)\) is the risk neutral probability that the implicit short put option of the creditors is in the money, i.e. that the company will be in default at the maturity of the debt).
(c) Refer Hull Practice Question 24.21. Let \(s\) be the credit spread implicitly defined by \(B_0 = e^{-(r+s)T}N\). By equating this with the formula above you get \[e^{-(r+s)T}N = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] Substitute \(V_0/G\) for \(Ne^{-rT}\) to get \(V_0 e^{-sT}/G = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + V_0/G \cdot \Phi(d_2)\). Divide by \(V_0/G > 0\) to get \(e^{-sT} = \Phi(-d_1)G + \Phi(d_2)\). Taking the natural logarithm and dividing by \(-T\) gives the desired result. It may be added that this is not the standard definition of gearing (which is with reference to equity rather than debt).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: BSM Model · Credit Risk | Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Merton model — equity as a call, derive debt value \(B_0\)

Step 1 — Equity = call option on firm assets
At maturity \(T\), shareholders get \(\max(V_T - N, 0)\) (limited liability).
Using BSM with asset value \(V_0\), volatility \(\sigma_V\), strike = debt face \(N\): \[E_0 = V_0\Phi(d_1) - Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] where \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(V_0/N) + (r+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_V^2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt{T}},\quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_V\sqrt{T}\]

Step 2 — Debt = Assets \(-\) Equity (no bankruptcy costs) \[B_0 = V_0 - E_0 = V_0 - V_0\Phi(d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[\boxed{B_0 = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)}\]

(b) Interpretation of \(\Phi(d_2)\)

Step 1 — Risk-neutral probability
In BSM, \(\Phi(d_2)\) = risk-neutral probability that \(V_T > N\) at maturity.

Step 2 — Credit interpretation \[\boxed{\Phi(d_2) = Q(\text{no default}), \quad \Phi(-d_2) = Q(\text{default})}\] Equivalently: \(\Phi(d_2)\) is the risk-neutral probability the firm's assets exceed debt at \(T\).

(c) Credit spread — derive \(s = -\dfrac{1}{T}\ln\!\bigl[\Phi(d_2) + \Phi(-d_1)G\bigr]\)

Step 1 — Define credit spread: \(B_0 = e^{-(r+s)T}N\)

Step 2 — Equate with Merton formula: \[e^{-(r+s)T}N = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]

Step 3 — Substitute gearing \(G = V_0 / (Ne^{-rT})\), so \(V_0 = G \cdot Ne^{-rT}\): \[e^{-(r+s)T}N = G\cdot Ne^{-rT}\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\]

Step 4 — Divide both sides by \(Ne^{-rT} > 0\): \[e^{-sT} = G\Phi(-d_1) + \Phi(d_2) = \Phi(d_2) + \Phi(-d_1)G\]

Step 5 — Take natural log and solve for \(s\): \[-sT = \ln\!\bigl[\Phi(d_2) + \Phi(-d_1)G\bigr]\] \[\boxed{s = -\frac{1}{T}\ln\!\bigl[\Phi(d_2) + \Phi(-d_1)G\bigr]}\]

Question 3

In the Vasicek Model the (instantaneous) short term interest rate \(r\) is described by the process: \[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] where \(a\), \(b\) and \(\sigma\) are positive constants, and \(z\) a Brownian Motion.
(a) What does this mean for the behaviour of the short term interest rate?
(b) In this model the solution for the price of a zero-coupon bond can be written \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\] Derive the duration of the bond, where the interest sensitivity is with respect to the short rate.
(c) In some models of the short term interest rate \(r\) (e.g. the Hull-White model) the drift rate is made a function of calendar time. What is the purpose of the extra flexibility compared to the Vasicek (or CIR) type of models?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hull chapter 31. It should be noted that the model implies a mean reversion of the spot rate to a level of \(b\) at a rate of \(a\). It should also be noted that the volatility term means that the short-term rate can go below 0.
(b) When the duration is thus defined we get \[D = -\frac{\partial P/\partial r}{P} = B(t,T)\] One may note that the standard definition of duration will give the maturity of a zero coupon bond as the duration.
(c) Refer Hull section 31.3. The time-dependent drift term in the Hull-White model is introduced to be able to incorporate a given, initial term structure, making the values derived from it “arbitrage-free” in relation to the existing securities priced on the current term structure (assuming these to be arbitrage free). This is in contrast to the CIR and Vasicek models of the “Equilibrium”-type that put restrictions on the possible initial term structure (as it is a function of the current spot rate only).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Vasicek model — behaviour of short rate

Model: \(dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\)

Mean reversion to level \(b\) at speed \(a\):
When \(r > b\): drift = \(a(b-r) < 0\) → rate pulled down.
When \(r < b\): drift = \(a(b-r) > 0\) → rate pulled up.
Stochastic term \(\sigma\,dz\): constant volatility, normally distributed → can go negative.

(b) Duration of Vasicek zero-coupon bond

Step 1 — Bond price form: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\)

Step 2 — Take partial derivative w.r.t. \(r\) \[\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = A(t,T)\cdot(-B(t,T))e^{-B(t,T)r} = -B(t,T)P\]

Step 3 — Duration definition \[\boxed{D = -\frac{1}{P}\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = B(t,T) = \frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}}\] Note: for a zero coupon bond the standard duration equals \(T-t\), but here \(D = B(t,T) \leq T-t\) because of mean reversion.

(c) Hull-White model — purpose of time-dependent drift

Vasicek (equilibrium model): initial term structure determined by few parameters — may not match market prices.
Hull-White replaces constant \(ab\) with \(\theta(t)\): calibrated to exactly fit the current observed term structure.
This makes the model arbitrage-free with respect to current bond prices (no-arbitrage/fitting model vs equilibrium model).

August 2024 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Assume that the log of price \(Z\) of a non-dividend paying stock can be modelled (under the original probability measure \(P\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dZ = \mu\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] where \(\mu\) and \(\sigma > 0\) are constants, and where \(dt\) and \(dz\) are the standard shorthand notations for a small time-step and a Brownian increment.
(a) Consider the transformation \(G\) of \(Z\) given by the exponential function, i.e. \(G(x) = e^x\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(S := G(Z)\).
(b) For a given initial value \(Z_t = Z_0\) what is the distribution of \(Z_T\)?
(c) What are main empirical shortcomings of the model of \(dZ\) as a model of stock returns?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer Hull, chapter 14 and 15. To use Itô's lemma on the transformation \(G\), note that \(\frac{\partial G}{\partial x} = e^x = \frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial x^2}\) (and that \(G\) does not depend on time \(t\)). Thus we have \[dS = dG(Z) = \Bigl(\frac{\partial G}{\partial Z}\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial Z^2}\sigma^2\Bigr)dt + \frac{\partial G}{\partial Z}\sigma\,dz = \bigl(\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\]
(b) By integration the change \(Z_T - Z_0\) will have a Normal distribution with mean \(\mu T\) and variance \(\sigma^2 T\). One may note that the prices will be log-normally distributed, but this comment is not required.
(c) One may first note that the model has continuous sample paths, which is at best an approximation to real world discrete price changes. Already in the assumptions we have also excluded dividends. The normal distribution of returns is at odds with empirically observed fat tailed return distributions.
