From the table it is seen that the payments for the payer swaption and the forward starting swap equals the payments on the receiver swaption. Thus barring arbitrage the equation must hold. If \(s_K\) is the current forward swap rate the value \(RFS\) is by definition zero. (Hull 9th ed., practice question 29.18).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Interest Rate Derivatives   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Payer and receiver swaption definitions

Payer swaption: right to pay fixed \(s_K\), receive LIBOR from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\). Valuable when market swap rate \(s_{T_1} > s_K\).
Receiver swaption: right to receive fixed \(s_K\), pay LIBOR. Valuable when \(s_{T_1} < s_K\).

(b) Receiver swaption as call on a bond

Consider a fixed-rate coupon bond with coupon = swap rate \(s_K\), principal = notional, maturity = \(T_2\). At \(T_1\): receiving fixed \(s_K\) and paying LIBOR = owning this bond and financing with floating rate debt (par). Exercise the receiver swaption iff bond value at \(T_1 > \text{par}\) ⟺ \(s_{T_1} < s_K\). Therefore: receiver swaption = call on bond with strike = par. Coupon = \(s_K\), principal = notional.

(c) Swaption put-call parity

(i) Show \(PS + RFS = RS\)

Step 1 — Long receiver (\(RS\)) + short payer (\(-PS\)): at \(T_1\) exactly one swaption is exercised. Net position: receive fixed \(s_K\), pay LIBOR from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\) with certainty.

Step 2 — This is identical to the forward-starting receive-fixed swap \(RFS\). By no-arbitrage: \[RS - PS = RFS\] \[\boxed{PS + RFS = RS}\] where \(RFS = \displaystyle\sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s_K - f_k)\), with \(f_k\) the current forward rates.

(ii) Deduce \(PS = RS\) when \(s_K\) = forward swap rate

Step 1 — The forward swap rate \(s^*\) is the value of \(s_K\) that makes the forward-starting swap have zero value: \[RFS_{s^*} = \sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s^* - f_k) = 0\]

Step 2 — Substitute \(s_K = s^*\) into the parity: \[PS + \underbrace{RFS_{s^*}}_{=\,0} = RS\] \[\boxed{PS = RS}\] When the strike equals the forward swap rate, payer and receiver swaptions are equally valued — the interest rate analogue of put-call parity at-the-forward.

August 2021 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

(a) Define a forward contract on a stock.
(b) Consider a stock that does not pay dividends with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\). Assume a constant continuously compounded interest rate of \(r\). Using an arbitrage argument find the forward price \(F_t\) at time \(t\) on a forward contract on the stock that matures at time \(T > t\).
(c) Consider the value of a forward contract with forward price \(K\) at time \(t < T\) and assume that the stock price follows the geometric Brownian motion \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] Using Itô's lemma find the process followed by the value of the forward contract. What will the drift be under the risk neutral measure?
(d) Assume now that the stock pays a constant continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\). What will the forward price be?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Definition (Forward Contract). A Forward Contract is an agreement to buy or sell a quantity of an asset of a specified quality and type (called the underlying asset) delivered at a specified future time \(T\) (the expiration date) and at a specified place for an agreed delivery price \(K\) as measured in some defined numeraire/currency. The price is initially (at \(t\)) set at a level \(K = F(t,T)\), such that the value \(V_K(t,T)\) of the contract is 0, i.e. by definition \(V_{F(t,T)}(t,T) = 0\). The price \(F(t,T)\) is called the forward price at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\).
(b) The following arbitrage argument can be made: Buy the stock spot financed by a loan of \(S_t\) repaid at \(T\). The only cashflow on this will be the repayment \(S_t e^{r(T-t)}\) at \(T\), leaving you with the stock. An alternative is to enter into a forward contract with delivery at \(T\). To eliminate arbitrage it must be that the forward price at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\) is \(F_t = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\).
(c) For a forward contract with forward price \(K\) the value is \(V_t = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\). Using Itô's lemma you get that the differential form for the value process for the forward: \[dV_t = \bigl[\mu S_t - rKe^{-r(T-t)}\bigr]dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] For a currently priced forward (i.e. where \(K\) gives the forward contract a zero net present value \(K = F_t = e^{r(T-t)}S_t\)) we see that the drift is 0 under the risk neutral measure (by setting \(\mu = r\)), while a non-negative market value will grow at a rate \(r\). It will be considered ok just to derive this for the currently priced forward.
(d) In the arbitrage argument above you get too much if you buy the stock paying dividend, but you can repay and reduce the interest payments continuously leaving only \(S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\) to repay at \(T\). Alternatively consider the price of a synthetic stock paying no dividends and compare that to \(S_t\) and go on from there. In either case \(F_t = S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Definition of a forward contract

A forward contract is an agreement to buy (long) or sell (short) a specified asset at a specified future date \(T\) for a delivery price \(K\) agreed at inception. The forward price \(F(t,T)\) is set so the contract has zero value at inception: \(V_{F(t,T)}(t,T) = 0\).

(b) Forward price by arbitrage — no dividends

Step 1 — Two strategies to own 1 stock at \(T\):
A) Enter forward at \(F_t\). Net cash at \(T\): \(-F_t\) (pay forward price, receive stock).
B) Buy stock now for \(S_t\), borrow \(S_t\) at rate \(r\). At \(T\): own stock, repay \(S_t e^{r(T-t)}\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F_t = S_t\,e^{r(T-t)}}\]

(c) Process for forward contract value \(V_K(t,T)\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Value formula:
\[V_K(t,T) = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q[S_T - K] = e^{-r(T-t)}(F_t - K) = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô's lemma to \(V = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\):
Partial derivatives: \(\partial V/\partial S = 1\), \(\partial^2 V/\partial S^2 = 0\), \(\partial V/\partial t = rKe^{-r(T-t)}\). \[dV = \frac{\partial V}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial V}{\partial S}dS + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial S^2}(dS)^2\] \[= rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + dS_t\] \[= rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\]

Step 3 — Under the risk-neutral measure Q (replace \(\mu \to r\)): \[dV = rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + rS_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz = r\underbrace{(S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)})}_{=V}\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] \[\boxed{dV_K = r V_K\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz}\] Drift under Q = \(r V_K\) (forward value earns the risk-free rate). Diffusion = \(\sigma S_t\,dz\) (same absolute risk as the stock).

(d) Forward price — continuous dividend \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Buy \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares now, continuously reinvest dividends. At \(T\): 1 share. Cost now: \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\). Finance by borrowing, repay \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}e^{r(T-t)}\) at \(T\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F_t = S_t\,e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}}\]

Question 2

Assume that a stock with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\) pays no dividends before time \(T > t\), and that there is a constant continuously compounded risk free interest rate of \(r\). Let \(c(S_0,K,T,r)\) and \(p(S_0,K,T,r)\) be the price at time \(t=0\) of a European call and a European put, respectively, on the stock with the same strike \(K\) and expiry \(T\).
(a) Use the put-call-parity to find a relationship between the Thetas (\(\Theta\)) of the call and put. Comment on the result.
(b) Consider two call options with the same strike \(K\) but different expiry dates \(T_1\) and \(T_2\). Assume \(r \geq 0\) and use an arbitrage argument to show that the price of the longer call, \(c_2\), is (weakly) greater than the price of the shorter call, \(c_1\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The put-call parity for options with same maturity and same strike price on the same stock paying no dividend states that the price of long call less the short put must be equal to the forward price on the stock with the same delivery prices as the strike and the same maturity, i.e. \[c(S_0,K,T,r) - p(S_0,K,T,r) = S_0 - e^{-rT}K\] The Theta is the partial derivative with respect to calendar time, i.e. the negative partial derivative with respect to maturity \(T\). By taking the partial derivative we find: \[\Theta_c - \Theta_p = -re^{-rT}K\] This depends on the sign of \(r\). E.g. with a positive rate there is from discounting an implicit advantage for the call option of the later payment of the strike compared to the current date (and a similar disadvantage for the put of the strike received later). The negative sign on the right-hand side reflects that this relative advantage of the call between the two otherwise similar options becomes smaller as the maturity date gets closer. Hull (9ed, p.409) reports this for the special case of the BSM-model.
(b) One may first use the same argument as when showing that an early exercise of an American call under these assumptions is non-optimal. Consider a portfolio long the long call, long \(K\) zero coupon bonds maturing at \(T_2\) and short one stock. At time \(T_1\) this portfolio has a value of \[c_2(T_1) + e^{-r(T_2-T_1)}K - S_{T_1}\] while at \(T_2\) it will have a value of \[c_2(T_2) + K - S_{T_2} = \max[S_{T_2}-K;0] + K - S_{T_2} \geq 0\] So if this has a non-negative value at \(T_2\) the value found at \(T_1\) must also be non-negative, i.e. \[c_2(T_1) \geq S_{T_1} - e^{-r(T_2-T_1)}K\] Now we consider a portfolio of a long position in the long call and a short position in the short call. At time \(T_1\) first consider the case of \(S_{T_1} > K\). Then \[c_2(T_1) - c_1(T_1) \geq (S_{T_1} - e^{-r(T_2-T_1)}K) - (S_{T_1}-K) > 0\] Finally consider the case of \(S_{T_1} \leq K\). Then \(c_2(T_1) - c_1(T_1) \geq c_2(T_1) - 0 \geq 0\). We have just shown that for a European call the theta is non-positive (when rates are non-negative).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: BSM Model  ·  Put-Call Parity   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Theta relationship from put-call parity

Step 1 — Put-call parity (no dividends): \[c - p = S_0 - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]

Step 2 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(t\) (\(\Theta = \partial V/\partial t\)): \[\Theta_c - \Theta_p = \frac{\partial}{\partial t}\bigl(S_0 - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\bigr) = -rKe^{-r(T-t)}\] \[\boxed{\Theta_c - \Theta_p = -rKe^{-r(T-t)}}\]

Step 3 — Comment: With \(r>0\): \(\Theta_c < \Theta_p\) (call loses value more slowly than put with time). Intuitively: holding the call delays paying \(K\), earning interest on \(K\), which partially offsets time decay.

(b) \(c_2 \geq c_1\) when \(T_2 > T_1\) — arbitrage argument

Step 1 — At expiry \(T_1\): the holder of \(c_2\) still holds an option with remaining life \(T_2 - T_1 > 0\). The holder of \(c_1\) receives at most \(\max(S_{T_1}-K, 0)\).

Step 2 — Strategy: Suppose \(c_2 < c_1\). Then at \(t=0\): buy \(c_2\), sell \(c_1\), pocket \(c_1 - c_2 > 0\).

Step 3 — At \(T_1\): if \(c_1\) is exercised against you, you must pay \(S_{T_1} - K\). But your \(c_2\) is worth at least \(\max(S_{T_1} - K, 0)\) (you can exercise it too, or sell it). So you can cover any exercise of \(c_1\) using \(c_2\).

Step 4 — Conclusion: No net cost at \(T_1\), positive profit at \(t=0\) → arbitrage. Therefore: \[\boxed{c_2 \geq c_1 \text{ whenever } T_2 > T_1, r \geq 0}\] Longer maturity ⟹ more time value ⟹ weakly higher price.

Question 3

In the Cox-Ingersoll-Ross (CIR) Model the (instantaneous) short term interest rate \(r\) is described by the process: \[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\sqrt{r}\,dz\] where \(a\), \(b\) and \(\sigma\) are constants (\(\sigma^2 < 2ab\)), and \(dz\) a Brownian increment.
(a) What does this model mean for the behaviour of the short term interest rate?
(b) In this model the solution for the price of a zero-coupon bond can be written \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\] Derive the duration of the bond.
(c) In some models of the short term interest rate \(r\) (e.g. the Hull-White model) the drift rate is made a function of calendar time \(t\). What is the purpose of the extra flexibility compared to the CIR (or Vasicek) type of models?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) It should be noted that the model implies a mean reversion of the spot rate to a level of \(b\) at a rate of \(a\). It should also be explained that the volatility term means that the short term rate cannot go negative. (Hull 9th ed, p.710).
(b) The answer depends on the definition of duration applied. The version in Hull (9th ed., p.712) is \[D = -\frac{\partial P/\partial r}{P} = B(t,T)\]
(c) The time-dependent drift term in the Hull-White model is introduced to be able to incorporate a given, initial term structure, making the values derived from it “arbitrage-free” in relation to the existing securities priced on the current term structure (assuming these to be arbitrage free). This is in contrast to the CIR and Vasicek models of the “equilibrium” or “endogenous”-type that put restrictions on the possible initial term structure (as it is a function of the current spot rate with only few parameters), (Hull 9th ed., section 31.2–31.3).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) CIR model — behaviour of short rate

Model: \(dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\sqrt{r}\,dz\)

Mean reversion to \(b\) at speed \(a\): same as Vasicek.
Key difference: volatility = \(\sigma\sqrt{r}\) — scales with \(\sqrt{r}\).
When \(r\to 0\): volatility \(\to 0\), drift \(= ab > 0\), so rate is pushed positive.
Condition \(\sigma^2 < 2ab\) ensures \(r\) stays strictly positive (Feller condition).

(b) Duration of CIR bond

Bond price: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\)

Step 1 — Derivative w.r.t. r \[\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = -B(t,T)\cdot A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r} = -B(t,T)P\]

Step 2 — Duration \[\boxed{D = -\frac{1}{P}\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = B(t,T)}\]

(c) Hull-White extension of CIR

Replace constant \(ab\) with \(\theta(t)\) to fit the initial term structure exactly. Makes model arbitrage-free w.r.t. current bond prices (fitting/no-arbitrage model vs equilibrium model).

June 2020 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Consider a stock that does not pay dividends during the period under analysis. Let the price be \(S_t\) at time \(t\). At time 0 the stock price is 200, and during the next year the price can go up by 10% or down by 15%. The one year risk free interest rate is 0.
(a) Find the arbitrage free price \(p_0\) at time 0 of a put option with strike 200 and expiry after one year by constructing a risk free portfolio of the stock and the option.
(b) What is the risk neutral probability \(q\) of the up-move in the stock price?
(c) Suppose that the real world probability \(p\) of an up-move is 4/5. Why would you expect \(p > q\)? What is the expected return on the put option? Explain the result.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

The answer can be found using the binomial model:
Stock goes up 10%: \(S_u = 220\), put payoff = 0
Stock goes down 15%: \(S_d = 170\), put payoff = 30
(a) Consider a portfolio of 1 put option and long \(\Delta\) stocks. The payoffs at time 1 will be \(220\Delta\) and \(170\Delta + 30\). This is risk free if \(\Delta\) is chosen to 3/5 and will give a payoff of 132 at time 1. That amount can be borrowed at time 0 giving a cash-flow of 132. Deduct the price of the delta position in the stock of 120 and you have 12 left. This must be the price of the put if there is to be no arbitrage opportunities.
(b) The risk neutral probability \(q\) of the up-move can be found by \(200 = 220q + 170(1-q)\). Thus \(q = 3/5\). That provides another way to calculate the arbitrage free put price, since the discounted expected payoff is \(30 \times (1 - 3/5) = 12\).
(c) The scenario may be rationalised by the following: The real world probability of a state that is “good”, most likely with high stock prices, is higher than the risk neutral probability, since the risk neutral probability will correct for a risk premium penalizing payoffs in “good” states and benefiting payoffs in “bad” states. The expected return of the put option under the real world probability is negative \(((30/5 - 12)/12 = -50\%)\) and lower than the risk free rate (of 0%) since the put can serve as an insurance against the “bad” down-state. Note that the real world expected return of the stock is \((220 \times 4/5 + 170 \times 1/5)/200 - 1 = 5\%\), much higher than the risk free rate, thus indicating a high risk premium.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Binomial Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

Setup: \(S_0=200\), up: \(S_u=220\), down: \(S_d=170\). Strike \(K=200\). \(r=0\).

Put payoffs: \(P_u = \max(200-220,0)=0\), \(P_d = \max(200-170,0)=30\).

(a) Price by risk-free portfolio

Step 1 — Delta hedge: hold \(\Delta\) stocks + long put
Payoffs: \(220\Delta + 0 = 170\Delta + 30\)
\[50\Delta = 30 \implies \Delta = 3/5\]

Step 2 — Riskless payoff at T: \(220\times(3/5) = 132\)

Step 3 — No arbitrage: PV of riskless payoff (r=0) = current cost \[\Delta S_0 + p_0 = 132 \implies (3/5)(200) + p_0 = 132 \implies 120 + p_0 = 132\] \[\boxed{p_0 = 12}\]

(b) Risk-neutral probability

Step 1 — Stock must earn \(r=0\) under Q \[200 = 220q + 170(1-q) = 170 + 50q\] \[50q = 30 \implies \boxed{q = 3/5}\]

Step 2 — Verify put price \[p_0 = e^{-rT}[qP_u + (1-q)P_d] = 1\cdot[(3/5)(0) + (2/5)(30)] = 12\checkmark\]

(c) Real-world vs risk-neutral probabilities

Risk-neutral probability: \(q = 3/5\) (up), \(1-q = 2/5\) (down).
Real-world probability of up-move is higher than 3/5 (investors expect a positive risk premium on stocks).
Expected return of put under real-world \(P\):
If real-world up probability \(p > q = 3/5\), expected put payoff = \(p\cdot 0 + (1-p)\cdot 30 < 12\).
So expected return of the put is negative — below the risk-free rate of 0%.
\[\text{Expected return} = \frac{(1-p)\cdot 30 - 12}{12} < 0\] This makes sense: put is insurance against bad states, so it has a negative risk premium.

Question 2

In a tranched CDS (also a synthetic CDO) compare the most senior, the mezzanine and the most junior (also called “equity”) tranches.
(a) Explain how the tranches are exposed to the risk of defaults from the total underlying portfolio. Explain what is meant by “attachment point” and “detachment point”.
(b) Assume a large set of obligors (or “names”) in the underlying portfolio. If correlation is not perfect which tranche will gain proportionally the most by a lower credit spread on the underlying portfolio?
(c) For a given credit spread on the underlying portfolio of CDS which tranche will benefit from an increase in implied (i.e. derived from market prices) correlation?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The equity tranche can be seen as a leveraged exposure to the total underlying portfolio, but protected by limited liability. The more senior tranches are exposed to the risk on the total underlying portfolio only after more junior tranches has covered losses. The attachment and detachment points of a given tranche denotes the proportions of losses on the underlying portfolio that is covered by the given tranche, e.g. the equity tranche has an attachment point of 0 and a detachment point of e.g. 3%, whereas the most senior tranche has an attachment point of e.g. 22% and a detachment point of 100%.
(b) In general the spread on the junior tranche is higher than on the senior tranche as it has a higher expected loss from defaults in the underlying and a higher risk (the effect of leverage). The price of the most junior tranche will benefit the most (unless continuing to be worthless) from a fall in credit spreads.
(c) An increase in implied correlation (for a given spread) will benefit the equity tranche and hurt the most senior tranche. To see this compare the extremes where defaults are perfectly correlated to where they are independent: First note that the underlying portfolio and thus the sum of tranche values stay fixed (as the spread is the same). If defaults are perfectly correlated the (risk neutral) expected loss reflected in the given spread will take the form of no losses or of losses that will hit the equity tranche but likely also spill into the more senior tranches. If defaults are independent through the diversification (of the large set of underlying obligors) the losses will be close to the expected loss reflected in the given spread, hitting the equity tranche and maybe also spill into more senior tranches. So the price of the equity tranche will increase when the implied correlation increases. The price of the most senior tranche will fall (or stay the same if the subordination is so large that it will not suffer losses in the perfect correlation case). The change of the price of mezzanine trances cannot be established without further assumptions.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) CDO tranches — exposure, attachment and detachment points

Step 1 — Loss waterfall
Portfolio losses are absorbed from bottom up:
Equity tranche (0–A%): first losses, highest risk, highest spread.
Mezzanine tranche (A%–D%): intermediate losses, medium risk.
Senior tranche (D%–100%): last to be hit, lowest risk, lowest spread.

Step 2 — Define attachment/detachment
Attachment point: portfolio loss % at which this tranche starts absorbing.
Detachment point: loss % at which this tranche is fully wiped out. \[\boxed{\text{Equity: 0 to }A\%.\quad\text{Senior: }D\%\text{ to }100\%.}\]

(b) Effect of correlation — which tranche gains?

Step 1 — Low correlation: defaults spread out → equity absorbs predictable losses, senior very safe → low senior spread, high equity spread.

Step 2 — High correlation: either all or none default → senior also at risk → senior spread rises, equity spread falls.

Step 3 — For given average spread: total portfolio value fixed. Increasing correlation transfers value from senior to equity: \[\boxed{\uparrow\rho: \text{ equity value}\uparrow,\; \text{senior value}\downarrow}\]

(c) Correlation and tranche values — detailed reasoning

Step 1: With low \(\rho\), losses cluster near the expected loss → equity always covers → senior almost risk-free.

Step 2: With high \(\rho\) (all default or none), equity sometimes unscathed (no default) but sometimes wiped out AND losses spill into senior.

Question 3

Consider an economy with a (continuously compounded) risk free interest rate of \(r\) and a stock with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\). Suppose that the stock pays a deterministic continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\).
(a) What is the forward price at time \(t\) on the stock for maturity \(T > t\)?
(b) Suppose that a forward contract on the stock with forward price \(K\) exists. What will the value \(V(S_t, t; K)\) of that contract be at time \(t\)?
(c) The Black-Scholes-Merton partial differential equation for the price \(V\) of some general derivative is \[\frac{\partial V}{\partial t} + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial S^2} + (r-\delta)S\frac{\partial V}{\partial S} - rV = 0\] Show that the value of the forward contract satisfies this equation.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The value can be found taking the risk neutral expectation of the payoff at \(T\) from a strategy where dividends are continuously reinvested in the stock and discounting with the risk free rate: To achieve a value of \(S_T\) at \(T\) invest \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) in the stock at time \(t\) and reinvest dividends (the value will grow proportional to \(dS_t + \delta\,dt\)). The forward price will be a value \(F_t\) that satisfies: \[0 = e^{-r(T-t)}E^{\mathbb{Q}}_t[S_T - F_t]\] \[e^{-r(T-t)}F_t = e^{-r(T-t)}E^{\mathbb{Q}}_t[S_T] = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\] \[F_t = S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\]
(b) The value of a forward contract with a given forward price \(K\) will be \[V(S_t,t;K) = e^{-r(T-t)}E^{\mathbb{Q}}_t[S_T - K] = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]
(c) Taking partials it can be shown that this satisfies the BSM PDE.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing  ·  BSM Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward price with continuous dividend \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Buy \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares at \(t\), reinvest dividends. At \(T\): 1 share. Cost at \(t\): \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\).
Step 2 — Finance by borrowing, repay \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\cdot e^{r(T-t)}\) at \(T\) = forward price: \[\boxed{F(t,T) = S_t\,e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}}\]

(b) Value of forward contract with price \(K\)

Step 1 — Value = PV of (receive \(S_T\), pay \(K\)): \[V = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q[S_T - K] = e^{-r(T-t)}(F_t - K)\] Since \(F_t = S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\): \[\boxed{V(S_t,t;K) = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}}\]

(c) BSM PDE with dividends — full derivation

Step 1 — Delta-hedge: Short 1 derivative \(V\), long \(\Delta = V_S\) shares. Shares pay continuous dividend \(\delta\), so holding \(\Delta\) shares earns \(\delta S \Delta\,dt\) dividend income. \[\Pi = -V + V_S\cdot S\]

Step 2 — Change in portfolio including dividends: \[d\Pi = -dV + V_S\,dS + V_S\cdot\delta S\,dt \quad \text{(dividend income on the stock holding)}\] Apply Itô's lemma to \(V(S,t)\) with \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\): \[dV = \bigl(\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt + \sigma S V_S\,dz\] Substitute: \[d\Pi = -\bigl(\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt - \sigma S V_S\,dz + V_S(\mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz) + V_S\delta S\,dt\] \[= -\bigl(\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt + V_S\delta S\,dt \quad \text{(}\mu \text{ terms cancel, }\sigma\text{ terms cancel)}\]

Step 3 — Riskless portfolio earns \(r\): \(d\Pi = r\Pi\,dt\) \[-\bigl(\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr) + V_S\delta S = r(-V + V_S S)\] Rearrange: \[\boxed{\Theta + (r-\delta)SV_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} = rV}\] The only difference from standard BSM: \((r-\delta)\) instead of \(r\) in the \(SV_S\) term, because the stock's risk-neutral drift is \(r-\delta\).

August 2020 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 4 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted · Question 1 weighted 40%; Questions 2–4 weighted 20% each

Question 1

Consider an economy where the value of a bank account \(B_t\) at time \(t\) and the price of a non-dividend paying stock \(S_t\) at time \(t\) are described by the Itô-processes: \[dB_t = rB_t\,dt,\quad B_0 = 1\] \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz,\quad S_0 > 0\] where \(\mu\) is constant, \(\sigma\) is a positive constant, \(r\) is a constant risk free rate, and \(dz\) is the standard short hand notation for a Brownian increment.
(a) Why can we use \(S_t\) as a numeraire?
(b) Consider the process \(B_t/S_t\) and express this as an Itô process.
(c) What \(\mu\) will make this a martingale?
(d) What will the drift rate of \(\ln S_t\) be under this numeraire?
(e) A derivative pays \(S_T G(S_T)\) at time \(T > t\), where \(G\) is some function. With \(E^S\) denoting the expectation under the probability measure corresponding to \(S_t\) as a numeraire find an expression for the price \(f_t\) of this derivative at time \(t\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) \(S_t\) can be used as a numeraire because it is the price of a traded asset with a positive price.
(b) The process can be found (directly using \(d(X/Y) = (X/Y)(dX/X - dY/Y - dX\,dY/X\,Y + (dY/Y)^2)\) or as here following section 27.3 of Hull (8th ed)) by first considering the process \(d\ln B_t - d\ln S_t\). By Itô's lemma this is \[(r - \mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz\] Again using Itô's lemma (on the transformation exp) you get \[d\frac{B_t}{S_t} = (r - \mu + \sigma^2)\frac{B_t}{S_t}\,dt + \sigma\frac{B_t}{S_t}\,dz\]
(c) This is a martingale if \(\mu = r + \sigma^2\).
(d) You already found the Itô-process \(d\ln S_t\). With this value of \(\mu\) the drift is \(r + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\) (whereas it is \(r - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\) under the traditional risk neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\) where \(B_t\) is numeraire).
(e) The value \(f_t\) can be found using the fact that \(f_t/S_t\) is a martingale under this measure, thus \[f_t/S_t = E^S_t[f_T/S_T]\] and \[f_t = S_t E^S_t[S_T G(S_T)/S_T] = S_t E^S_t[G(S_T)]\]

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Numeraire & Change of Measure   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Why \(S_t\) can be a numeraire

\(S_t\) is the price of a traded asset with strictly positive price (since \(S_0 > 0\) and GBM keeps \(S_t > 0\) a.s.). Any positive traded asset can serve as numeraire.

(b) Process for \(B_t/S_t\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Compute \(d\ln(B_t/S_t) = d\ln B_t - d\ln S_t\): \[d\ln B_t = r\,dt \quad \text{(since }dB_t = rB_t\,dt\text{, no stochastic term)}\] \[d\ln S_t = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz \quad \text{(Itô on }\ln S\text{)}\] \[d\ln\frac{B_t}{S_t} = \bigl(r - \mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt - \sigma\,dz\]

Step 2 — Exponentiate: apply Itô to \(B/S = e^{\ln(B/S)}\).
Let \(X = \ln(B/S)\), \(dX = a\,dt + b\,dz\) where \(a = r - \mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\), \(b = -\sigma\).
Itô on \(e^X\): \[d\frac{B_t}{S_t} = \frac{B_t}{S_t}\bigl(dX + \tfrac{1}{2}(dX)^2\bigr) = \frac{B_t}{S_t}\bigl[(a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2)dt + b\,dz\bigr]\] Compute \(a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2 = r - \mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 = r - \mu + \sigma^2\): \[\boxed{d\frac{B_t}{S_t} = (r - \mu + \sigma^2)\frac{B_t}{S_t}\,dt - \sigma\frac{B_t}{S_t}\,dz}\]

(c) Martingale condition: \(\mu = r + \sigma^2\)

\(B_t/S_t\) is a martingale iff drift = 0: \[r - \mu + \sigma^2 = 0 \implies \boxed{\mu = r + \sigma^2}\]

(d) Process for \(\ln S_t\) when \(\mu = r + \sigma^2\)

\[d\ln S_t = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz = \bigl(r + \sigma^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz = \bigl(r + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\] Compare to standard Q-measure: \(d\ln S = (r-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz^Q\). The drift is higher under the S-measure by \(\sigma^2\) — this is the Girsanov correction when changing from \(Q\) to the S-numeraire measure.

Question 2

Let \(y(0,T)\) be the continuously compounded yields at time \(t=0\) on zero coupon bonds with maturities \(T > 0\), i.e. with prices \(P(0,T) = e^{-y(0,T)T}\).
(a) Find the instantaneous forward rate \(f(0,T)\) as contracted at 0 for maturity \(T\) (i.e. for a period \((T, T+dT)\)).
(b) Show that if \(y\) is increasing in \(T\), then \(f(0,T) > y(0,T)\) and interpret this result.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Using the definition of the instantaneous forward rate you calculate \[f(0,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(0,T)}{\partial T} = -\frac{\partial(-y(0,T)T)}{\partial T} = T\frac{\partial y(0,T)}{\partial T} + y(0,T)\]
(b) The result follows from the assumption that \(\partial y/\partial T > 0\). An interpretation is that as long as the forward rate curve is above the yield curve it pulls the yield curve (that averages for the period from 0 to \(T\)) up at the margin.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: HJM Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Instantaneous forward rate from yields

Step 1 — Bond price definition: \(P(0,T) = e^{-y(0,T)T}\)

Step 2 — Forward rate definition \[f(0,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(0,T)}{\partial T}\]

Step 3 — Compute \[f(0,T) = -\frac{\partial(-y(0,T)T)}{\partial T} = y(0,T) + T\frac{\partial y(0,T)}{\partial T}\] \[\boxed{f(0,T) = y(0,T) + T\frac{\partial y}{\partial T}}\]

(b) \(f(0,T) > y(0,T)\) when yield curve slopes up

Step 1 — From (a): \(f - y = T\,\partial y/\partial T\)

Step 2 — If \(\partial y/\partial T > 0\) (upward sloping): \(f - y > 0\), i.e. \(f > y\).

Interpretation: the forward rate is the marginal rate at the margin \(T\). If it exceeds the average yield (over 0 to T), it pulls the yield curve up — standard "averages are below marginals when marginals are rising" argument.

Question 3

Consider a one-period coupon bond that promises to pay its principal (of 1) and the accrued interest from a (continuously compounded) coupon of \(c\) at time \(t=1\). Assume that there is a risk free (continuously compounded) interest rate of \(r\). Suppose that the bond will default with \(\mathbb{Q}\)-probability \(q\) with a known fractional recovery of \(R\), \(0 < R < 1\) (of principal and accrued interest). Use a one-period binomial model to find the coupon \(c\) that will give the defaultable bond a par price at time \(t=0\). What is the credit spread?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

Since the value of the coupon bond can be found by using the expectation under the risk neutral measure and discounting by the risk free interest rate, \(c\) must fulfil: \[1 = e^{-r}\bigl[qRe^c + (1-q)e^c\bigr]\] with the par value on the left hand side and the present value of the expected cash flow on the right (using risk free rate for discounting and the risk neutral probability for the expectation). Solving this for \(c\) yields \[c = r - \ln(1 - q(1-R))\] Thus the credit spread is \[c - r = -\ln(1 - q(1-R)) \approx q(1-R)\] where we for the approximation use that \(\ln(1-x) \approx -x\) for small \(x\). Thus the credit spread is approximately the expected loss under the \(\mathbb{Q}\)-probability.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

Credit bond pricing in one-period binomial model

Setup: Bond promises to pay principal 1 + coupon \(e^c - 1\) at \(t=1\).
Default with Q-probability \(q\), recovery \(R\) of principal + interest.

Step 1 — No-default payoff: \(e^c\) (principal + interest).
Default payoff: \(Re^c\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: bond price today = 1 (par) \[1 = e^{-r}\bigl[q\cdot Re^c + (1-q)\cdot e^c\bigr] = e^{-r}e^c\bigl[1 - q(1-R)\bigr]\]

Step 3 — Solve for \(c\) \[e^r = e^c[1 - q(1-R)]\] \[c = r - \ln\bigl(1 - q(1-R)\bigr)\] \[\boxed{c = r - \ln(1-q(1-R))}\]

Credit spread: \(c - r = -\ln(1-q(1-R)) \approx q(1-R)\) for small \(q\).
Credit spread ≈ risk-neutral expected loss.

Question 4

Consider a European call option on a stock with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\). Assume that the stock pays no dividends before the expiry of the option.
(a) Define the delta \(\Delta\) of the call.
(b) For an at-the-money call (where the strike \(K\) of the option is equal to \(S_t\)) the delta is close to 1/2. Use the Black-Scholes-Merton call option formula to find the strike \(K\) where the delta is precisely 1/2. For your reference the call option formula can be written \[c = S_t\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)\] where \[d_1 = \frac{\ln\frac{S_t}{K} + (r + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t)}{\sigma\sqrt{T-t}}, \quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T-t}\] where \(r\) is a constant continuously compounded interest rate, \(T\) is the expiry date of the option and \(\sigma\) the constant volatility. \(\Phi(x)\) denotes the standard normal distribution function in \(x\).
(c) Assume \(S_t = 1\) and find this strike for parameters \((T-t) = 0.25\), the volatility \(\sigma = 0.4\) and the risk free interest rate \(r = 0\).
(d) What is the delta of the corresponding put option?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The delta is the partial derivative of the option price with respect to the price of the underlying stock.
(b) The delta for a call in the BSM-model is \(\Phi(d_1)\). For this to be 1/2 \(d_1\) must be 0. This is when \[\ln\frac{S_t}{K} + \left(r + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2\right)(T-t) = 0\] or \[K = e^{(r + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t)}S_t\]
(c) For the numerical example you get \[K = e^{\frac{1}{2}(0.4)^2 \cdot 0.25} = e^{0.02} \approx 1.02\] i.e. 2% above the price of the stock.
(d) The delta of the put is the delta of the call minus 1 (which can be shown by the put-call-parity).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: BSM Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Delta of a call option

\[\boxed{\Delta = \frac{\partial c}{\partial S_t}}\] Measures how much the option price changes per unit change in stock price. Used to hedge option positions.

(b) Strike \(K\) where \(\Delta = 1/2\)

Step 1 — BSM delta for a call: \(\Delta = \Phi(d_1)\)

Step 2 — Set \(\Phi(d_1) = 1/2 \implies d_1 = 0\) \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_t/K) + (r + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t)}{\sigma\sqrt{T-t}} = 0\]

Step 3 — Solve for \(K\): \[\ln(S_t/K) = -(r + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t)\] \[\boxed{K = S_t\,e^{(r+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t)}}\] The option is slightly out-of-the-money when \(\Delta = 1/2\) (not exactly at-the-money when \(r > 0\)).

(c) Numerical example

Given \(S_t = 1\), \(\sigma = 0.4\), \(T-t = 0.25\), \(r = 0\): \[K = S_t\,e^{(0+\frac{1}{2}(0.4)^2)(0.25)} = e^{\frac{1}{2}(0.16)(0.25)} = e^{0.02} \approx 1.02\] \[\boxed{K \approx 1.02}\] So the strike where \(\Delta = 1/2\) is about 2% above the current stock price.

(d) Delta of the corresponding put

From put-call parity: \(c - p = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\). Differentiate w.r.t. \(S_t\): \[\Delta_c - \Delta_p = 1 \implies \boxed{\Delta_p = \Delta_c - 1 = \Phi(d_1) - 1 = -\Phi(-d_1)}\] For the at-\(\Delta\)-half strike above: \(\Delta_p = 1/2 - 1 = -1/2\).

June 2019 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

In Vasicek's model, the risk-neutral process for the short rate \(r\) is \[dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] where \(a, b, \sigma\) are constants. Suppose \(a > 0\).
(a) Describe the qualitative characteristics of this model of the short rate.
(b) Consider a zero-coupon bond that pays $1 at time \(T\). Vasicek shows that its price at time \(t\), denoted \(P(t,T)\), can be expressed \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\] using functions \(A\) and \(B\) that he finds (which we don't need in detail right here). Show that \[\frac{\partial P(t,T)}{\partial r(t)} = -B(t,T)P(t,T)\]
(c) Argue that the process for \(P(t,T)\) in the traditional risk-neutral world satisfies \[dP(t,T) = r(t)P(t,T)\,dt - \sigma B(t,T)P(t,T)\,dz(t)\]

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Cf. Hull p. 682–684. The model incorporates mean reversion, as the short rate is pulled to level \(b\) at rate \(a\). Page 684 explains why mean reversion is desirable for a model of the short rate. Superimposed upon this mean reversion is a normally distributed stochastic term \(\sigma\,dz\).
(b) The first expression appears on page 685. The derivative of \(P(t,T)\) with respect to \(r(t)\) becomes \[\frac{\partial P(t,T)}{\partial r(t)} = A(t,T)\bigl(-B(t,T)\bigr)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)} = -B(t,T)P(t,T)\] This is also (30.9).
(c) Cf. Hull p. 687. The expected growth rate of \(P(t,T)\) in the traditional risk-neutral world is \(r(t)\) because \(P(t,T)\) is the price of a traded security. The coefficient of \(dz(t)\) in the process for \(P(t,T)\) can be calculated from Itô's lemma as \(\sigma\,\partial P(t,T)/\partial r(t)\). The claimed process for \(P(t,T)\) follows from these considerations, using the answer from (b).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Vasicek model — qualitative characteristics

Step 1 — Model: \(dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\)

Step 2 — Mean reversion: When \(r>b\): drift \(<0\) → pulled down. When \(r < b\): drift \(> 0\) → pulled up. Speed \(a\), long-run level \(b\).

Step 3 — Stochastic term: Constant volatility \(\sigma\), normally distributed → can go negative. \[\boxed{dr = a(b-r)\,dt + \sigma\,dz}\]

(b) Show \(\partial P/\partial r = -B(t,T)P\)

Step 1 — Affine form: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\)

Step 2 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(r\): \[\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = A(t,T)\cdot\bigl(-B(t,T)\bigr)\cdot e^{-B(t,T)r} = -B(t,T)P\] \[\boxed{\frac{\partial P(t,T)}{\partial r(t)} = -B(t,T)P(t,T)}\]

(c) Show \(dP = r(t)P\,dt - \sigma B(t,T)P\,dz\) under Q

Step 1 — Apply Itô's lemma to \(P(r,t)\) using \(dr = a(b-r)dt + \sigma dz\) (under Q): \[dP = \frac{\partial P}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial P}{\partial r}dr + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 P}{\partial r^2}(dr)^2\]

Step 2 — Under Q, bond must earn the risk-free rate \(r\): \[\text{drift of }dP = r\cdot P\] The diffusion term uses \(\partial P/\partial r = -B(t,T)P\) from (b) and \(\sigma\,dz\) from the short rate: \[\text{diffusion of }dP = \frac{\partial P}{\partial r}\cdot\sigma\,dz = -B(t,T)P\cdot\sigma\,dz\]

Step 3 — Combine: \[\boxed{dP(t,T) = r(t)P(t,T)\,dt - \sigma B(t,T)P(t,T)\,dz}\] Interpretation: bond earns the risk-free rate \(r\) in drift, with interest rate risk proportional to \(B(t,T)\) (duration).

Question 2

Suppose \(f\) and \(g\) are the prices of two non-dividend paying securities with volatilities \(\sigma_f\) and \(\sigma_g\), depending on a single source of risk captured by the Wiener process \(z\). Suppose that the market price of risk is \(\sigma_g\). Let \(r\) denote the risk-free rate.
(a) Argue that \(f\) and \(g\) satisfy \[df = (r + \sigma_g\sigma_f)f\,dt + \sigma_f f\,dz \quad\text{and}\quad dg = \bigl(r + \sigma_g^2\bigr)g\,dt + \sigma_g g\,dz\]
(b) Apply Itô's lemma to find \(d\ln f\) and \(d\ln g\). Recall that \(\ln\tfrac{f}{g} = \ln f - \ln g\). Show that \[d\ln\frac{f}{g} = -\frac{(\sigma_f - \sigma_g)^2}{2}\,dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_g)\,dz\]
(c) Using the equation for \(\ln\tfrac{f}{g}\) from (b), apply Itô's lemma to determine the equation for \(f/g\). Show that \(f/g\) has zero drift, and comment on the interpretation of this result.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) From Hull 27.1, when \(\lambda\) is the market price of the single source of risk, security price \(f\) obeys \[df = (r + \lambda\sigma_f)f\,dt + \sigma_f f\,dz\] Once \(\lambda = \sigma_g\), the two expressions follow.
(b) Cf. Hull p. 636. First, Itô's lemma gives \[d\ln f = \left(r + \sigma_g\sigma_f - \frac{\sigma_f^2}{2}\right)dt + \sigma_f\,dz\] and, likewise, \[d\ln g = \left(r + \frac{\sigma_g^2}{2}\right)dt + \sigma_g\,dz\] Then \[d\ln f - d\ln g = \left(\sigma_g\sigma_f - \frac{\sigma_f^2}{2} - \frac{\sigma_g^2}{2}\right)dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_g)\,dz\] which is identical to the desired expression.
(c) If we let \(x = \ln\tfrac{f}{g}\), we're seeking to find the equation for \(G(x) = e^x = \tfrac{f}{g}\). Itô's lemma gives \[d\frac{f}{g} = (\sigma_f - \sigma_g)\frac{f}{g}\,dz\] which indeed has zero drift. The interpretation is that the relative security price \(\tfrac{f}{g}\) is a martingale. As explained on the top of p. 636, this is the equivalent martingale measure result.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Market Price of Risk  ·  Numeraire & Change of Measure   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) \(f\) and \(g\) satisfy the given processes when \(\lambda = \sigma_g\)

Market price of risk: \(\lambda = (\mu-r)/\sigma\) for all non-dividend-paying traded assets.
For \(f\): \(\mu_f = r + \lambda\sigma_f = r + \sigma_g\sigma_f\). \(\checkmark\)
For \(g\): \(\mu_g = r + \lambda\sigma_g = r + \sigma_g^2\). \(\checkmark\)

(b) \(d\ln(f/g)\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Apply Itô to \(\ln f\) and \(\ln g\): \[d\ln f = \bigl(\mu_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_f\,dz = \bigl(r + \sigma_g\sigma_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_f\,dz\] \[d\ln g = \bigl(\mu_g - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_g^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_g\,dz = \bigl(r + \sigma_g^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_g^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_g\,dz = \bigl(r + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_g^2\bigr)dt + \sigma_g\,dz\]

Step 2 — Subtract: \[d\ln\frac{f}{g} = \bigl(\sigma_g\sigma_f - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_f^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_g^2\bigr)dt + (\sigma_f - \sigma_g)\,dz = -\tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2 dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\,dz\] \[\boxed{d\ln\frac{f}{g} = -\tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2 dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)dz}\]

(c) \(f/g\) is a martingale — change of numeraire

Step 1 — Exponentiate: apply Itô to \(f/g = e^{\ln(f/g)}\).
Let \(X = \ln(f/g)\), \(dX = a\,dt + b\,dz\) where \(a = -\tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2\), \(b = \sigma_f - \sigma_g\).
Itô on \(e^X\): \[d\frac{f}{g} = \frac{f}{g}\bigl[(a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2)dt + b\,dz\bigr]\] Compute drift coefficient: \[a + \tfrac{1}{2}b^2 = -\tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2 + \tfrac{1}{2}(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2 = 0\] The drift terms cancel exactly: \[\boxed{d\frac{f}{g} = (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\frac{f}{g}\,dz}\] Zero drift ⟹ \(f/g\) is a martingale. This is the change-of-numeraire result: when \(g\) is the numeraire, the ratio \(f/g\) is driftless — equivalent to saying \(f\) is a martingale under the \(g\)-measure.

Question 3

Consider a futures contract on a non-dividend paying stock with a time to maturity of \(T\). Let the current (\(t=0\)) price of the stock be \(S_0\), and assume that the risk-free rate of interest is a constant \(r\).
(a) The futures price \(F_0\) at \(t=0\) satisfies \(F_0 = S_0 e^{rT}\). What is the delta of the futures contract?
(b) Compare the price of a European call option on the stock with the price of a European call option on the futures contract when the two options have the same strikes and maturities, and they mature at the same time as the futures contract.
(c) Explain the following in words (no mathematical derivation is expected): in a risk-neutral world, the futures price behaves in the same way as the price of a stock that pays dividend yield \(r\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Cf. Hull p. 399. The delta is \[\frac{\partial F_0}{\partial S_0} = e^{rT}\]
(b) Following Hull 17.3, towards maturity when \(T\) is close to zero, the futures price approximates the stock price. These two options therefore have identical payoffs at expiry. Being European, then they have identical values also before expiry.
(c) Cf. Hull 17.7 for full explanations. Entering into a long futures contract has value zero. Therefore, under risk-neutral valuation, \(F_0 = \hat{E}[F_T]\) for any time \(T\). The exact same martingale property, \(S_0 = \hat{E}[S_T]\), holds for a stock that pays dividend at rate \(r\). Its drift is \(r - r = 0\).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Delta of a futures contract

Step 1 — Futures price formula: \(F_0 = S_0 e^{rT}\)

Step 2 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(S_0\) \[\boxed{\Delta_\text{futures} = \frac{\partial F_0}{\partial S_0} = e^{rT} > 1}\] Delta exceeds 1 because futures embeds the cost-of-carry \(e^{rT}\).

(b) European call on stock vs call on futures — same K, T

Step 1 — At expiry \(T\): futures price converges to spot: \(F_T^T = S_T\).
Call on futures payoff: \(\max(F_T^T - K, 0) = \max(S_T - K, 0)\) = same as call on stock.

Step 2 — Both European → same PV: \[c^\text{stock} = e^{-rT}E^Q[\max(S_T-K,0)] = c^\text{futures}\] \[\boxed{c^\text{futures} = c^\text{stock}}\text{ for non-dividend-paying stock}\]

(c) Futures price as a Q-martingale

Step 1 — Entering a futures contract has zero value (marked to market daily, no upfront payment).
Under risk-neutral valuation: \(F_0 = E^Q[F_T]\) for any \(T\).
\(\Rightarrow\) Futures price is a Q-martingale.

Step 2 — Equivalence to dividend-paying stock
A stock paying dividends at rate \(r\) has drift \(r-r=0\) under Q → also a Q-martingale.
\[\boxed{F_0 = E^Q[F_T] = E^Q[S_T] = S_0 e^{rT}}\]

August 2019 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Let the price of a traded financial instrument, \(S\), be modelled (under the probability measure \(\mathbb{P}\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\] where \(\mu\) and \(\sigma > 0\) are constants, and \(z\) is a Brownian motion.
(a) Describe the qualitative characteristics of this model, and discuss its possible shortcomings as a model of a stock price.
(b) Assume a constant risk free rate of \(r\), and that the instrument pays a continuous dividend stream of \(\delta\) proportional to the price \(S\). What will the drift rate of the price be under the standard risk neutral probability measure (\(\mathbb{Q}\)) and a no-arbitrage assumption?
(c) Consider a derivative on \(S\) with value \(V\) equal to \(S^n\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(V\). Is the volatility of \(V\) higher, if \(n\) is higher?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The issue that the model assumes no dividends aside (it is dealt with below), the discussion should cover: The model implies continuous sample paths whereas stock prices can make large instantaneous changes (and in any case prices are only quoted in certain tick sizes). The model is Markov (which can be seen as a form of weak informational efficiency, cf. Hull (8ed, section 13.1–2)). The model implies constant volatility, where we in practice observe marked (and sticky) changes in volatility. The model implies normally distributed returns (whereas empirical stylized facts point to negative skewness and some excess kurtosis — not really discussed in Hull).
(b) The drift rate under \(\mathbb{Q}\) should be \(r - \delta\) so as to make the total return of holding the stock equal to the risk free interest rate (cf. Hull (8ed, section 16.3)).
(c) An application of Itô's lemma gives \[dV = \left(\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 n(n-1)S^{n-2} + \mu S^n S^{n-1}\right)dt + nS^{n-1}\sigma S\,dz\] \[= \left(n\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\right)V\,dt + n\sigma V\,dz\] So this is also of a geometric Brownian form. And it can be seen that volatility is increasing in \(n\). This is Practice Question 13.10 in Hull (8ed); note that it is not the same as the value of a derivative that pays off \(S_T^n\) at time \(T\), cf. Practice Question 14.12 in Hull (8ed).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM  ·  Market Price of Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) GBM — qualitative characteristics and shortcomings

Characteristics: Continuous paths. Constant drift \(\mu\) and volatility \(\sigma\). Lognormal prices, normal returns. Markov (only current price matters).

Shortcomings:
1. Real prices can jump (discontinuous paths).
2. Constant volatility — empirical evidence shows clustering and smile/skew.
3. Normal returns — fat tails, negative skewness in practice.
4. Markov: some evidence of long memory in financial data.

(b) Drift under Q with continuous dividend \(\delta\)

Under Q, holding the stock earns total return \(r\): capital gain + dividend = \(r\).
Capital gain drift = \(r - \delta\): \[\boxed{dS = (r-\delta)S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz^Q}\]

(c) Change of measure — Girsanov's theorem

Step 1 — Under P: \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz^P\)

Step 2 — Under Q: \(dS = (r-\delta)S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz^Q\)

Step 3 — Relate the Brownian motions
Subtract: \(\mu - (r-\delta) = \sigma(\theta)\) where \(\theta\) = market price of risk. \[dz^Q = dz^P + \theta\,dt,\qquad \theta = \frac{\mu - (r-\delta)}{\sigma}\] Under Q: \(dz^Q\) is a standard Brownian motion (Girsanov's theorem).

Question 2

The HJM-model describes the simultaneous evolution of the term structure of interest rates. Let the evolution of instantaneous forward rates contracted at \(t\) for time \(T\) be described by the Itô-process \[df(t,T) = m(t,T,\Omega)\,dt + s(t,T,\Omega)\,dz\] where \(\Omega\) is a set of state variables.
(a) Under certain conditions we have the following no-arbitrage condition for the drift term: \[m(t,T,\Omega) = s(t,T,\Omega)\int_t^T s(t,\tau,\Omega)\,d\tau\] Comment on this result, and in particular explain under which probability measure it is derived.
(b) The short rate in this model is in general non-Markov. Explain what this means, and why it is a complication for implementation.
(c) As a special case let \(s(t,T,\Omega)\) be a constant \(s\). Derive the process followed by forward rates. Comment on the distribution of the forward rates.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) What should be commented is that the volatility barring arbitrage determines the drift rate, and that the result presented is derived under the traditional risk neutral probability measure \(\mathbb{Q}\), cf. Hull (8th ed., pp. 716–7).
(b) As given in the lecture notes, a probability space \((\Omega,\mathcal{F},P)\) and a process \(X_t : \Omega \mapsto \mathbb{R}^n\) is Markov if \[P(X_{t_n} \leq x_n \mid X_{t_{n-1}} = x_{n-1}, \ldots, X_{t_1} = x_1) = P(X_{t_n} \leq x_n \mid X_{t_{n-1}} = x_{n-1}), \quad \forall n,\; \forall t_1 < \cdots < t_n\] Hull has a less formal definition in section 13.1 (of the 8th edition). I.e. if the short rate is not Markov we cannot in general model the evolution of rates without including the full history of rates. In practice this means that we cannot e.g. use a recombining interest rate tree, which adds a substantial computational burden if more than a few time steps are to be simulated, cf. Hull (8th ed., section 31.1).
(c) By integration we find for this simple model that \[df(t,T) = s^2(T-t)\,dt + s\,dz\] (this is not derived in the syllabus). This means that the changes to the forward rates, and thus the forward rates, will be normally distributed, which in particular means a positive probability for negative interest rates. It can be noted — but this is not directly in the syllabus (it is in Hull (8th ed., Practice Question 31.3)) — that this special case is equivalent to the Ho-Lee model.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: HJM Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) HJM drift condition

HJM models the evolution of instantaneous forward rates \(f(t,T)\): \[df(t,T) = m(t,T)\,dt + s(t,T)\,dz\] No-arbitrage requires: \[\boxed{m(t,T) = s(t,T)\int_t^T s(t,\tau)\,d\tau}\] Interpretation: the drift of forward rates is completely determined by their volatility structure. Volatility drives both risk and drift — no free parameters for drift once volatility is specified.

(b) HJM and non-Markov property

In general, HJM models are non-Markov: the current forward rate curve \(\{f(t,T)\}_{T\geq t}\) is needed (not just the short rate \(r(t)\)) to evolve the term structure.
Special case: if \(s(t,T) = \sigma e^{-a(T-t)}\) (exponentially decaying volatility), the model reduces to the Markov Vasicek/Hull-White model.
The LMM is a discrete-tenor version of HJM.

(c) Ho-Lee as special case of HJM

In the Ho-Lee model: \(s(t,T) = \sigma\) (constant volatility, independent of \(T\)).

Step 1 — Apply HJM drift condition: \[m(t,T) = \sigma\int_t^T\sigma\,d\tau = \sigma^2(T-t)\]

Step 2 — Forward rate process: \[\boxed{df(t,T) = \sigma^2(T-t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz}\] Forward rates are normally distributed. Equivalent to Ho-Lee short rate model \(dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\).

Question 3

(a) Consider a derivative with value \(V(S,t)\) as some function of the current stock price \(S\) and time \(t\) (and further implicit parameters). Define the Delta, Gamma and Theta of the derivative. What can they be used for?
(b) Assume that the stock pays no dividends before time \(T\), and that there is a constant risk free interest rate of \(r\). Let \(c(S,K,T,r)\) and \(p(S,K,T,r)\) be the price at time \(t=0\) of a European call and a European put, respectively, on the stock with the same strike \(K\) and expiry \(T\). Derive the call-put-parity.
(c) Use the call-put-parity to find a relationship between the Deltas of the call and put. Repeat this for Gamma and Theta, respectively.
(d) Suppose a portfolio of the stock and/or derivatives of that stock is Delta-neutral, and that there are no arbitrage possibilities. Let the value of the portfolio be \(\Pi(S,t)\). Use the Black-Scholes-Merton PDE to characterise the relation between the Theta and Gamma of the portfolio.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The Delta and Theta of the derivative is the partial derivative of the value \(V\) with respect to the underlying stock price and the calendar time parameter \(t\), respectively. Gamma is the second order derivative of the value with respect to the stock price (Cf. Hull (8ed) p.380ff). The Greeks above can be used to measure economic risks of a portfolio including options, and to manage and possibly hedge part of the risks.
(b) By considering the payoff at maturity we see that a portfolio with long European call and a short European put with strikes \(K\) and expiries \(T\) has the same payoff as a forward on the stock with forward price \(K\) and maturity \(T\), thus also (under a no-arbitrage assumption) \[c(S_0,K,T,r) - p(S_0,K,T,r) = S_0 - P(0,T)K\] where here \(P(0,T) = \exp(-rT)\).
(c) By considering the call-put-parity you immediately get for the Deltas \[\Delta_c = 1 + \Delta_p\] and using this for the gammas \[\Gamma_c = \Gamma_p\] For Theta we get \[\Theta_c + rKe^{-rT} - \Theta_p = 0\] (using that the derivative with respect to calendar time is minus the derivative with respect to the expiry date).
(d) For a portfolio with value \(\Pi(S,t)\) dependent on a non-dividend-paying stock we have by no arbitrage the Black-Scholes-Merton PDE: \[\frac{\partial\Pi}{\partial t} + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\frac{\partial^2\Pi}{\partial S^2} + rS\frac{\partial\Pi}{\partial S} - r\Pi = 0\] or \[\Theta + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma + rS\Delta = r\Pi\] When delta is zero you have \[\Theta + \frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = r\Pi\] One interpretation from this is that a delta-neutral, positive-gamma portfolio barring arbitrage will have (for small \(r\) or small \(\Pi\)) a negative theta (i.e. time-decay), cf. Hull (8ed, section 18.7).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: BSM Model  ·  Put-Call Parity   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Delta, Gamma, Theta

\(\Delta = \partial V/\partial S\): price change per unit stock move. Used for delta-hedging.
\(\Gamma = \partial^2 V/\partial S^2\): rate of change of \(\Delta\). Measures convexity; determines hedge rebalancing frequency.
\(\Theta = \partial V/\partial t\): time decay. Usually negative for long option positions.

(b) Put-call parity and Greeks

Step 1 — Put-call parity (no dividends): \[c(S_0,K,T,r) - p(S_0,K,T,r) = S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\]

Step 2 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(S_0\): \(\Delta_c - \Delta_p = 1\), so \(\boxed{\Delta_p = \Delta_c - 1}\)

Step 3 — Differentiate again w.r.t. \(S_0\): \(\boxed{\Gamma_c = \Gamma_p}\)

Step 4 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(t\): \(\boxed{\Theta_c - \Theta_p = -rKe^{-r(T-t)}}\) (Since \(\partial(Ke^{-r(T-t)})/\partial t = -rKe^{-r(T-t)}\) and \(\partial S_0/\partial t = 0\).)

(c) BSM PDE via delta-hedge

Step 1 — Construct delta-neutral portfolio: Short 1 derivative \(V\), long \(\Delta = V_S\) shares. \[\Pi = -V + V_S\cdot S\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô to \(d\Pi\) using \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\): \[dV = \bigl(\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt + \sigma S V_S\,dz \quad \text{(Itô on }V\text{)}\] \[d\Pi = -dV + V_S\,dS = -\bigl(\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt - \sigma S V_S\,dz + V_S(\mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz)\] \[= -\bigl(\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt \quad \text{(the }\mu\text{ and }\sigma\text{ terms cancel})\]

Step 3 — Riskless portfolio earns \(r\): \(d\Pi = r\Pi\,dt\) \[-\bigl(\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)dt = r(-V + V_S\cdot S)dt\] Rearrange: \[\boxed{\Theta + rSV_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} = rV}\]

(d) BSM PDE and delta-neutral Theta–Gamma relationship

BSM PDE: \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = r\Pi\)
For delta-neutral (\(\Delta = 0\)): \[\boxed{\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = r\Pi}\] Interpretation: long-gamma (\(\Gamma > 0\)) portfolio has negative theta — you pay time decay to hold convexity. Short-gamma portfolio earns theta but loses on large moves.

June 2018 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Consider a commodity with current price \(S_0\). Assume that it can be stored, that there are commodities at storage (so that it may be sold), and that the net storage costs of the commodity can be described as a constant proportional continuously paid cost of \(\delta\).
(a) Describe the workings of a forward contract on the commodity.
(b) Assume a constant continuously compounded risk-free interest rate of \(r\). Using an arbitrage argument find the forward price \(F_0\) at time 0 on the forward contract that matures at time \(T\). (Hint: You may think at an analogy with a stock paying a continuously paid dividend rate.)
(c) Consider the value of a forward contract with forward price \(K\) at time \(t < T\) and assume that the commodity price follows the geometric Brownian motion \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] Using Itô's lemma find the process followed by the value of the forward contract. What will the drift be under the risk neutral measure?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Definition (Forward Contract). A Forward Contract is an agreement to buy or sell a quantity of an asset of a specified quality and type (called the underlying asset) delivered at a specified future time \(T\) (the expiration date) and at a specified place for an agreed delivery price \(K\) as measured in some defined numeraire/currency. The price is initially (at \(t\)) set at a level \(K = F(t,T)\), such that the value \(V_K(t,T)\) of the contract is 0, i.e. by definition \(V_{F(t,T)}(t,T) = 0\). The price \(F(t,T)\) is called the forward price at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\).
(b) Cf. e.g. Hull (8ed) p. 312–3 and p.292. Since the commodity can be stored there is an arbitrage between spot and forward purchase, and the argument is analogous to the case of a stock with a continuously paid dividend rate: Buy the commodity spot financed by a loan at an interest of \(r\) and accumulate interest and storage costs. The accumulated liability at \(T\) will be \(S_0 e^{(r+\delta)T}\), and you have the commodity. An alternative is to enter into a forward contract with delivery at \(T\). To eliminate arbitrage it must be that the forward price at 0 for delivery at \(T\) is \(F_0 = S_0 e^{(r+\delta)T}\).
(c) For a forward contract with forward price \(K\) the value is \(V_K = S_t e^{\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\). Using Itô's lemma you get that the differential form for the value process for the forward: \[dV_K = \bigl[(\mu-\delta)e^{\delta(T-t)}S_t - rKe^{-r(T-t)}\bigr]dt + \sigma S_t e^{\delta(T-t)}\,dz\] For a currently priced forward (i.e. where \(K\) gives the forward contract a zero net present value \(K = F_t = e^{(r+\delta)(T-t)}S_t\)) we see that the drift is 0 under the risk neutral measure (by setting \((\mu-\delta) = r\)), while a non-zero market value will grow at a rate \(r\). It will be considered ok just to derive this for the currently priced forward.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward contract on a commodity

A forward contract is an agreement to buy or sell a quantity of a commodity at a specified future date \(T\) for a delivery price \(K\) agreed at inception. The forward price \(F(0,T)\) is set so the contract has zero value at initiation.

(b) Forward price — commodity with storage cost \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Two strategies to own 1 unit at T
A) Enter forward at \(F_0\), pay at T.
B) Buy 1 unit of commodity now at \(S_0\). Pay storage cost \(\delta\) (proportional, continuous). Total cost at T: \(S_0 e^{(r+\delta)T}\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage \[\boxed{F_0 = S_0\,e^{(r+\delta)T}}\] Storage costs increase the forward price (treated like a negative dividend/yield).

(c) Value of commodity forward at t

If forward price was set at \(F_0\) at \(t=0\), current value: \[V_t = e^{-r(T-t)}\bigl(F_t - F_0\bigr) = S_t e^{\delta(T-t)} - F_0 e^{-r(T-t)}\]

Question 2

Suppose certain derivatives have values that depend on a single state variable given by the process \[\frac{d\theta}{\theta} = m\,dt + s\,dz\] where \(dz\) is a Wiener process.
(a) Consider two such derivatives, assume a continuously compounded risk-free interest rate of \(r\), and use an arbitrage argument to derive and define the market price of (\(\theta\)-)risk, \(\lambda\) (You may assume that the prices of the derivatives follow geometric Brownian motions).
(b) If \(\theta\) is itself a traded asset, what can we say about the relation between \(m\), \(s\) and the market price of risk?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Consider two derivatives with prices following two different GBMs with the same underlying Wiener process \(dz\). Use these to form a locally risk free portfolio, and using that this, barring arbitrage, must return the risk free rate you can derive the market price per unit of risk (\(\lambda\)) as the drift of the derivative price in excess of the risk free rate divided by the volatility, i.e. of the form \[\frac{\mu - r}{\sigma} = \lambda\] This is the same for both, hence all such, derivatives (Hull, 8ed, section 27.1).
(b) If \(\theta\) is itself a traded asset, then (in arbitrage equilibrium) \[\frac{m - r}{s} = \lambda\] too.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Market Price of Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Market price of risk — full arbitrage argument

Step 1 — Two derivatives \(f_1, f_2\) on same \(\theta\): \[df_i = \mu_i f_i\,dt + \sigma_i f_i\,dz,\quad i=1,2\]

Step 2 — Form a riskless portfolio: Hold \(+\sigma_2 f_2\) of \(f_1\) and \(-\sigma_1 f_1\) of \(f_2\). \[d\Pi = \sigma_2 f_2\,df_1 - \sigma_1 f_1\,df_2\] \[= \sigma_2 f_2(\mu_1 f_1\,dt + \sigma_1 f_1\,dz) - \sigma_1 f_1(\mu_2 f_2\,dt + \sigma_2 f_2\,dz)\] \[= f_1 f_2(\mu_1\sigma_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1)\,dt + f_1 f_2 \underbrace{(\sigma_1\sigma_2 - \sigma_1\sigma_2)}_{=0}\,dz\] Risk cancels — \(d\Pi\) is deterministic.

Step 3 — No-arbitrage: riskless portfolio earns \(r\). Portfolio value \(\Pi = (\sigma_2-\sigma_1)f_1f_2\): \[f_1f_2(\mu_1\sigma_2 - \mu_2\sigma_1)\,dt = r(\sigma_2-\sigma_1)f_1f_2\,dt\] Divide by \(f_1f_2\) and rearrange: \((\mu_1-r)\sigma_2 = (\mu_2-r)\sigma_1\): \[\boxed{\lambda := \frac{\mu_1-r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2-r}{\sigma_2}}\]

(b) If \(\theta\) is itself a traded asset

Then \(\theta\) satisfies no-arbitrage: \(\lambda = (m-r)/s\). So \(\lambda\) = excess return on \(\theta\) per unit of risk, which is the market price of risk.

(c) BSM PDE via market price of risk

Step 1 — Apply Itô's lemma to \(V(S,t)\) with \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\): \[dV = \underbrace{\bigl(\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\bigr)}_{\mu_V V}\,dt + \underbrace{\sigma S V_S}_{\sigma_V V}\,dz\] So: \(\mu_V V = \Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS}\) and \(\sigma_V V = \sigma S V_S\).

Step 2 — Apply no-arbitrage: \(\mu_V - r = \lambda\sigma_V\): \[\mu_V V - rV = \lambda\cdot\sigma_V V\] \[\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} - rV = \lambda\sigma S V_S\]

Step 3 — If \(S\) is the traded underlying, \(\lambda = (\mu-r)/\sigma\), so \(\lambda\sigma S V_S = (\mu-r)S V_S\): \[\Theta + \mu S V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} - rV = (\mu-r)S V_S\] \[\Theta + rS V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} = rV\] \[\boxed{\Theta + rS V_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 V_{SS} = rV}\] No-arbitrage forces BSM PDE — drift \(\mu\) does not appear (risk-neutral pricing).

Question 3

Suppose a company \(i\) is in default before or at time \(t\) with probability \(Q_i(t)\).
(a) We will often assume that \(Q_i(t)\) is weakly increasing in \(t\). Why?
(b) In a model of defaults a random variable \(x_i\) is used to indicate a default before or at time \(T\). It is assumed that \(x_i\) has a standard normal distribution, and that a default occurs at or before time \(T\) if \[\Phi(x_i) \leq Q_i(T)\] where \(\Phi\) denotes the standard normal cumulative distribution function. Interpret this. Why is such a model of computational value?
(c) In the Vasicek one-factor model of credit risk the default indicators \(x_i\) for different companies \(i = 1,\ldots,n\) are given by \[x_i = a_i F + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}\,Z_i\] where the \(a_i\)s are constants, and \(F\), \((Z_i)_{i=1,\ldots,n}\) are independent standard normal random variables. Describe and interpret this model. When can it be a reasonable description of default?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) This follows directly from the definition. The assumption in the model is that if a company defaults it stays in default.
(b) It is easier to run simulations using the random variable that is normally distributed, and especially easier to simulate correlated defaults when the marginal distributions are standard normal, rather that complicated empirical distributions.
(c) Cf. Hull p.538–40. Note that \(F\) is a common factor determining the covariance structure, whereas \(Z_i\) are idiosyncratic factors, and \(a_i\) are sensitivities to the common factor. Note that \(\text{Var}[x_i] = a_i^2 + (1-a_i^2) = 1\) and \(\text{Cov}[x_i,x_j] = a_i a_j\) using that \(F\) and \(Z_i\) are standard normal and independent. In implementation the \(a_i\) are often approximated as the correlation between \(i\)'s equity return and some broad market index (i.e. P-type correlations). They could also be inferred from e.g. prices of tranched CDOs (i.e. Q-type correlations)).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) \(Q_i(t)\) is weakly increasing in \(t\)

Step 1 — Economic reason: default is an absorbing state — once a firm defaults, it stays in default.

Step 2 — Mathematical consequence: the probability of having defaulted by \(t\) can only grow: \[\boxed{t_1 < t_2 \implies Q_i(t_1) \leq Q_i(t_2)}\]

(b) Gaussian default model — threshold and conditional probability

Step 1 — Model: \(x_i = a_iF + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i\), \(F,Z_i\sim N(0,1)\) independent. \(\text{Var}(x_i)=1\).

Step 2 — Default rule: \(\Phi(x_i)\leq Q_i(T)\), i.e. \(x_i\leq\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T))=:C_i(T)\).

Step 3 — Copula correlation: \(\text{Cov}(x_i,x_j)=a_ia_j \implies \boxed{\rho_{ij}=a_ia_j}\)

Step 4 — Conditional default prob given \(F\): \[\sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i \leq C_i - a_iF \implies Z_i\leq\frac{C_i-a_iF}{\sqrt{1-a_i^2}}\] \[\boxed{P(\text{default}_i\mid F)=\Phi\!\left(\frac{C_i(T)-a_iF}{\sqrt{1-a_i^2}}\right)}\]

(c) Numerical threshold

Step 1: Given \(Q_i(T)\), compute \(C_i(T)=\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T))\).
Example: \(Q_i(T)=0.05 \implies C_i(T)=\Phi^{-1}(0.05)\approx -1.645\).

Step 2: Use \(C_i\) in Step 4 above to get default probability conditional on any value of \(F\).

August 2018 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Let the time \(t\) spot price on a commodity be \(S_t\).
(a) Two models for the commodity price are given: \[d\ln S = \bigl(\theta(t) - a\ln S\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\] \[dS = \bigl(\theta^*(t) - a\ln S\bigr)S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\] Use Itô's lemma to show that they are equivalent when \(\theta(t) = \theta^*(t) - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\).
(b) Interpret the model.
(c) What is required for the above model of the commodity price to be a model of the price under a risk neutral equivalent martingale measure \(\mathbb{Q}\)?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Use Itô's lemma on the transformation \(\ln\) of the second equation.
(b) One may take outset in the first formulation. The model of log prices has a drift rate with a time varying mean reversion. The drift rate of the log price is 0 when \(S = \exp(\theta^*(t) - a)\). The model of the log price has a constant volatility. No further interpretation is necessary, but you can use the flexibility of the \(\theta\)-term to capture productivity gains in the production of the commodity, inflation (if nominal prices are considered) or seasonal variations.
(c) Under a risk neutral equivalent martingale measure the expected return of holding the commodity must be the risk free rate. Here we assume that storage is possible. This constitutes a satisfactory answer (as commodities are not explicitly dealt with in the syllabus). But the analysis may be carried further. Assume the risk free rate to be \(r(t)\), that storage is feasible, and that net storage costs (costs less convenience yield) can be described as a continuous proportional payment stream of \(\delta(S,t)\), then we must have \(\theta^*(t) - a\ln S = r(t) + \delta(S,t)\). Thus the modelled drift rate may be interpreted as a model of the net storage costs.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Show the two models are equivalent via Itô's lemma

Model 2: \(dS = (\theta^*(t) - a\ln S)S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\)

Step 1 — Apply Itô to \(G = \ln S\), so \(d\ln S\) from Model 2 \[d\ln S = \frac{dS}{S} - \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{(dS)^2}{S^2} = (\theta^*(t)-a\ln S)\,dt + \sigma\,dz - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\,dt\] \[d\ln S = (\theta^*(t) - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 - a\ln S)dt + \sigma\,dz\]

Step 2 — Compare to Model 1: \(d\ln S = (\theta(t) - a\ln S)dt + \sigma\,dz\)
Matching: \(\theta(t) = \theta^*(t) - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\). \(\checkmark\) \[\boxed{\text{Both models are equivalent when }\theta(t) = \theta^*(t) - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2}\]

(b) Interpretation

Log price \(\ln S\) mean-reverts to \(\theta(t)/a\) at speed \(a\).
\(\theta(t)\) can be time-varying to capture productivity trends, inflation, or seasonality.
Constant volatility \(\sigma\) in log terms.

(c) Risk-neutral equivalent martingale measure

Under a risk-neutral measure \(Q\), the expected return on holding the commodity must equal the risk-free rate \(r\) (assuming storage is possible and no convenience yield).
Replace \(\mu\to r\) in the drift: under \(Q\), drift becomes \((r - a\ln S)S\,dt\), i.e. \(\theta(t)\) is adjusted.

Question 2

Consider a one factor interest rate model with the following process for the instantaneous short rate \(r\): \[dr = m(r,t)\,dt + s(r,t)\,dz\]
(a) View the price of a zero-coupon bond with expiry at date \(T\) as a function \(P_T(r,t)\). Use Itô's lemma to find an expression for the process that \(dP_T(r,t)\) follows.
(b) Assume that this is modelled under a risk-neutral probability measure \(\mathbb{Q}\). What will be the drift rate of \(P_T\)?
(c) In some models of this form the price of the zero coupon bond can be expressed as \[P_T(t,r) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r}\] Show that \(B(t,T)\) can be interpreted as a measure of the interest rate risk of the bond.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Using Itô's lemma we get (omitting the arguments): \[dP_T = \left(\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}m + \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t} + \frac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2}s^2\right)dt + \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}s\,dz\]
(b) The expected return must be the risk free rate, so the drift rate of the zero coupon price must be \(rP_T\).
(c) Note that \[\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r} = -B(t,T)P_T\] Thus \(B\) can be seen to measure the relative capital loss on the zero coupon bond from an increase in the short rate. \(B\) is the duration of the bond (Hull 8ed p.687).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Itô's lemma on bond price \(P_T(r,t)\)

Step 1 — Write Itô's lemma with \(dr = m\,dt + s\,dz\): \[dP_T = \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r}dr + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 P_T}{\partial r^2}(dr)^2\]

Step 2 — Substitute \((dr)^2 = s^2\,dt\) \[\boxed{dP_T = \underbrace{\left(P_T^{(t)} + mP_T^{(r)} + \tfrac{1}{2}s^2P_T^{(rr)}\right)}_{\text{drift}}\,dt + \underbrace{sP_T^{(r)}}_{\text{diffusion}}\,dz}\]

(b) Drift under risk-neutral Q

Step 1 — Under Q, bond earns short rate \(r\)
The drift must equal \(r\cdot P_T\): \[\boxed{P_T^{(t)} + m^Q P_T^{(r)} + \tfrac{1}{2}s^2 P_T^{(rr)} = r P_T}\] This is the fundamental bond pricing PDE under Q.

(c) Duration from affine bond form

Step 1 — Affine form: \(P_T = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r}\)

Step 2 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(r\): \[P_T^{(r)} = A(t,T)\cdot(-B(t,T))\cdot e^{-B(t,T)r} = -B(t,T)P_T\]

Step 3 — Duration: \[\boxed{D = -\frac{1}{P_T}\frac{\partial P_T}{\partial r} = B(t,T)}\] \(B(t,T)\) = interest rate sensitivity (duration) of the bond w.r.t. the short rate \(r\).

Question 3

Consider a Credit Default Swap (CDS).
(a) Describe the instrument and its payment structure.
(b) Consider a tranched CDS. Explain the payment structure and define the terms attachment point and detachment point.
(c) Consider a tranched CDS on a large portfolio on underlying bonds (names). For a given average level of credit risk, e.g. expressed by the average credit spread on the underlying portfolio, explain how different levels of the assessed correlation of defaults of the issuers will influence the relative pricing of the tranches.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Definition (Credit Default Swap). A Credit Default Swap (CDS) is a contract between a protection seller and a protection buyer based on a specified Nominal Principal. The protection buyer pays a running premium (the CDS spread) until maturity of the contract or a Credit Event on the Reference Entity of the CDS. At a Credit Event the protection seller pays a Specified Amount (Fixed or (1-Recovery) times the Nominal Principal) to the protection buyer. See Hull p. 549.
(b) The structure and terms are defined in Hull p. 560ff.
(c) This discussion is covered in Hull section 24.9 p. 561. A low level of correlation will make it likely that the losses can be absorbed by the tranches low in the capital structure in most states of the world whereas a high correlation will make it more likely that even high tranches occasionally will be hit. Thus for a given average level of credit risk the equity tranche will benefit from an increase in the perceived correlation and the highest (super senior) tranche will suffer. The effect on mezzanine tranches can be ambiguous.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) CDS — description and payment structure

Step 1 — Define the contract
CDS: bilateral contract between protection buyer and seller on reference entity, nominal \(N\).

Step 2 — Payment flows
Before credit event: buyer pays CDS spread \(s\times N\) periodically.
At credit event: seller pays \((1-R)\times N\) (loss given default) to buyer. \[\boxed{\text{Fair spread: }s = \lambda(1-R)}\] where \(\lambda\) = risk-neutral hazard rate, \(R\) = recovery rate.

(b) Tranched CDS — structure

Step 1 — Waterfall: losses flow equity → mezzanine → senior.

Step 2 — Attachment point: portfolio loss % where tranche starts losing.

Step 3 — Detachment point: loss % where tranche is fully wiped out. \[\boxed{\text{Equity}:[0,A\%],\;\text{Mezz}:[A\%,D\%],\;\text{Senior}:[D\%,100\%]}\]

(c) Correlation effect on tranche spreads

Step 1 — Low correlation: defaults diversified → losses predictable, stay in junior tranches → senior spread low, equity spread high.

Step 2 — High correlation: defaults cluster → large losses hit senior → senior spread rises, equity spread falls.

Step 3 — Intuition for given average credit spread
