High \(\rho\) makes loss distribution bimodal (all or nothing) → tail risk for senior tranche. \[\boxed{\uparrow\rho:\;\text{senior spread}\uparrow,\;\text{equity spread}\downarrow}\]

What is Geometric Brownian Motion (GBM) and why do we use it for stock prices?
Intuition: GBM says the percentage return \(dS/S\) follows a normal distribution with constant mean \(\mu\) and constant volatility \(\sigma\). Because we model returns (not levels), prices can never go negative, and grow multiplicatively.
Model: \[dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\] Equivalently: \(\quad d(\ln S) = (\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz\)
Shortcomings: constant \(\sigma\) (no volatility smile), continuous paths (no jumps), constant drift. These are tested in almost every exam!
State Itô's Lemma. Why is it needed (and what's wrong with ordinary calculus)?
Intuition: Because \((dz)^2 = dt\) (not zero!), the standard chain rule \(dG = G_S\,dS\) misses the second-order term. Itô's Lemma adds the \(\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\) correction.
For \(G = G(S,t)\) where \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\): \[dG = \Bigl(G_t + \mu S G_S + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\Bigr)dt + \sigma S G_S\,dz\]
Key rule: \((dz)^2 = dt,\quad dz\,dt = 0,\quad (dt)^2 = 0\). The extra term \(\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\,dt\) is the Itô correction — it makes stochastic calculus different from ordinary calculus.
Use Itô's Lemma to find \(d(\ln S)\) when \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\).
Set \(G = \ln S\). Compute derivatives: \(G_S = 1/S,\quad G_{SS} = -1/S^2,\quad G_t = 0\).
Applying Itô: \[d(\ln S) = \Bigl(0 + \mu S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S} + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\cdot(-\tfrac{1}{S^2})\Bigr)dt + \sigma S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S}\,dz\] \[\boxed{d(\ln S) = (\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz}\]
Key insight: The \(-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\) Itô correction means the expected log return is less than \(\mu\). Integrating: \(S_T = S_0\exp[(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T + \sigma z_T]\).
What is the distribution of \(S_T\) under GBM? What are \(\mathbb{E}[S_T]\) and \(\text{Var}(\ln S_T)\)?
Integrating \(d(\ln S)\): \(\ln S_T = \ln S_0 + (\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T + \sigma z_T\), so \(\ln S_T \sim N\bigl(\ln S_0+(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T,\;\sigma^2 T\bigr)\).
\[S_T = S_0\exp\!\Bigl[(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T + \sigma\sqrt{T}\,\varepsilon\Bigr],\quad\varepsilon\sim N(0,1)\] \[\mathbb{E}[S_T] = S_0 e^{\mu T},\qquad \text{Var}(\ln S_T) = \sigma^2 T\]
Example: \(S_0=100, \mu=10\%,\sigma=20\%, T=1\): \(\mathbb{E}[S_1]=100e^{0.1}=110.5\). The median is \(100e^{(0.10-0.02)}=108.3\). Mean \(>\) median because of lognormal skew.
Apply Itô's Lemma to \(G = S^n\). What process does it follow?
Derivatives: \(G_S = nS^{n-1},\quad G_{SS} = n(n-1)S^{n-2},\quad G_t = 0\).
\[dG = \bigl(n\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\bigr)G\,dt + n\sigma G\,dz\] So \(G = S^n\) is also GBM with drift \(n\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}n(n-1)\sigma^2\) and volatility \(n\sigma\).
Special case \(n=2\): \(dS^2 = (2\mu+\sigma^2)S^2\,dt + 2\sigma S^2\,dz\). The \(\sigma^2\) extra drift term is the Itô correction. Volatility is doubled.
Derive the no-arbitrage forward price for a non-dividend-paying stock.
Two strategies to get stock at T: (A) Enter a forward at price \(F\). (B) Buy stock now for \(S_0\), financed by borrowing \(S_0 e^{rT}\). Both give same payoff, so no arbitrage requires same cost.
\[\boxed{F = S_0 e^{rT}}\] If \(F > S_0 e^{rT}\): sell forward, buy stock (cash and carry). If \(F < S_0 e^{rT}\): buy forward, short stock.
Example: \(S_0 = 50, r = 5\%, T = 0.5\text{ yr}\): \(F = 50 e^{0.025} = 51.27\). If market quotes \(F = 52\), sell forward and buy stock for a risk-free profit of \(\approx \$0.73\).
What are the forward prices for (a) dividend-paying stock, (b) FX, (c) commodity with storage?
Logic: dividends/foreign interest/convenience yield reduce the cost of carry, so the forward price is lower.
(a) Continuous dividend \(\delta\): \(\;F = S_0 e^{(r-\delta)T}\) (b) FX, foreign rate \(r_f\): \(\;F = S_0 e^{(r-r_f)T}\) (Covered Interest Parity) (c) Commodity, storage cost \(u\), convenience yield \(y\): \(\;F = S_0 e^{(r+u-y)T}\)
FX example: EUR/USD = 1.10, \(r_{USD}=5\%\), \(r_{EUR}=3\%\), \(T=1\): \(F = 1.10\cdot e^{0.02} = 1.122\). EUR forward is more expensive in USD because USD has higher rate.
What is the value \(V_t\) of a long forward contract (delivery price \(K\)) at time \(t < T\)?
At initiation \(K = F_0\) so \(V_0 = 0\). Later, the stock price changed, so the contract has value.
\[V_t = S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)} - K e^{-r(T-t)}\] This is: PV of receiving stock minus PV of paying delivery price.
Example: Entered forward at \(K = 51.27\), now \(t = 0.25\), \(S_{0.25} = 55\), \(r=5\%\), \(T=0.5\): \(V = 55 - 51.27e^{-0.0125} = 55 - 50.63 = 4.37\).
What are the risk-neutral probabilities in the binomial model and how do you derive them?
Idea: Under risk-neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\), the expected return on the stock must equal \(r\). Set \(e^{rh} = qu + (1-q)d\) and solve for \(q\).
\[q = \frac{e^{(r-\delta)h} - d}{u - d}, \qquad 1-q = \frac{u - e^{(r-\delta)h}}{u - d}\] Option price: \(\;f_0 = e^{-rh}\bigl[q f_u + (1-q)f_d\bigr]\)
Example: \(S=100, u=1.1, d=0.9, r=5\%, h=1\): \(q = (e^{0.05}-0.9)/(1.1-0.9) = (1.0513-0.9)/0.2 = 0.756\). Call with \(K=100\): \(f_u=10, f_d=0\). \(f_0 = e^{-0.05}(0.756\times10) = 7.19\).
What is the replicating portfolio \((\Delta, B)\) in the binomial model?
Key idea: Any derivative can be replicated by holding \(\Delta\) shares and \(B\) in bonds. Since this portfolio has the same payoffs as the option, they must have the same price (no-arbitrage).
\[\Delta = \frac{f_u - f_d}{(u-d)S}, \qquad B = e^{-rh}\frac{u f_d - d f_u}{u - d}\] \[f_0 = \Delta S + B\]
Example (cont.): \(\Delta = (10-0)/((1.1-0.9)\times100) = 0.5\). Buy 0.5 shares, borrow \(B = e^{-0.05}(1.1\times0 - 0.9\times10)/0.2 = -42.7\). Cost: \(0.5\times100 - 42.7 = 7.3 \approx 7.19\). ✓
How do you calibrate \(u\) and \(d\) in the Cox-Ross-Rubinstein (CRR) binomial tree?
Match the volatility: The standard deviation of log returns in one step should equal \(\sigma\sqrt{h}\) from GBM.
\[u = e^{\sigma\sqrt{h}}, \qquad d = e^{-\sigma\sqrt{h}} = 1/u\] where \(h = T/n\) is the step length. As \(n\to\infty\), binomial \(\to\) BSM price.
Example: \(\sigma = 20\%, T = 1, n = 2\): \(h = 0.5\), \(u = e^{0.2\times0.707} = 1.152\), \(d = 0.868\). Check: \(\ln u = 0.141 = \sigma\sqrt{h}\). ✓
State the BSM call price formula. What do \(d_1\), \(d_2\) and \(\Phi\) represent?
Intuition: \(Se^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1)\) = PV of getting the stock (if option finishes ITM). \(Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\) = PV of paying the strike. \(\Phi(d_2)\) = risk-neutral probability of finishing ITM.
\[c = Se^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S/K)+(r-\delta+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}, \quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T}\]
Example: \(S=100, K=100, r=5\%, \sigma=20\%, T=1, \delta=0\): \(d_1 = (0+0.07)/0.2 = 0.35\), \(d_2 = 0.15\). \(\Phi(0.35)=0.637, \Phi(0.15)=0.560\). \(c = 100(0.637) - 95.12(0.560) = 10.45\).
State the BSM put formula. How do you use put-call parity to derive it?
From PCP: \(p = c - Se^{-\delta T} + Ke^{-rT}\). Substituting BSM call and using \(\Phi(-x) = 1-\Phi(x)\):
\[p = Ke^{-rT}\Phi(-d_2) - Se^{-\delta T}\Phi(-d_1)\] \[\text{Put-Call Parity: }c - p = Se^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\]
Example (cont.): \(p = 95.12(1-0.560) - 100(1-0.637) = 41.85 - 36.30 = 5.55\). Check PCP: \(c-p = 10.45-5.55 = 4.90 = 100-95.12\). ✓
State the BSM PDE. How is it derived and what is its economic meaning?
Derivation: Form a riskless portfolio \(\Pi = V - \Delta S\). By Itô's Lemma, if \(\Delta = V_S\) the \(dz\) term cancels. The riskless portfolio must earn \(r\), giving the PDE.
\[\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma + rS\Delta = rV\] where \(\Theta = V_t\), \(\Delta = V_S\), \(\Gamma = V_{SS}\).
Interpretation: Left side = theta decay + gamma P&L from convexity + delta drift. Right side = risk-free return on option value. Every derivative on \(S\) satisfies the same PDE; only boundary conditions differ.
How do you price a European FX option? What changes from BSM?
FX option: The foreign currency is like a stock with a continuous "dividend" equal to the foreign risk-free rate \(r_f\). Replace \(\delta\) with \(r_f\) everywhere.
\[c = Se^{-r_f T}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S/K)+(r-r_f+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}\] Also: Forward FX rate \(F = Se^{(r-r_f)T}\) (CIP)
Intuition: If you own foreign currency, you earn \(r_f\) continuously (like dividends). So the effective cost of carry is \(r - r_f\). If \(r_f > r\), the forward is at a discount.
What is Delta? State its formula for BSM calls and puts. What does delta-hedging mean?
Delta = sensitivity of option price to a \$1 move in \(S\). Also = probability of expiring ITM (approximately) and = number of shares in replicating portfolio.
\[\Delta_c = e^{-\delta T}\Phi(d_1) \in [0,1], \qquad \Delta_p = -e^{-\delta T}\Phi(-d_1) \in [-1,0]\] Delta-hedge: sell \(\Delta_c\) shares per call sold \(\Rightarrow\) portfolio is instantaneously riskless.
Example: \(\Delta_c = 0.637\) means: if \(S\) rises by $1, call value rises by $0.637. To hedge 100 calls sold, buy 63.7 shares. The hedge must be re-balanced as \(S\) moves (dynamic hedging).
What are Gamma and Vega? Why does a delta-hedged portfolio still have Gamma risk?
Gamma = rate of change of Delta (curvature of option price). Vega = sensitivity to volatility. A delta-hedged portfolio has zero first-order risk but still gains/loses from large moves (gamma) and volatility changes (vega).
\[\Gamma = \frac{e^{-\delta T}\phi(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt{T}} \geq 0, \qquad \mathcal{V} = Se^{-\delta T}\phi(d_1)\sqrt{T} \geq 0\] Both calls and puts have the same \(\Gamma\) and \(\mathcal{V}\) (from PCP).
Key relationship (BSM PDE rewritten): \[\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = rV - rS\Delta\] High gamma → option value is very sensitive to large moves. ATM options near expiry have highest gamma.
State Put-Call Parity and derive it by no-arbitrage. What happens if it's violated?
Two portfolios with same payoff at T: (A) Long call + \(Ke^{-rT}\) cash. (B) Long put + \(Se^{-\delta T}\) (prepaid forward). Both pay \(\max(S_T-K,0) + \max(K-S_T,0)\) net. By no-arbitrage, same cost today.
\[c - p = Se^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT} = F_{0,T}^P - Ke^{-rT}\] where \(F_{0,T}^P = Se^{-\delta T}\) is the prepaid forward price.
Arbitrage if violated: If \(c - p > Se^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\): sell call, buy put, buy stock, borrow. Profit today, zero net cash at T. This arbitrage is very fast to exploit in practice.
What is the market price of risk \(\lambda\)? Why must it be the same for all derivatives on the same underlying?
Definition: \(\lambda\) is the extra expected return per unit of risk (volatility). All derivatives driven by the same Brownian motion \(dz\) are exposed to the same source of risk, so they must all offer the same price of risk — otherwise arbitrage exists.
For any derivative \(f\) on asset \(x\) (with \(dx = a\,dt + b\,dz\)): \[\lambda = \frac{\alpha_f - r}{\sigma_f} = \frac{a - r}{b} \quad\text{(same for all }f\text{)}\] The risk-neutral drift replaces \(a\) with \(a - \lambda b\).
Application: Short rate \(r\) has market price of risk \(\lambda(r,t)\). The bond pricing PDE becomes: \[\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\frac{\partial^2 P}{\partial r^2} + (a - \lambda\sigma)\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} + \frac{\partial P}{\partial t} - rP = 0\]
How do you switch from the real-world measure \(\mathbb{P}\) to the risk-neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\)?
Girsanov's theorem: Add a drift adjustment \(\lambda\,dt\) to the Brownian motion. Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), \(d\widetilde{z} = dz + \lambda\,dt\) is a standard Brownian motion, and all assets earn the risk-free rate.
\[d\widetilde{z} = dz + \lambda\,dt\] Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(\;dS = (r-\delta)S\,dt + \sigma S\,d\widetilde{z}\) Radon-Nikodym derivative: \(\;\dfrac{d\mathbb{Q}}{d\mathbb{P}} = e^{-\lambda z_T - \frac{1}{2}\lambda^2 T}\)
Key result: Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), you replace the real drift \(\mu\) with the risk-neutral drift \(r - \delta\). This is why BSM pricing uses \(r\) as the drift for \(S\), not \(\mu\).
State the Vasicek model. What are its main features and limitation?
Mean-reverting: The short rate \(r\) is pulled back to its long-run mean \(b\) at speed \(a\). This captures the observed mean-reversion in interest rates — rates don't grow without bound.
\[dr = a(b - r)\,dt + \sigma\,d\widetilde{z}\] ZCB price: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)\,e^{-B(t,T)\,r(t)}\) where \(B = \tfrac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\), \(\;A = \exp\!\Bigl[(B-(T-t))(ab-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)/a^2 - \tfrac{\sigma^2 B^2}{4a}\Bigr]\)
Main limitation: Vasicek allows \(r < 0\) (negative rates), since volatility is constant. The CIR model fixes this with \(\sigma\sqrt{r}\), ensuring \(r \geq 0\). Vasicek is still used because it's analytically tractable.
How does CIR differ from Vasicek? What condition ensures \(r > 0\)?
CIR replaces constant \(\sigma\) with \(\sigma\sqrt{r}\). This makes volatility proportional to the level of \(r\), so as \(r\to 0\) the diffusion shrinks to zero — rates can't go negative.
\[dr = a(b - r)\,dt + \sigma\sqrt{r}\,d\widetilde{z}\] Feller condition for \(r > 0\) a.s.: \(\;2ab \geq \sigma^2\) ZCB price also has form \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r}\) but with different \(A, B\).
Key exam point: Both Vasicek and CIR are affine models (\(\ln P\) is affine in \(r\)), giving closed-form bond prices. Ho-Lee (\(dr=\theta(t)dt+\sigma\,d\widetilde{z}\)) is the simplest model that fits the current yield curve exactly.
How are zero-coupon bond prices \(P(t,T)\) related to forward rates \(f(t,T)\) and the short rate?
Three equivalent representations: ZCB price, yield, and forward rate all carry the same information about the term structure.
\[P(t,T) = \exp\!\left(-\int_t^T f(t,u)\,du\right) = e^{-y(t,T)(T-t)}\] \[f(t,T) = -\frac{\partial \ln P(t,T)}{\partial T}, \qquad r(t) = f(t,t) = \lim_{T\to t}f(t,T)\]
Key relation: Under risk-neutral pricing: \(P(t,T) = \mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}_t\!\left[e^{-\int_t^T r(s)\,ds}\right]\). This is the discounted expectation of 1, and connects short-rate models to ZCB prices.
What is the HJM framework and why is it more general than short-rate models?
HJM models the entire forward rate curve \(f(t,T)\) directly (for all maturities \(T\) simultaneously), rather than just the short rate. It automatically fits the current yield curve as input.
\[df(t,T) = \alpha(t,T)\,dt + \sigma(t,T)\,d\widetilde{z}(t)\] The short rate: \(r(t) = f(t,t)\). ZCB: \(P(t,T) = \exp(-\int_t^T f(t,u)\,du)\).
Advantage: Any short-rate model (Vasicek, CIR, Ho-Lee) is a special case of HJM with a specific \(\sigma(t,T)\). HJM's constraint is the no-arbitrage drift condition — the drift is not free to choose.
State the HJM no-arbitrage drift condition. How is it derived?
No-arbitrage forces the drift of \(f(t,T)\) to be determined by the volatility. The derivation uses the fact that \(P(t,T)/B(t)\) must be a \(\mathbb{Q}\)-martingale (where \(B\) is the money market account).
\[\alpha(t,T) = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T \sigma(t,u)\,du\] The drift is always positive (since volatility \(\sigma\geq0\)). This means under \(\mathbb{Q}\), forward rates tend to rise — the HJM drift is determined entirely by volatility.
Special case (Ho-Lee): \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma\) (constant). Then \(\alpha(t,T) = \sigma^2(T-t)\). The drift increases linearly with maturity — longer forwards have larger upward drift under \(\mathbb{Q}\).
What is the LIBOR Market Model (LMM)? What is special about the forward measure?
LMM models forward LIBOR rates \(L_i(t)\) directly — the rates that are directly observable in the market. Under the \(T_{i+1}\)-forward measure, each \(L_i\) is a martingale (no drift), making pricing tractable.
Under the \(T_{i+1}\)-forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^{T_{i+1}}\): \[dL_i(t) = \sigma_i L_i(t)\,d\widetilde{z}_i(t)\] This means \(L_i\) is GBM under the forward measure — perfect for applying Black's formula.
Why forward measure? When you price an asset that pays at \(T_{i+1}\), use \(P(0,T_{i+1})\) as numeraire: \(V_0 = P(0,T_{i+1})\,\mathbb{E}^{T_{i+1}}[\text{payoff}]\). Under this measure \(L_i\) has no drift — very convenient.
How do you price a caplet and a cap? What is Black's formula?
A cap protects against rising rates: it pays \(\max(L_i - K, 0)\) at each reset date. Each payment is a caplet. Price the cap as a sum of caplets using Black's formula (since \(L_i\) is lognormal under the forward measure).
\[\text{Caplet}_i = \tau P(0,T_{i+1})\bigl[L_i(0)\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(L_i/K)+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_i^2 T_i}{\sigma_i\sqrt{T_i}},\quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_i\sqrt{T_i}\] \[\text{Cap} = \sum_{i=1}^n \text{Caplet}_i\]
Intuition: Black's formula for caplets is exactly BSM with \(L_i(0)\) as the "stock price" and \(P(0,T_{i+1})\) as the discount factor. The "implied vol" \(\sigma_i\) is quoted for each caplet maturity.
How is a payer swaption priced? What is the annuity factor \(A\)?
A payer swaption gives the right to enter a pay-fixed swap at rate \(K\). Use the swap rate \(S_0\) as the "stock" and the annuity \(A\) as the numeraire. Under the swap (annuity) measure, \(S_0\) is a martingale.
\[\text{Payer swaption} = A\bigl[S_0\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\] \[A = \sum_{i=1}^n \tau_i P(0,T_i), \quad S_0 = \frac{P(0,T_0) - P(0,T_n)}{A}\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_0/K)+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}, \quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T}\]
Swap rate formula derivation: A fixed-for-floating swap pays \((L_i - K)\tau\) at each \(T_i\). Setting value = 0 and solving for \(K\) gives \(S_0\) as the ratio of (floating leg PV) to (annuity). This is a key exam derivation.
Explain the Merton (1974) model of credit risk. What is equity in this framework?
Key insight: Equity holders own a call option on the firm's assets \(V\) — they get \(\max(V_T - D, 0)\) at maturity (after paying debt \(D\)). Debt holders get \(\min(V_T, D)\).
\[E = V\Phi(d_1) - De^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(V/D)+(r+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_V^2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt{T}}, \quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_V\sqrt{T}\] \[\mathbb{Q}(\text{default}) = \Phi(-d_2)\]
Example: Firm value \(V=100\), debt \(D=80\), \(r=5\%\), \(\sigma_V=20\%\), \(T=1\): \(d_1 = (\ln(100/80)+0.07)/0.2 = 1.47\), \(d_2=1.27\). \(\mathbb{Q}(\text{default}) = \Phi(-1.27) = 10.2\%\).
What is the credit spread? How does the hazard rate \(h\) relate to recovery rate \(\phi\)?
Credit spread = extra yield demanded for default risk. Under risk-neutral measure, the spread compensates for expected loss: (probability of default) × (loss given default).
Credit spread: \(\;s = y_{\text{risky}} - r \approx h(1-\phi)\) where \(h\) = risk-neutral hazard rate, \(\phi\) = recovery rate (fraction of face recovered). \[P(\text{no default by }T) = e^{-hT}, \qquad s = -\tfrac{1}{T}\ln\!\left(\tfrac{P_{\text{risky}}}{P_{\text{risk-free}}}\right)\]
Example: 5-year corporate bond yields 7%, risk-free rate 5%, so \(s = 2\%\). If recovery \(\phi=40\%\): \(h = s/(1-\phi) = 0.02/0.6 = 3.33\%\). Annual default probability \(\approx 3.33\%\). Over 5 years: \(1-e^{-0.167} = 15.4\%\).
How do you price a derivative by Monte Carlo simulation? What is the log-Euler scheme?
Algorithm: (1) Simulate \(N\) paths of \(S_T\) under \(\mathbb{Q}\). (2) Compute payoff for each path. (3) Average payoffs and discount at \(r\). The log-Euler scheme is exact for GBM (no discretisation error).
Log-Euler (exact for GBM): \[\ln S_{t+h} = \ln S_t + (r-\delta-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)h + \sigma\varepsilon\sqrt{h}, \quad \varepsilon\sim N(0,1)\] Standard error: \(\;\text{SE} = \hat{\sigma}_{\text{payoff}}/\sqrt{N}\)
Variance reduction: (a) Antithetic variates: use \(-\varepsilon\) for each \(\varepsilon\). (b) Control variates: use a known-price option as control. Both reduce SE without more simulations. Error \(\propto 1/\sqrt{N}\) so \(4\times\) more paths halves the error.
What is the finite difference method for option pricing? What are the explicit vs implicit schemes?
Discretise the BSM PDE on a grid of \((S, t)\) points. Work backwards from the payoff at \(T\) to get the price at \(t=0\). Finite differences are flexible — handle American options, path dependence, etc.
Explicit (forward Euler in time): simple but may be unstable if \(\Delta t\) is too large. Implicit (backward Euler): always stable, requires solving a linear system at each step. Crank-Nicolson: average of explicit/implicit, second-order accurate in time — most common.
Stability: Explicit scheme requires \(\Delta t \leq (\Delta S)^2/(\sigma^2 S^2)\). American options: at each node, take \(\max(\text{continuation value, exercise value})\). FD grids typically have 50–200 time steps and 100–400 stock steps.
What is a martingale? Give an example in finance. Why is it important?
A martingale is a process whose best forecast of its future value is its current value — it has no predictable drift. Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), discounted asset prices are martingales. This is the foundation of risk-neutral pricing.
\(M_t\) is a \(\mathbb{Q}\)-martingale if \(\;\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}_t[M_s] = M_t\) for all \(s > t\). Key: \(e^{-rt}S_t\) (discounted stock price) is a \(\mathbb{Q}\)-martingale. Risk-neutral pricing: \(V_0 = e^{-rT}\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\)
Why it works: If discounted prices are martingales under \(\mathbb{Q}\), no strategy can earn more than the risk-free rate on average — no arbitrage. The price of any derivative is its discounted expected payoff under \(\mathbb{Q}\).
What is a numeraire? How does changing the numeraire change the measure?
Any traded asset with positive price can be a numeraire. Changing numeraire changes the probability measure. Using \(P(t,T)\) as numeraire gives the T-forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^T\) — very useful for options that pay at \(T\).
Under \(\mathbb{Q}^T\) (T-forward measure, using ZCB as numeraire): \[V_0 = P(0,T)\,\mathbb{E}^T[V_T]\] Under stock measure (S as numeraire, \(\mu=r+\sigma^2\)): \[V_0 = S_0\,\mathbb{E}^S[V_T/S_T]\]
Application: Pricing a caplet: payoff is \(\tau\max(L(T_1,T_2)-K,0)\) paid at \(T_2\). Use \(P(0,T_2)\) as numeraire. Under \(\mathbb{Q}^{T_2}\), \(L(t,T_1,T_2)\) is a martingale (no drift). Apply Black's formula directly.
State Girsanov's theorem. How is it used in derivatives pricing?
Key result: Adding a constant drift \(\lambda\) to a Brownian motion under \(\mathbb{P}\) creates a new Brownian motion under a new measure \(\mathbb{Q}\). This is how we switch from the real world to the risk-neutral world.
If \(\widetilde{z}_t = z_t + \lambda t\) then \(\widetilde{z}\) is a \(\mathbb{Q}\)-Brownian motion where: \[\frac{d\mathbb{Q}}{d\mathbb{P}}\bigg|_{\mathcal{F}_T} = e^{-\lambda z_T - \frac{1}{2}\lambda^2 T}\] Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(\;dS = (r-\delta)S\,dt + \sigma S\,d\widetilde{z}\)
Intuition: The real-world drift \(\mu\) is replaced by the risk-neutral drift \(r-\delta\). The "cost" of this change is the Radon-Nikodym density — it re-weights scenarios to make risky assets equivalent to risk-free investment on average.
What are the main shortcomings of GBM for modelling stock prices? (Exam favourite!)
GBM assumes: constant drift \(\mu\) and volatility \(\sigma\), continuous paths, no jumps. Reality disagrees on all counts.
  • Constant \(\sigma\): implied volatility smiles/skews exist in practice — violates GBM.
  • Normal log-returns: real returns have fat tails (leptokurtosis) and negative skewness.
  • No jumps: crash events (1987, 2008) are far too large to be diffusion-driven.
  • Continuous paths: prices gap overnight; GBM is path-continuous.
  • Constant drift: mean reversion and regime changes are observed empirically.
💡 Exam tip: usually worth 2–3 marks — state 3+ distinct points clearly.
Use Itô's Lemma to derive \(d(\ln S)\) where \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\). State the result and integrate.
Apply Itô with \(G = \ln S\): \[G_S = \tfrac{1}{S},\quad G_{SS} = -\tfrac{1}{S^2},\quad G_t = 0\] \[d(\ln S) = \underbrace{\mu S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S}}_{\mu} + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\cdot\!\left(-\tfrac{1}{S^2}\right)\biggr)dt + \sigma S\cdot\tfrac{1}{S}\,dz\] \[\boxed{d(\ln S) = \left(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\right)dt + \sigma\,dz}\]
Integrate over \([0,T]\): \[\ln S_T = \ln S_0 + \left(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\right)T + \sigma z_T\] \[S_T = S_0\exp\!\left[\left(\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\right)T + \sigma\sqrt{T}\,\varepsilon\right],\quad \varepsilon\sim\mathcal{N}(0,1)\]
💡 The \(-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\) Itô correction is the most-tested single formula in the course.
Derive the no-arbitrage forward exchange rate \(F_0\) for currency with foreign rate \(r_f\). When does the foreign currency trade at a forward discount?
Covered interest parity: \[F_0 = S_0\,e^{(r-r_f)T}\] where \(S_0\) = spot (domestic per foreign), \(r\) = domestic rate, \(r_f\) = foreign rate.
Forward discount: if \(r > r_f\), then \(F_0 > S_0\) — foreign currency trades at a forward discount (you need more domestic to buy it forward). If \(r < r_f\), forward premium.
BSM for FX option (Garman-Kohlhagen): \[c = S_0 e^{-r_f T}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_0/K)+(r-r_f+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}\]
💡 Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(dS = (r-r_f)S\,dt + \sigma S\,d\tilde{z}\). Dividends \(\delta\) replace \(r_f\).
What are Arrow-Debreu (state) prices? How do you extract them from a binomial tree? Why are they useful?
Intuition: An Arrow-Debreu price \(\psi_i\) is the price today of a security that pays \(\$1\) in state \(i\) and \(\$0\) everywhere else. They encode both probability and discounting.
In a one-period binomial tree: \[\psi_u = \frac{q}{1+r},\quad \psi_d = \frac{1-q}{1+r}\] where \(q\) is the risk-neutral up-probability. Note: \(\psi_u + \psi_d = \frac{1}{1+r} = P(0,1)\).
Pricing any payoff \((V_u, V_d)\): \[V_0 = \psi_u V_u + \psi_d V_d\]
💡 Extracting state prices from option prices is a key exam technique — e.g. "find \(\psi_u, \psi_d\) given call and put prices."
State put-call parity for European options on a futures contract with futures price \(F\).
Futures PCP: \[c - p = (F_0 - K)\,e^{-rT}\] Compare to stock PCP: \(c - p = S_0 e^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\).
Why: The forward price of the futures is \(F_0\) itself (no carry cost on a futures). Holding a long call and short put synthetically replicates a long futures at \(K\), worth \((F_0-K)e^{-rT}\) today.
BSM for futures (Black's formula): \[c = e^{-rT}\bigl[F_0\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_0/K)+\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 T}{\sigma\sqrt{T}}\]
💡 Black's formula = BSM with \(S\to F_0\) and \(\delta = r\) (or equivalently \(e^{-rT}F_0\) in place of \(Se^{-\delta T}\)).
State the BSM PDE for a derivative \(V(S,t)\). How does it relate \(\Theta\), \(\Delta\), and \(\Gamma\)?
BSM PDE: \[\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = rV\] where \(\Theta = \partial V/\partial t\), \(\Delta = \partial V/\partial S\), \(\Gamma = \partial^2 V/\partial S^2\).
Interpretation: For a delta-hedged portfolio (long option, short \(\Delta\) shares): the portfolio earns the risk-free rate. Gamma makes money when the stock moves big; Theta bleeds over time — they are in direct tension: \[\Theta \approx -\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma\quad\text{(for ATM, approximately)}\]
💡 High \(\Gamma\) ↔ high \(\Theta\) decay. The PDE is the mathematical form of this trade-off.
What is the risk-neutral drift of a dividend-paying stock with continuous yield \(\delta\)? Derive it from no-arbitrage.
Under \(\mathbb{P}\): \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\)
Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): \(dS = (r - \delta)S\,dt + \sigma S\,d\tilde{z}\)
Intuition: The total return on a stock is price appreciation + dividends. Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), total return must equal \(r\). If the stock pays dividend yield \(\delta\), price appreciation must be only \(r - \delta\).
Change of measure (Girsanov): \[\lambda = \frac{\mu - (r-\delta)}{\sigma}\quad\text{(market price of risk)}\] \[d\tilde{z} = dz + \lambda\,dt\]
💡 FX: treat foreign rate \(r_f\) as the dividend yield. Commodities: treat convenience yield \(y\) similarly.
State the Ho-Lee model for the short rate. What is the ZCB price, and why is Ho-Lee "too simple"?
Ho-Lee: \[dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] \(\theta(t)\) is chosen to fit the initial term structure \(P(0,T)\) exactly. No mean reversion.
ZCB price (affine): \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)\,e^{-B(t,T)r_t}\] with \(B(t,T)=T-t\) and \(\ln A(t,T)\) determined by \(\theta\).
Shortcomings:
  • No mean reversion — rates can go to \(-\infty\) or \(+\infty\).
  • Constant volatility \(\sigma\) — same shock at all maturities.
  • Normal rates → can go negative.
💡 Hull-White fixes Ho-Lee by adding mean reversion: \(dr = (\theta(t)-ar)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\). Exam often asks to "modify" Ho-Lee.
How does Hull-White extend Ho-Lee? Write the SDE and state what \(a\) controls. What is the ZCB formula?
Hull-White model: \[dr = \bigl(\theta(t) - a\,r\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\] \(a > 0\) = mean-reversion speed; \(\theta(t)\) = time-varying level fit to term structure.
Effect of \(a\): pulls \(r\) back toward \(\theta(t)/a\). Larger \(a\) → faster reversion → shorter-maturity rates dominate → flatter term structure responses.
ZCB price (affine): \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)\,e^{-B(t,T)r_t},\quad B(t,T) = \frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}\]
💡 Ho-Lee is the special case \(a=0\): \(B(t,T)\to T-t\). The exam often asks you to explain what adding \(a\) improves.
State the HJM framework. What no-arbitrage condition must the forward rate drift satisfy? Derive it.
HJM setup: \[df(t,T) = \alpha(t,T)\,dt + \sigma(t,T)\,dz\] where \(f(t,T)\) is the instantaneous forward rate.
No-arbitrage requires (HJM drift condition): \[\alpha(t,T) = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\!\sigma(t,u)\,du\]
Derivation sketch: The ZCB \(P(t,T)=\exp(-\int_t^T f(t,u)\,du)\). Apply Itô → get \(dP/P = \ldots\,dt + \ldots\,dz\). Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), the drift of \(P/B\) must be zero. This forces the drift constraint on \(\alpha\).
Ho-Lee as HJM: \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma\) (constant) → \(\alpha(t,T) = \sigma^2(T-t)\).
💡 The HJM condition is the key result: given any volatility surface \(\sigma(t,T)\), the drift is fully determined — no free parameters!
Show that the forward LIBOR rate \(F_k(t)\) is a martingale under the \((k+1)\)-forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\). What is its SDE?
Key idea: \(F_k(t)\) is the fair rate for a forward loan from \(T_k\) to \(T_{k+1}\), so \(F_k(t) = \frac{1}{\tau}\left(\frac{P(t,T_k)}{P(t,T_{k+1})}-1\right)\). With numeraire \(N=P(\cdot,T_{k+1})\), \(F_k/1\) is already a price ratio — it must be a \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\)-martingale.
SDE under \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\) (no drift): \[dF_k = \zeta_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}^{k+1}\] → Lognormal → Black's caplet formula applies directly.
Under rolling (spot) numeraire: \[dF_k = \zeta_k F_k\left[\sum_{j=m(t)}^{k}\frac{\tau_j\zeta_j F_j}{1+\tau_j F_j}\right]dt + \zeta_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}\] The drift is non-zero — simulation requires this "drift adjustment."
💡 Change of measure from \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\) to spot measure introduces the drift sum — this is why LMM Monte Carlo is complex.
What is a Credit Default Swap (CDS)? Derive the fair CDS spread \(s\) in terms of the hazard rate \(h\) and recovery \(\phi\).
CDS: Protection buyer pays spread \(s\) per year; protection seller pays \((1-\phi)\) (loss given default) if the reference entity defaults. Both legs stop at default.
Fair spread (continuous, flat hazard rate):
Premium leg PV = Protection leg PV: \[s \approx h(1-\phi)\] More precisely: \[s = \frac{h(1-\phi)\,\int_0^T e^{-(r+h)t}dt}{\int_0^T e^{-(r+h)t}dt} = h(1-\phi)\]
Credit triangle: \[s = h(1-\phi) \iff h = \frac{s}{1-\phi}\] Bond spread: \(s_{\text{bond}} \approx h(1-\phi)\) — same formula!
💡 Exam: if given CDS spread and recovery, always compute the implied hazard rate \(h = s/(1-\phi)\).
Describe the Gaussian copula (one-factor) model for correlated defaults. How does correlation affect CDO tranche losses?
One-factor Gaussian model: \[X_i = \sqrt{\rho}\,M + \sqrt{1-\rho}\,\varepsilon_i\] where \(M\sim\mathcal{N}(0,1)\) = systematic factor, \(\varepsilon_i\sim\mathcal{N}(0,1)\) = idiosyncratic. Default if \(X_i < \Phi^{-1}(q_i)\).
Conditional on \(M\), defaults are independent: \[p_i(M) = \Phi\!\left(\frac{\Phi^{-1}(q_i)-\sqrt{\rho}\,M}{\sqrt{1-\rho}}\right)\]
Correlation effects on tranches:
  • High \(\rho\): either almost all default or almost none → equity tranche benefits (correlated survival), senior tranche suffers.
  • Low \(\rho\): diversifiable, equity tranche absorbs; senior is safe.
  • \(\rho=1\): extreme case — all default together, every tranche loses equally (proportional to notional).
💡 Mezzanine tranches are most sensitive to correlation assumptions — "correlation smile" problem in practice.
What is the difference between a cash CDO and a synthetic CDO? What is each tranche's role?
Cash CDO: Holds actual loans/bonds. Cash flows from assets pass through to tranches. Equity (junior) tranche absorbs first losses; mezzanine next; senior tranche last.
Synthetic CDO: Uses CDS contracts rather than physical assets. The SPV sells CDS protection on a reference portfolio; premiums fund tranche payments. No physical transfer of assets needed.
Tranche attachment/detachment: if portfolio loss \(L > A\) (attachment), the tranche starts losing. If \(L > D\) (detachment), the tranche is wiped out. \[\text{Tranche loss} = \min(L-A,\, D-A)^+\]
💡 Exam context: in the Gaussian model, compute expected tranche losses by integrating \(\mathbb{E}[\min(L(\omega)-A,D-A)^+]\) over scenarios.
State the change of numeraire theorem precisely. If \(V_0 = e^{-rT}\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\), how do you price under the \(T\)-forward measure?
Theorem: For any two numeraires \(N\) and \(G\) (positive tradeable assets), there exists a measure \(\mathbb{Q}^G\) under which every price ratio \(V/G\) is a martingale: \[\frac{V_t}{G_t} = \mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}^G}\!\left[\frac{V_T}{G_T}\,\Big|\,\mathcal{F}_t\right]\]
Forward measure \(\mathbb{Q}^T\) (numeraire = ZCB \(P(\cdot,T)\)): \[V_0 = P(0,T)\,\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}^T}[V_T]\] Under \(\mathbb{Q}^T\), the forward price \(F(t,T) = V_t/P(t,T)\) is a martingale.
Radon-Nikodym derivative: \[\frac{d\mathbb{Q}^G}{d\mathbb{Q}} = \frac{G_T/G_0}{B_T/B_0}\] where \(B_t = e^{rt}\) is the money-market account.
💡 Key use: pricing swaptions under the swap measure (annuity as numeraire), caplets under \(\mathbb{Q}^{k+1}\), bonds under forward measure.
What is a floor / floorlet? Write the floorlet payoff and the Black pricing formula. How does a floor relate to a cap via put-call parity?
Floorlet payoff at \(T_{i+1}\): \[\tau\,\max(K - L_i, 0) = \tau(K-L_i)^+\] A floor is a portfolio of floorlets — protects a floating-rate borrower (or pays when rates fall below \(K\)).
Black's floorlet formula: \[\text{Floorlet}_i = \tau\,P(0,T_{i+1})\bigl[K\Phi(-d_2) - F_i\Phi(-d_1)\bigr]\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_i/K)+\frac{1}{2}\zeta_i^2 T_i}{\zeta_i\sqrt{T_i}},\quad d_2 = d_1 - \zeta_i\sqrt{T_i}\]
Cap-Floor parity: \[\text{Cap} - \text{Floor} = \text{Swap (pay fixed at } K\text{)}\] Exactly analogous to put-call parity.
💡 A floorlet = a put option on the LIBOR rate = a call option on the ZCB. Both interpretations appear in exams.
In the HJM framework, what is the SDE for the ZCB price \(P(t,T)\) under the risk-neutral measure \(\mathbb{Q}\)?
Forward rate SDE: \(df(t,T)=\alpha(t,T)\,dt+\sigma(t,T)\,dz\)
ZCB: \(P(t,T)=\exp\!\bigl(-\int_t^T f(t,u)\,du\bigr)\). Apply Itô: \[\frac{dP(t,T)}{P(t,T)} = r_t\,dt - \underbrace{\left(\int_t^T\!\sigma(t,u)\,du\right)}_{\Sigma(t,T)}d\tilde{z}\]
Under \(\mathbb{Q}\): drift of \(P\) equals \(r_t P\) (risk-free growth) — this is guaranteed by the HJM no-arbitrage drift condition. The bond's volatility is \(\Sigma(t,T)=\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,du\).
Ho-Lee special case: \(\sigma(t,T)=\sigma\) → \(\Sigma(t,T)=\sigma(T-t)\) → bond vol increases with maturity.
💡 Duration connection: \(\Sigma(t,T) \approx \sigma \times D\) where \(D\) = duration. This is why longer bonds are more volatile.
Derive the risk-neutral pricing formula \(V_0 = e^{-rT}\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\) from the no-arbitrage condition on the discounted price process.
Step 1: No-arbitrage ↔ existence of an equivalent martingale measure \(\mathbb{Q}\) (First Fundamental Theorem).
Step 2: Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), all discounted prices are martingales: \[\frac{V_t}{B_t} = \mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}\!\left[\frac{V_T}{B_T}\,\Big|\,\mathcal{F}_t\right]\]
Step 3: Set \(t=0\), \(B_0=1\), \(B_T=e^{rT}\): \[V_0 = e^{-rT}\,\mathbb{E}^{\mathbb{Q}}[V_T]\]
Girsanov's theorem provides the explicit \(\mathbb{Q}\): replace \(dz\) with \(d\tilde{z} = dz + \lambda\,dt\), where \(\lambda = (\mu-r)/\sigma\) is the market price of risk. Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), drift of \(S\) becomes \(r\).
💡 The key insight: we don't need to know the true drift \(\mu\) to price derivatives — only \(r\) and \(\sigma\) matter.
When is early exercise of an American option optimal? How does the binomial tree capture this?
American call on non-dividend stock: Never optimal to exercise early — the option is worth more alive (time value + insurance). Proof: \(c_{\text{Am}} = c_{\text{Eu}}\).
American put / call on dividend stock: May be optimal to exercise early when the intrinsic value exceeds the continuation value.
Binomial tree rule: At each node, set: \[V_{\text{node}} = \max\bigl(\underbrace{S-K}_{\text{intrinsic}},\; \underbrace{e^{-r\Delta t}(qV_u+(1-q)V_d)}_{\text{continuation}}\bigr)\]
American call on dividend stock: exercise just before ex-dividend date if dividend exceeds the time value of keeping the option alive.
💡 Key exam question: "Show the American call on a non-dividend stock is never exercised early" — use the bound \(c \geq S - Ke^{-rT} > S - K\).
📋 Exam Format & Pattern

3 hours · Written · No aids — every formula must be memorised.

3 questions per exam (Aug 2020 has 4), each with 3–4 sub-parts that build on each other.

Typical structure:

  • Q1: Itô's lemma application + forward pricing or FX
  • Q2: BSM / Binomial / Greeks / PCP / Credit
  • Q3: Short rate models / HJM / LMM / Change of numeraire / IR derivatives
⚡ First 60 Seconds on Any Problem
  1. Identify the state variable: \(S\), \(r\), \(F\), \(f(t,T)\)?
  2. Identify the model: GBM, BSM, Vasicek, HJM, …?
  3. Identify the measure: \(\mathbb{P}\), \(\mathbb{Q}\), \(\mathbb{Q}^T\), \(\mathbb{Q}^{swap}\)?
  4. Check for dividends/foreign rate/storage: adjust drift accordingly
  5. Read terminal condition: what does the derivative pay at maturity?
📐 Itô's Lemma — Step Template
  1. Write: "Let \(G=G(S,t)\). By Itô's Lemma:"
  2. Compute all three: \(G_t\), \(G_S\), \(G_{SS}\) explicitly
  3. Write the full Itô formula with all terms shown
  4. Substitute your partials
  5. Simplify: state "Therefore \(dG=[\ldots]dt+[\ldots]dz\)"

Never skip steps — partial credit depends on showing partials.

💹 No-Arbitrage Forward — Step Template
  1. State: "Consider two strategies to own [asset] at time \(T\):"
  2. Strategy A: Enter forward (cost today = 0, pay \(F\) at \(T\))
  3. Strategy B: Replicate synthetically (buy/borrow + carry)
  4. Both strategies give same payoff at \(T\) → equal cost today
  5. Solve for \(F\). State arbitrage if violated.
🏦 BSM PDE Verification — Step Template
  1. Compute \(\Delta=\partial V/\partial S\)
  2. Compute \(\Gamma=\partial^2 V/\partial S^2\)
  3. Compute \(\Theta=\partial V/\partial t\)
  4. Evaluate LHS: \(\Theta+\frac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta\)
  5. Show it equals \(rV\) ✓
📊 Market Price of Risk — Step Template
  1. Write SDEs: \(df_i/f_i=\mu_i dt+\sigma_i dz\) for \(i=1,2\)
  2. Construct: \(\Pi=\sigma_2f_2\cdot f_1 - \sigma_1f_1\cdot f_2\) (kills \(dz\))
  3. Argue: riskless → earns \(r\) by no-arbitrage
  4. Derive: \((\mu_1-r)/\sigma_1=(\mu_2-r)/\sigma_2\equiv\lambda\)
🔢 Short Rate / ZCB — Step Template
  1. Apply Itô to \(P(r,t)\): write \(dP\) explicitly
  2. State: "Under \(\mathbb{Q}\), drift of \(P\) must equal \(rP\,dt\)"
  3. Write the fundamental ZCB PDE
  4. Identify affine form \(P=Ae^{-Br}\) → \(D=B(t,T)\)
  5. State the specific \(B\) formula for the named model (Vasicek, etc.)
⚠️ Top 8 Mistakes to Avoid
  • Forgetting \(-\frac12\sigma^2\) in \(d(\ln S)\)
  • Confusing forward price \(F\) with forward value \(V\)
  • Wrong sign on duration: \(D=B>0\) always
  • Wrong measure: always state which measure you're working in
  • Calling \(\Phi(d_2)\) the delta — it's the exercise probability; \(\Phi(d_1)\) is delta
  • Forgetting dividend in PCP: \(c-p=Se^{-\delta T}-Ke^{-rT}\)
  • HJM drift sign: \(\alpha(t,T)=\sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)du\) — positive
  • Copula: high correlation → seniors suffer (not benefit)
🎯 Must-Memorise List (12 items)
  1. Itô's lemma (full formula + 4 applications)
  2. All 4 forward price formulas
  3. BSM call & put formulas + \(d_1,d_2\)
  4. BSM PDE: \(\Theta+\frac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma+rS\Delta=rV\)
  5. PCP + 3 Greek relationships from PCP
  6. Risk-neutral probabilities \(q=(a-d)/(u-d)\)
  7. Market price of risk \(\lambda=(\mu-r)/\sigma\)
  8. ZCB affine: \(P=Ae^{-Br}\), \(D=B\); Vasicek \(B=\frac{1-e^{-a\tau}}{a}\)
  9. HJM drift condition: \(\alpha=\sigma\int_t^T\sigma\,du\)
  10. Credit triangle: \(\bar\lambda=s/(1-R)\)
  11. Merton: equity = BSM call on firm; default prob \(=\Phi(-d_2)\)
  12. Change of numeraire: \(V/N\) martingale under \(\mathbb{Q}^N\)
